iii 
 
ABSTRACT 
 
To fully realize the benefit of the two-group interfacial area transport equation (IATE) as 
a constitutive model for the interfacial area concentration in the two-fluid model, it is imperative 
that models be developed to dynamically transition from one-group to two-group flows. With this 
in mind, the two-group IATE is derived in detail to establish new expansion source terms that 
correctly account for the effects of intergroup bubble transport. In addition to this theoretical 
effort, the state-of-the-art four-sensor conductivity probe is used to establish a reliable 
experimental database of local two-phase flow parameters to characterize one-group to two-group 
transition flows and to support model development. The experiments are performed in vertical-
upward air-water two-phase flow in a 5.08cm pipe. Additionally, the local conductivity probe is 
improved through systematic studies into: 1) signal “ghosting” electrical interference among 
probe sensors, 2) sampling frequency sensitivity, 3) measurement duration sensitivity, and 4) 
probe sensor orientation. Wake-dominated bubble transport characterizes the transition from one-
group to two-group flows. Therefore, the necessary intergroup and intragroup wake entrainment 
source terms that are required for two-group interfacial area transport in transition flows are 
developed. Furthermore, an approach is developed to initiate the shearing-off source and reduce 
the one-group interaction mechanisms as an established two-group flow develops. The new 
interfacial area transport model for one-group to two-group transition flows is evaluated against 
the experimental database. The model accurately captures the exchange of void fraction and 
interfacial area concentration between group-I and group-II in transition flows. Overall, the 
group-I void fraction and interfacial area concentration are predicted within ±6% and ±4%, 
respectively, of the experimental data. The group-II void fraction and interfacial area 
concentration are predicted within ±9% and ±11%, respectively, of the experimental data.

1 
 
  
Chapter 1
 
Introduction 
The study of gas-liquid two-phase flows is of great importance due to their impact on the 
safety and efficiency of various systems and processes in the nuclear energy, chemical 
manufacturing, petroleum, and other industries. The ability to predict the transport of mass, 
momentum, and energy in a two-phase flow is central to designing and maintaining many 
practical engineering systems. The most distinctive feature of a two-phase flow is the presence of 
one or more deforming interfaces that separate the two phases. The interfaces can take on a 
multitude of different configurations, which gives rise to a complex and dynamic interfacial 
structure. Owing to the complexity of two-phase flows, there exists a wide range of modeling 
approaches. It is outside the scope of the present dissertation to review all of the available 
approaches. However, a brief overview of one approach, the two-fluid model, is provided in 
Section 1.1 to provide necessary background for the current research. Specifically, the importance 
of the interfacial area concentration (𝑎𝑖), a fundamental geometric two-phase flow parameter that 
describes the surface area of the interface separating the phases per unit mixture volume, is 
established. In Section 1.2, a survey of the pertinent literature related to the development of the 
interfacial area transport equation as a constitutive model for the interfacial area concentration in 
the two-fluid model is performed. In view of the literature survey, the significance and 
uniqueness of the current dissertation is highlighted in Section 1.3, and the major objectives of 
this dissertation are outlined in Section 1.4. 

2 
 
1.1 Research Background 
1.1.1 Two-Fluid Model 
 
In principle, it is possible to develop a local instantaneous two-phase flow formulation. 
However, the presence of the interfaces separating the two phases yields an immensely 
complicated multi-boundary problem that is far from tractable even for relatively simple flows. 
Moreover, a local instantaneous description of a two-phase flow is far too detailed for practical 
analyses, where only the macroscopic, or average, transport of mass, momentum, and energy is of 
interest. Therefore, for practical analysis of two-phase flows, macroscopic balance equations for 
the mass, momentum, and energy for each phase have been established through proper averaging 
of the local instantaneous balance equations (Ishii, 1975; Drew and Passman, 1999).   
 
Ishii (1975) established the time-averaged two-fluid model, where the balances of mass, 
momentum, and energy of each phase are considered separately through two sets of averaged 
balance equations. In a two-phase flow, the phases are coupled through the interface. Therefore, 
interaction terms that describe the transport of mass, momentum, and energy between the phases 
naturally arise in the set of averaged balance equations. A simplified form of the two-fluid model 
that can be applied in most practical analyses is given as (Ishii and Mishima, 1984): 
𝜕𝛼𝑘𝜌𝑘
𝜕𝑡
+ ∇⋅(𝛼𝑘𝜌𝑘𝒗𝑘) = 𝛤𝑘 
[1-1] 
𝜕𝛼𝑘𝜌𝑘𝒗𝑘
𝜕𝑡
+ ∇⋅(𝛼𝑘𝜌𝑘𝒗𝑘𝒗𝑘) = −𝛼𝑘∇𝑝𝑘+ ∇⋅𝛼𝑘(𝝉̿𝑘+ 𝝉𝑘
𝑡) + 𝛼𝑘𝜌𝑘𝒈 
+𝒗𝑘𝑖𝛤𝑘+𝑴𝑖𝑘−∇𝛼𝑘⋅𝝉𝑖 
[1-2] 
𝜕𝛼𝑘𝜌𝑘ℎ𝑘

Equations [1-1], [1-2], and [1-3] describe the average balances of mass, momentum, and 
energy (enthalpy), respectively, within the kth-phase; k is set equal to f or g to denote the liquid or 
gas phase, respectively. 𝛼𝑘 is the void fraction of the kth-phase, which physically represents the 
volume of kth-phase per unit mixture volume. Alternatively, 𝛼𝑘 can be interpreted as the 
probability that a point is occupied by the kth-phase. 𝒗𝑘, 𝜌𝑘, 𝑝𝑘, 𝝉̿𝑘, 𝝉𝑘
𝑡, are the velocity, density, 
pressure, average viscous stress, and turbulent stress for the kth-phase, and 𝒈 represents the 
acceleration due to gravity. ℎ𝑘, 𝒒̿𝑘, 𝒒𝑘
𝑡, and 𝛷𝑘 are the enthalpy, conduction heat flux, turbulent 
heat flux, and energy dissipation for the kth-phase. 𝛤𝑘 and 𝑴𝑖𝑘 are the mass generation rate and 
generalized interfacial drag force for the kth-phase. The subscript i signifies the interface, and 
thus, the terms: 𝒗𝑘𝑖, ℎ𝑘𝑖, and 𝑞𝑘𝑖
′′  are the velocity, pressure, enthalpy, and heat flux of the kth-
phase at the interface. 𝝉𝑖 is the mean interfacial shear stress. As previously mentioned,  𝑎𝑖 is the 
interfacial area concentration, which physically represents the amount of interfacial surface area 
per unit mixture volume. 
In addition to the balance laws within each phase, the balance laws of the interface must 
also be satisfied. Thus, from an average of the local instantaneous jump conditions (interfacial 
balance laws), the following macroscopic jump conditions that constrain the interfacial transfer 
terms in Equations [1-1] through [1-3] are given as (Ishii, 1975; Ishii and Mishima, 1984):  
∑𝛤𝑘
𝑘
= 0 
[1-4] 
∑𝑴𝑖𝑘
𝑘
= 𝟎 
[1-5] 
∑(ℎ𝑘𝑖𝛤𝑘+ 𝑎𝑖𝑞𝑘𝑖
′′ )
𝑘
= 0 
[1-6] 

4 
 
Physically, Equation [1-4] indicates that mass is conserved across the interface; the mass 
generation process results in a loss from one phase and an equal gain by the other. Similarly, 
Equation [1-5] asserts that the interfacial surface forces that are represented by the generalized 
interfacial drag term (𝑴𝑖𝑘) are equal and opposite between the phases. Likewise, Equation [1-6] 
affirms conservation of energy between the phases across the interface.  
The two-fluid model average balance equations, Equations [1-1] through [1-3], are not 
closed by themselves. Constitutive equations are required to relate unknown quantities to the 
fundamental variables of choice. Drew and Lahey (1979) provide detailed discussion on both the 
formal and phenomenological approaches to developing the constitutive equations in the two-
fluid model. Extensive development of this topic is also presented by Ishii (1975), Drew and 
Passman (1999), and Ishii and Hibiki (2006). 
Since the two-fluid model treats each phase separately through two sets of balance 
equations, the two-fluid model can provide a detailed description of a wide range of two-phase 
flow phenomena compared to simpler mixture formulations such as the homogenous equilibrium 
model (Wallis, 1969) or drift-flux model (Zuber and Findlay, 1965; Ishii, 1975; Ishii, 1977). For 
this reason, the two-fluid model is the most accurate two-phase flow model for practical analyses, 
and it is utilized in many thermal-hydraulic systems analysis and computational fluid dynamics 
codes. Although the two-fluid model is a higher fidelity model, its accuracy is limited by the 
validity of the constitutive relations supplied to it. Specifically, in addition to the closure 
relationships that are required for single-phase flows, closure models are required for the 
interfacial transfer terms that account for the transfers of mass, momentum, and energy across the 
interface. These terms couple the balance equations for each phase together, so the accuracy of 
their specification is critical in the two-fluid model. Moreover, obtaining accurate closure for the 
interfacial area concentration is of particular importance because the interfacial area 
concentration provides information about the interfacial structure of the flow.  

5 
 
1.1.2 Importance of the Interfacial Area Concentration  
In the two-fluid model (Ishii, 1975), interaction terms appear in the field equations that 
couple the phases together through the transport of mass, momentum, and energy across the 
interface. Therefore, these terms are directly related to the interfacial area concentration.  For 
example, the standard drag force component of the generalized interfacial drag term written for 
the dispersed gas-phase (𝑴𝑖𝑔
𝐷) can be expressed as (Ishii and Mishima, 1984): 
𝑴𝑖𝑔
𝐷= −𝑎𝑖[𝐶𝐷
4 (𝑟𝑠𝑚
𝑟𝐷
) 𝜌𝑐𝒗𝑟|𝒗𝑟|
2
] 
[1-7] 
where, 𝐶𝐷 and 𝒗𝑟 are the drag coefficient and the relative velocity between the dispersed-gas and 
continuous-liquid phases, respectively. The ratio of the Sauter-mean radius (𝑟𝑠𝑚) to the drag 
radius (𝑟𝐷) represents a shape factor, which is one for spherical bubbles. Also, the mass 
generation rate can be written as (Ishii and Mishima, 1984): 
𝛤𝑘= 𝑎𝑖𝑚𝑘 
[1-8] 
where 𝑚𝑘 is the mass transfer per unit area for the kth-phase. With Equation [1-8], the interfacial 
energy transfer term can be written as (Ishii and Mishima, 1984):  
(ℎ𝑘𝑖𝛤𝑘+ 𝑎𝑖𝑞𝑘𝑖
′′ ) = 𝑎𝑖(ℎ𝑘𝑖𝑚𝑘+ 𝑞𝑘𝑖
′′ ) 
[1-9] 
In view of Equations [1-7] through [1-9], a general interfacial transfer term (𝐼𝐾) can be expressed 
as a product of the interfacial area concentration and a driving flux that describes the local 
transport mechanisms as (Ishii and Mishima, 1984): 

6 
 
𝐼𝐾 ~ 𝑎𝑖× 𝐷𝑟𝑖𝑣𝑖𝑛𝑔 𝐹𝑙𝑢𝑥 
[1-10] 
 
The interfacial area concentration is an inverse length scale of the interface that describes 
the geometric capacity for interfacial transport. The transport of mass, momentum, and energy 
across the interface depends on the amount of surface area available for interaction between the 
phases. Thus, the transport characteristics vary as the interfacial structure changes. The interfacial 
area concentration is a fundamental geometric two-phase flow parameter that provides 
information about the configuration of the interfacial structure. Therefore, accurate specification 
of the interfacial area concentration is critical to the overall accuracy of the two-fluid model.  
Conventionally, the interfacial area concentration has been specified through algebraic, 
flow regime dependent correlations developed from limited ranges of experimental data (Ishii et 
al., 2009). A flow regime is a global (i.e. system scale) characterization of the two-phase flow 
interfacial structure that is often determined simply through flow visualization. Therefore, there is 
an inherent level of subjectivity in a flow regime-based description of two-phase flows. Delhaye 
and Bricard (1994) summarize several empirical correlations that relate the interfacial area 
concentration to global flow parameters, such as pressure drop and flow rates of the phases. 
While the interfacial area concentration is related to these parameters, it also strongly depends on 
system geometry as well as on the local void fraction (Ishii and Mishima, 1980). Thus, one 
cannot expect generality from correlations of global flow parameters, as was demonstrated in 
Delhaye and Bricard’s (1994) study. Based on geometric considerations, Ishii and Mishima 
(1980) developed expressions for the interfacial area concentration for assumed interfacial 
structures in bubbly, slug, churn-turbulent, and annular flow regimes Although Ishii and 
Mishima’s (1980) approach was more mechanistic, a priori knowledge of the flow regime is still 
required to select the appropriate expression for the interfacial area concentration. 

7 
 
In the flow regime-based approach, the flow regime is first identified based on static 
transition criteria. These criteria are often presented in the form of a flow regime map that defines 
flow regime boundaries in terms of global two-phase flow parameters such as the gas and liquid 
superficial velocities (Mishima and Ishii, 1984). Then, appropriate constitutive relations for the 
flow regime are selected to close the two-fluid model. This approach suffers from several aspects. 
First, the definition of a flow regime and its boundaries are inherently subjective. Physically, the 
development of the interfacial structure (i.e. flow regime transition) is a dynamic, continuous 
process that cannot be captured through fixed transition criteria. Flow regime transitions 
gradually occur in regions around these boundaries, and as such, an inherent level of uncertainty 
is introduced by relying on fixed transition criteria. Granted, for a specific system, one could 
develop a tailored flow regime map that would adequately describe the major flow regimes and 
transition regions. However, this approach would not be readily extensible to other systems since 
the interfacial structure is dependent on the flow duct geometry and upstream flow condition (e.g. 
inlet condition). These effects cannot be captured in general by a simple set of flow regime 
transition criteria. In addition to the deficiencies of the flow regime map, flow regime specific 
correlations can be discontinuous at the flow regime boundaries, which can lead to artificial 
bifurcations and numerical oscillations in simulations with the two-fluid model. Furthermore, 
numerical treatments that are used to smooth the transitions between different constitutive 
relations employed in each regime can cause uncertainty in calculations (Ishii et al., 2009). 
 
An alternative approach to the conventional flow regime-based methodology is to utilize 
a transport equation to specify the interfacial area concentration. Such an approach is more 
consistent with the two-fluid model because the interfacial area concentration can be treated as a 
dynamic variable that reflects the changes in the interfacial structure as the flow evolves. In view 
of this, a survey of the literature relevant to the development of the interfacial area transport 
equation as a constitutive model for the interfacial area concentration is performed in Section 1.2. 

8 
 
1.2 Literature Survey 
1.2.1 Interfacial Area Transport Equation  
Ishii (1975) first suggested that the interfacial area concentration in the two-fluid model 
should be specified through its own transport equation as: 
𝜕𝑎𝑖
𝜕𝑡+ ∇⋅(𝑎𝑖𝒗𝑖) = 𝜙𝐿 
[1-11] 
where 𝒗𝑖 is the average interfacial velocity, and 𝜙𝐿 is a net source/sink term. 𝜙𝐿 accounts for 
various mechanisms that can change the interfacial structure and, consequently, the interfacial 
area concentration such as expansion/contraction, coalescence, or breakup of bubbles or droplets. 
Constitutive equations for 𝒗𝑖 and 𝜙𝐿 are required to be able to use Equation [1-11] to specify the 
interfacial area concentration in the two-fluid model. Numerous studies have sought to develop 
such a transport equation for the interfacial area concentration. The efforts can be broadly 
categorized into 1) derivation of the interfacial area transport equation and 2) development of 
constitutive models for various source/sink mechanisms. Efforts towards deriving the interfacial 
area transport equation are summarized in this Section. Previous works on model development for 
the source/sink mechanisms to the interfacial area concentration are discussed in Section 1.2.2. 
 
There exist two major approaches to deriving a transport equation for the interfacial area 
concentration. One approach is based on considering the evolution of an instantaneous geometric 
description of the interface in a two-phase flow. Through appropriate averaging of the 
instantaneous description of the interface, transport equations for the interfacial area 
concentration as well as other higher-order average two-phase flow geometric parameters such as 
the mean and Gaussian curvatures can be obtained (Drew, 1990; Drew and Passman, 1999; Morel 
et al., 1999; Morel, 2007; Liu, 2008; Liu et al., 2010). No assumption on the shape of the 

9 
 
interface is required in this approach, which allows for a completely general treatment of the 
interfacial structure throughout the entire range of two-phase flow regimes. However, as pointed 
out by Morel (1999), various terms that arise in the interfacial area transport equation derived in 
this way are difficult to relate to measureable two-phase flow quantities.   
 The second approach to deriving an interfacial area transport equation considers two-
phase flows that consist of dispersed fluid particles of one phase (i.e. bubbles or droplets) in a 
continuous region of the other phase. The fluid particles are characterized by one or more 
parameters such as volume, diameter, etc., and their population is described by a particle 
distribution function. The evolution of the particle distribution function is described by an 
equation similar to the classical Boltzmann transport equation, where the total change of the 
particle distribution function is balanced by various source/sink terms. The source/sink terms 
account for changes in the particle distribution function due to particle interactions (i.e. 
coalescence or breakup) and nucleation/collapse phenomena. A transport equation for the 
interfacial area concentration can be derived by weighting the particle distribution function 
transport equation by the particle surface area. Several studies have sought to develop an 
interfacial area transport equation following this approach (Kalkach-Navarro et al., 1994; 
Kocamustafaogullari and Ishii, 1995; Millies et al., 1996; Ishii and Kim, 2004; Yao and Morel, 
2004). In fact, most of the work on interfacial area transport has been based on interfacial area 
transport equations derived according to such a statistical approach. 
As pointed out by Liu et al. (2010), since the interfacial area transport equation obtained 
from the second method is based on the statistical characteristics of dispersed fluid particles, it 
cannot describe the interfacial area concentration of continuous-gas-liquid interfacial structures 
such as the gas-liquid-film interface in annular two-phase flow. From the standpoint of practical 
application, however, the equations derived from both methods effectively have the same form as 
Equation [1-11]. They contain unknown velocities and source/sink terms that require constitutive 

10 
 
models to be useful. Each approach can complement the other in identifying and developing the 
appropriate source/sink terms for various two-phase flow regimes. Since the present dissertation 
is concerned with the interfacial area transport equation in cap-bubbly transition two-phase flows, 
the second approach to deriving the interfacial area transport equation is considered exclusively 
in the remainder of this dissertation.  
The current state-of-the-art interfacial area transport equation is the two-group interfacial 
area transport equation developed by Ishii and Kim (2004) using the statistical approach 
analogous to the classical Boltzmann transport equation. In general dispersed-gas-continuous-
liquid two-phase flows, there exists a wide range of bubble sizes, and the bubble transport 
phenomena (e.g. bubble shape, bubble interaction mechanisms, interfacial forces, etc.) vary 
significantly with bubble size. Therefore, a multi-group interfacial area transport approach is 
required to treat a wide range of two-phase flow conditions. Currently, in view of differences in 
interfacial drag characteristics and major bubble interaction mechanisms, bubbles are categorized 
into two characteristic groups (Ishii and Kim, 2004). Group-I includes spherical and distorted 
bubbles, while group-II includes cap, slug, and churn-turbulent bubbles. The boundary between 
the two groups is defined by a critical bubble volume ( 𝑉𝑐) which can be specified through the 
maximum distorted bubble limit (𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥) given by Ishii and Zuber (1979) as: 
𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥= 4√
𝜎
𝑔Δ𝜌 
[1-12] 
Equation [1-12] is obtained by equating the drag coefficient correlations for distorted and cap 
bubbles given by Ishii and Zuber (1979), and 𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥 is a volume equivalent diameter equal to 
approximately 10mm in air-water flows at ambient conditions. A schematic of the two-group 
bubble categorization scheme is shown in Figure 1-1. 

11 
 
 
Figure 1-1: Two-group bubble categorization scheme (Wu et al., 1998; Ishii et al., 2009).

where the subscripts 1 and 2 denote group-I and group-II parameters, respectively. The first and 
second terms on the left-hand sides of Equations [1-13] and [1-14] represent the local time rate of 
change and the advection, respectively, of the group interfacial area concentrations (𝑎𝑖1 and 𝑎𝑖2). 
The first terms on the right-hand sides are sources/sinks to the group interfacial area 
concentrations due to expansion/contraction of the bubbles in each group. The 
expansion/contraction of bubbles results from evaporation/condensation across the interfaces of 
existing bubbles in each group and changes in the mass density of the gas-phase. Likewise, the 
second terms on the right-hand sides represent intergroup transfer of interfacial area concentration 
between the groups due to expansion/contraction of bubbles across the group boundary. The non-

12 
 
dimensional parameter 𝐷𝑐1
∗= 𝐷𝑐𝐷𝑆𝑚1

 is a measure of the average size of group-I bubbles with

diameter. Additionally, the coefficient 𝜒 accounts for the amount of intergroup transfer between 
the two groups due to the expansion/contraction of bubbles across the group boundary.  The 𝜙𝑗,𝑘 
terms are the interfacial area concentration sources/sink rates to the kth-group due to the jth-type 
bubble interaction mechanism. The 𝜙𝑝ℎ,𝑘 terms represent the net interfacial area concentration 
source/sink to the kth-group due to nucleation/collapse phenomena. The derivation of both the 
one- and two-group interfacial area transport equations following the statistical approach 
analogous to the Boltzmann transport equation is revisited and discussed in detail in Chapter 2. 
The two-group interfacial area transport equations can be used to predict the interfacial 
area concentration in a wide range of two-phase flow conditions. In order to solve Equations [1-
13] and [1-14] for the group interfacial area concentrations, the velocities 𝒗𝑖1and 𝒗𝑖2 need to be 
specified. Moreover, closure models for the interfacial area concentration source/sink terms (𝜙𝑗,𝑘 
and 𝜙𝑝ℎ,𝑘) need to be developed. Developing models for the major bubble interaction 
mechanisms and nucleation/collapse phenomena that govern the evolution of the interfacial area 
concentration is the major challenge to closing the two-group interfacial area transport equation. 
This dissertation focuses on hydrodynamic effects (i.e. bubble interactions) in the two-
group interfacial area transport equations. Thus, available models for bubble interaction 
mechanism source/sink terms are discussed in Section 1.2.2. Regarding the nucleation/collapse 
source/sink terms, it is noted that there have been studies to develop source terms for wall 
nucleation (Kocamustafaogullari and Ishii, 1995) and sink terms due to bubble collapse (Park et 
al., 2007) in subcooled boiling flows. More recently, there have been significant experimental and 
theoretical efforts to develop one- and two-group interfacial area transport models for heated 
steam-water flows in a vertical annulus (Ozar, 2009; Brooks, 2014; Brooks et al., 2014).   

13 
 
1.2.2 Bubble Interaction Mechanism Source Term Models 
1.2.2.1 Major Two-Group Bubble Interaction Mechanisms  
Five major categories of bubble interaction mechanisms have been identified for two-
group interfacial area transport: 1) random-collision coalescence (RC), 2) wake-entrainment 
coalescence (WE), 3) turbulence-impact breakup (TI), 4) shearing-off (SO), and 5) surface-
instability breakup (SI) (Wu et al., 1998; Ishii et al., 2002). Random-collision coalescence is the 
coalescence of bubbles that are driven together by turbulent fluctuations in the liquid phase. 
Wake-entrainment coalescence occurs when a following bubble enters the wake region of a 
leading bubble, causing it to accelerate and coalesce with the leading bubble. Turbulence-impact 
breakup is the disintegration of bubbles due to impact by turbulent eddies in the liquid phase. 
Shearing-off is a disintegration process where numerous small bubbles are broken off from the 
rims at the bases of cap/slug bubbles. Lastly, when large bubbles are not restricted by the duct 
wall, they are cap-shaped and can grow due to coalescence interactions and expansion until a 
maximum size is reached. This maximum size is dictated by instability of the bubble interface 
(Batchelor, 1987; Kitscha and Kocamustafaogullari, 1989). Thus, the surface-instability breakup 
mechanism causes bubbles to break up once they reach the maximum size. This maximum size 
can be prescribed by the maximum stable cap bubble limit (𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥) which is given as (Kataoka 
and Ishii, 1987): 
𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥= 40√
𝜎
𝑔𝛥𝜌 
[1-15] 
For ambient air-water systems, Equation [1-15] yields a value of approximately 10cm. 
With two characteristic bubble groups, both intra- and intergroup bubble interactions are 
possible. Intragroup interactions are interactions where the interacting and resultant bubble(s) 

14 
 
reside within the same group, while intergroup interactions are interactions where the interacting 
and resultant bubble(s) reside in different groups. Figure 1-2 shows the nomenclature that is used 
to describe the intra- and intergroup source/sink terms in the two-group interfacial area transport 
equation (Ishii and Kim, 2004; Sun et al., 2004). For a general source/sink term (𝑋) a superscript 
in parentheses indicates the relationship between the interacting and resultant bubbles for a given 
mechanism. Subscripts indicate the bubble interaction mechanism and the group affected by the 
interaction.  For example, the superscript “(11,2)” indicates an intergroup interaction between two 
group-I bubbles that yields a group-II bubble. The subscript “WE,2” indicates that the interaction 
mechanism is wake-entrainment, and the term applies to the group-II equation.  
 
Figure 1-2: Two-group source/sink term nomenclature.  
For intragroup interactions, a shorthand version of the general notation is used since the 
interacting and resultant bubbles reside within the same group.  A single superscript number in 
parentheses is used to indicate the group in which the interaction is taking place, and a subscript 
indicating only the interaction mechanism is used.  For example, the general superscript “(2,22)” 
indicating an intragroup breakup interaction of a group-II bubble yielding two group-II bubbles is 
written more simply as “(2).” The nature of the interaction (i.e. breakup or coalescence) is then 
inferred from the interaction mechanism indicated in the subscript.

1.2.2.2 One-Group Bubble Interaction Models 
If the flow of interest is a purely one-group flow (i.e. no group-II bubbles), the number of 
interaction terms reduces to just three (i.e. 𝜙𝑅𝐶
(1), 𝜙𝑊𝐸

(1)), and the two-group interfacial

(1998) and Kim (1999) developed mechanistic models for these three major one-group interaction 
mechanisms to establish the one-group interfacial area transport equation for vertical-upward 
bubbly flows. The resulting one-group interfacial area concentration source/sink terms are given 
as (Wu et al., 1998; Kim, 1999):

The super- and subscript 1’s that indicate group-I have been excluded from the terms in 
Equations [1-16] through [1-17] since it is understood that only a one-group flow is under 
consideration. Considering the physics of these terms, the turbulence-impact mechanism is driven 
by the turbulent fluctuation velocity (𝑢𝑡). However, the inertia force due to the turbulent 
fluctuations must be sufficient to overcome the surface tension force to break up the bubble. 
Therefore, the bubble Weber number (𝑊𝑒) which is defined based on  𝑢𝑡, must be greater than 
the critical Weber number for breakup (𝑊𝑒𝑐𝑟) in order for breakup to occur. Moreover, the larger 
𝑊𝑒 is compared to 𝑊𝑒𝑐𝑟, there is a greater chance that breakup will occur. Random-collision 
coalescence is also drive by 𝑢𝑡, and the probability that two bubbles will collide increases with 
increasing void fraction towards the maximum packing void fraction (𝛼𝑚𝑎𝑥). Wake-entrainment 
coalescence is driven by the bubble relative velocity (𝑢𝑟). The wake region behind the leading 
bubble is related to the drag on the bubble. Therefore, the bubble drag coefficient (𝐶𝐷) arises in 
the wake-entrainment model.   
 
𝐶𝑇𝐼, 𝐶𝑅𝐶, 𝐶, and 𝐶𝑊𝐸 are empirical coefficients that capture stochastic effects of bubble 
interaction phenomena, such as coalescence efficiency, that would be difficult to characterize 
analytically in the context of a macroscopic two-phase flow model (Wu et al., 1998; Kim, 1999). 
The coefficients have been established based on extensive experimental databases, which are 

18 
 
summarized in Section 1.2.3.2. Experimental conditions were selected to highlight certain 
mechanisms in order to determine the coefficients. High liquid flow rate conditions (i.e. high 𝑢𝑡) 
with low void fraction were selected to isolate the turbulence-impact mechanism and set its 
coefficient. Likewise, high liquid flow rate conditions with higher void fractions were selected to 
determine the random-collision coefficients. With decreasing liquid flow rate, the effects of the 
turbulence based mechanisms diminish. Therefore, lower liquid flow rate conditions were used to 
determine the wake-entrainment coefficient. Additional work was also performed to extend the 
applicability of the model to co-current vertical-downward bubbly two-phase flows (Ishii et al., 
2004). Table 1-2 shows the one-group model coefficients that were determined for co-current 
vertical-upward and vertical-downward two-phase flows in round pipes and co-current vertical-
upward two-phase flow in a confined duct. Evaluation of the one-group interfacial area transport 
equation against an extensive two-phase flow experimental database established in round pipes 
and a confined duct has shown that the interfacial area concentration can be predicted well, 
generally within ±20% of experimental data (Ishii et al., 2002; Kim et al., 2002; Ishii et al., 2009)

1.2.2.3 Two-Group Bubble Interaction Models 
 
In a two-group flow, the group-II bubble length scale can be comparable to the flow duct 
length scale. Therefore, the duct geometry will affect the two-group flow structure. Not only will 

19 
 
it affect the shape of group-II bubbles, but the bubble motion and major bubble interaction 
mechanisms will also be affected. Therefore, it is necessary to consider the effects of duct size 
when developing the two-group interfacial area transport equation.     
Sun (2001) developed a comprehensive set of two-group bubble interaction source term 
models following a systematic integral approach (Kocamustafaogullari and Ishii, 1995). Sun 
(2001) considered air-water two-phase flows in a confined duct. The cross-section of the duct had 
a width and depth of 200mm and 10mm, respectively. Due to the high aspect ratio of the duct 
cross-section, the group-II bubbles were sandwiched between the duct walls in the depth 
direction. However, the bubbles were unconstrained in the width direction since it was greater 
than the maximum stable cap bubble limit. Therefore, stable slug flows could not be established 
in the confined duct (Sun, 2001; Sun et al., 2004). Moreover, bubble motion was not restricted in 
the width direction, leading to consideration of all the interactions in Table 1-1 (Sun, 2001). 
In large diameter pipes (i.e. 𝐷ℎ≳𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥), such as coolant pipes in nuclear reactors 
and other industrial applications, bubbles can grow to the maximum cap bubble size. Therefore, 
stable slug flows cannot form because the large bubbles break up due to the surface-instability 
mechanism before they can fill the duct cross-section and elongate (Kataoka and Ishii, 1987). 
Thus, there can be significant recirculation and increased lateral movement of the bubbles, as they 
are less confined by the pipe wall in large diameter pipes (Smith, 2002). These three-dimensional 
effects present additional challenges in developing the already more complex two-group 
formulation. With these considerations, Smith (2002) developed two-group bubble interaction 
models applicable to large diameter pipes considering all of the possible interactions in Table 1-1.  
 
If the duct length scale is less than 𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥, cap bubbles can grow and fill the duct 
cross-section. They can then elongate, and stable slug flows can form. Since the duct dimensions 
restrict the group-II flow structure, simplifying assumptions can be made about the major bubble 
interaction mechanisms in such moderate size ducts. Moderate size ducts are relevant to many 

20 
 
practical systems. For example, two-phase flow in a moderate diameter pipe may be comparable 
to the flow in a reactor subchannel, which is somewhat confined by the fuel rods, allowing for the 
formation of slug bubbles. 
 
Hibiki and Ishii (2000) developed a two-group interfacial area transport equation 
considering two-phase flows near the bubbly-slug flow regime transition boundary in a 5.08cm 
pipe. Since the pipe diameter was less than 𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥, surface-instability breakup was neglected. 
Additionally, it was assumed that the shearing-off mechanism would be insignificant in bubbly-
slug transition flows. Random-collision interactions with group-II bubbles were excluded because 
the pipe size limits the lateral motion of the group-II bubbles. Additionally, intergroup 
coalescence interactions of group-I bubbles to form group-II bubbles were assumed to be unlikely 
for the flow conditions investigated. Likewise, the complete breakup of group-II bubbles into 
group-I bubbles was neglected. Also, intragroup wake-entrainment between group-I bubbles was 
not included in the model. With these simplifications, the source/sink terms considered in Hibiki 
and Ishii’s (2000) two-group model are shown in Table 1-3.

Because Hibiki and Ishii (2000) neglected intergroup coalescence interactions between 
group-I bubbles that form group-II bubbles, their model is limited to established two-group flows 
that already contain a significant amount of group-II bubbles. It cannot dynamically transition 

21 
 
from a purely one-group flow to a two-group flow because there is no mechanism to generate 
group-II bubbles from a purely one-group flow condition. Additionally, it is noted that only a 
double-sensor conductivity probe was used to acquire the measurements in the experimental 
database used in the development of Hibiki and Ishii’s (2000) two-group model. Since a double-
sensor probe can only measure the interfacial area concentration of near-spherical bubbles, it is 
questionable to use such a database for two-group model development. Furthermore, 𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥, 
Equation [1-12], was not used to define the boundary between group-I and group-II bubbles in 
that database. The group boundary was taken to be 20mm instead of 10mm. Therefore, the 
experimental data are skewed towards a larger group-II bubble size, such that the flows that were 
studied were more established group-II conditions.  
 
Fu (2001) also developed a two-group interfacial area transport model for moderate 
diameter (2.5cm ≤ D ≤ 10cm) round pipes. Considering the added complexity and difficulty of 
experimental verification of the bubble interactions in the two-group interfacial area transport 
equation, an assessment of the possible bubble interactions was performed in order to simplify the 
required terms according to their nature and degree of importance (Fu, 2001; Fu and Ishii, 2002a).  
From experimental observation, the wake-entrainment and shearing-off mechanisms were found 
to cause the majority of the intergroup bubble interactions.  Furthermore, intragroup wake-
entrainment was found to be the dominant sink to the number of group-II bubbles.  While this 
interaction constitutes a sink to group-II interfacial area concentration, it also influences the 
overall flow structure and hence, other bubble interactions.  Lastly, the disintegration of group-II 
bubbles was found to be enhanced by the high turbulence intensity and active eddy-bubble 
interaction in the wake region of slug bubbles (Fu, 2001). 
In addition to identifying these major bubble interactions, several simplifying 
assumptions were made in Fu’s assessment (Fu, 2001; Fu and Ishii, 2002a).  Due to the similar 
nature of the intergroup interactions between group-I and group-II bubbles that occur primarily in 

22 
 
the wake region of group-II bubbles, it was assumed that random-collision of group-I and group-
II bubbles can be accounted for by wake-entrainment of group-I bubbles into group-II bubbles.  
In relation to this assumption, the contribution from group-I bubbles interacting in the nose region 
of group-II bubbles was assumed to be negligible because of the lower group-I bubble number 
density (i.e. lower interaction frequency) and lower turbulence intensity in this region.  Random-
collision coalescence of group-II bubbles was assumed to be negligible since the interaction of 
group-II bubbles in moderate diameter pipes predominately occurs in the wake region of the 
leading bubbles. The intergroup turbulent disintegration of a group-II bubble into group-I bubbles 
was not addressed.  However, the turbulent disintegration of a group-II bubble into a group-I and 
group-II bubble was considered as part of the shearing-off effect.  Lastly, the disintegration of 
group-II bubbles at the maximum size due to surface-instability was considered negligible owing 
to the moderate pipe size (Fu, 2001; Fu and Ishii, 2002a). Table 1-4 summarizes the bubble 
interaction mechanisms in Fu’s (2001) two-group interfacial area transport model; additional 
details of this model are presented in Chapter 4.

Although Fu (2001) identified intragroup breakup of group-II bubbles due to turbulent 
disintegration as a major interaction mechanism and developed a model for it, this term was cited 
to be negligible and dropped from the formulation during the model assessment against 
experimental data (Fu 2001, Fu and Ishii, 2002b).  The existing one-group models (Wu et al., 
1998; Kim, 1999) for random-collision and wake-entrainment were generalized to account for 
intragroup and intergroup coalescence of group-I bubbles. Therefore, unlike Hibiki and Ishii’s 
(2000) model, Fu (2001) did consider intergroup mechanisms that can cause the transition from a 
purely one-group flow condition to a two-group flow condition.  However, these models have not 
been sufficiently assessed against experimental data.  
1.2.3 Experimental Studies 
 
Owing to the complexity of two-phase flows, one will likely always rely on some degree 
of phenomenological modeling to develop two-phase flow models that are useful in practical 
analyses. As such, reliable experimental databases are essential to two-phase flow model 
development. Over the past several decades, great advances have been made in the development 
of local two-phase flow instrumentation such as the local multi-sensor conductivity probe 
(Revankar and Ishii, 1993; Kim et al., 2000), the multi-sensor optical fiber probe (Le Corre et al., 
2003), and the wire-mesh sensor (Prasser et al., 1998). These tools have provided the capability to 
establish detailed experimental databases of local two-phase flow parameters, including the void 
fraction and interfacial area concentration. Since the local multi-sensor conductivity probe is used 
in this dissertation, background regarding its development and operation are discussed in Section 
1.2.3.1. 

24 
 
1.2.3.1 Local Multi-Sensor Conductivity Probe 
The local conductivity probe is one of the few two-phase flow measurement techniques 
capable of obtaining detailed measurements of local time-averaged two-phase flow parameters. A 
few of the seminal works with the conductivity probe include the pioneering work of Neal and 
Bankoff (1963) where a single-sensor probe was developed to obtain local void fraction 
measurements. Also, Serizawa et al. (1975) used a double-sensor conductivity probe to obtain 
local void fraction and bubble velocity measurements. Additionally, Kataoka et al. (1986) 
established methods to measure the local time-averaged interfacial area concentration with 
double- and four-sensor probes. Kim et al. (2000) developed a miniaturized four-sensor 
conductivity probe and signal processing scheme, allowing for measurements throughout a wide 
range of two-phase flow conditions. Extensive reviews of the conductivity probe and other local 
two-phase flow measurement techniques are available in the literature (Jones and Delhaye, 1976; 
Cartellier and Achard, 1991).  
The conductivity probe measurement principle is based on the inherent difference in the 
electrical conductivities of the gas and liquid phases in a two-phase flow. To demonstrate the 
measurement principle, consider a probe consisting of two needle-like sensors that are separated 
by a known distance (Δ𝑠) and are electrically insulated along their lengths except for their very 
tips. Figure 1-4(a) shows a schematic of such a double-sensor conductivity probe as a bubble 
passes through the sensors and the ideal sensor response. Figure 1-4(b) shows how a conductivity 
probe can be implemented into a two-phase flow system. A voltage (𝑉𝐷𝐶) is applied to the sensors 
which are connected to the grounded, metallic probe body through the surrounding conductive 
liquid. The voltage drop across the liquid from the kth-sensor to ground (𝑉𝑘) is measured with a 
data acquisition system (DAQ). When a sensor tip is in the liquid, the circuit is complete, yielding 
a baseline voltage signal. When a sensor tip is in the gas phase, however, the circuit is broken 

25 
 
owing to the much lower conductivity of the gas phase, yielding a high voltage signal. These 
characteristic signals contain information about the residence time of the gas phase at the sensor 
tips, and the time delay between the interfaces hitting the upstream and downstream sensors. 
As such, the voltage signal from a single-sensor conductivity probe can be used to 
measure the local time-averaged void fraction (𝛼̅𝑡) according to: 
𝛼̅𝑡= 1
𝑇∑Δ𝑡𝑔𝑗
𝑁𝑏
𝑗=1
 
[1-19] 
where 𝑇 is the measurement duration, 𝑁𝑏 is the number of bubbles that contact the upstream 
sensor, and  Δ𝑡𝑔𝑗 is the residence time of the jth bubble at the sensor tip, which would be the 
upstream sensor in a multi-sensor probe. With two sensors separated by an axial distance (Δ𝑠) in 
the direction of the flow, the average axial bubble velocity (𝑣̅𝑔) can be estimated as:

upstream and downstream sensors, and 𝑁𝑒𝑓𝑓 is the number of bubbles that register effective 
signals at both sensors. The time-averaged interfacial area concentration (𝑎̅𝑖
𝑡) is defined as (Ishii, 
1975; Kataoka et al., 1986): 
𝑎̅𝑖

|𝒗𝑖∙𝒏𝑖|)

[1-21] 
where 𝒗𝑖 and 𝒏𝑖 are the interfacial velocity and interfacial unit normal vectors for the jth interface, 
and 𝑁 is the number of interfaces passing a point within the time interval 𝑇. Under certain 
statistical assumptions about 𝒗𝑖 and ni, the interfacial area concentration can be evaluated from 
the measured velocity obtained from a double-sensor probe (Kataoka et al., 1986; Wu and Ishii, 

26 
 
1999; Le Corre and Ishii, 2002). However, the double-sensor probe method is only applicable to 
bubbly flows where the bubbles can be regarded as approximately spherical in shape. More 
generally, the interfacial area concentration can be evaluated using a four-sensor conductivity 
probe (Kataoka et al., 1986). In comparison to the double-sensor probe depicted in Figure 1-4, a 
four-sensor probe has two additional downstream sensors. Thus, a four-sensor probe has three 
upstream-downstream sensor pairs from which three time delays can be measured. With these 
three measured time delays and the four-sensor conductivity probe geometry, the interfacial area 
concentration can be calculated without assuming a specific bubble shape (Kataoka et al., 1986; 
Tan and Ishii, 1990; Revankar and Ishii, 1993). In modern conductivity probe measurements, a 
data acquisition system is used to record the raw voltage signals from the sensors. Processing 
software is then used to condition the signals, pair the signals from the upstream and downstream 
sensors, and calculate the two-phase flow parameters.  
Owing to the complexity of two-phase flows and sensor-interface interactions with local 
probes, characterization of the uncertainties in measured two-phase flow parameters is a major 
challenge. Theoretical studies using Monte-Carlo analysis have been performed to study probe 
uncertainty and develop probe design criteria and correction factors which can be applied to 
measurements with double-sensor and four-sensor probes (Wu and Ishii, 1999; Le Corre and 
Ishii, 2002). Additionally, several experimental benchmarking studies have been undertaken that 
demonstrate the accuracy of the conductivity probe. The conductivity probe has been 
benchmarked against image analysis (Revankar and Ishii, 1993; Hibiki et al., 1998; Kim et al., 
2000), the optical fiber probe (Le Corre et al., 2003), and the wire mesh sensor (Manera et al., 
2009). These benchmark studies generally indicate ±10% agreement between the measured two-
phase flow parameters obtained with each method. However, few studies have been performed to 
systematically assess the effects of different components of a conductivity probe measurement 
system on the measured two-phase flow parameters.  

27

application of a conductivity probe in a two-phase flow system.

concentration, bubble velocity, and bubble Sauter-mean diameter obtained with conductivity 
probes. Kataoka (2010) and Lin and Hibiki (2014) provide substantial listings of additional 
experimental databases of local two-phase flow parameters for both adiabatic and heated flows.

1.3 Significance and Uniqueness of the Current Dissertation 
 
While there have been significant efforts to establish the interfacial area transport 
equation for purely one-group and established two-group flows, there has been no systematic 
study on the transition between the groups. This transition corresponds to the cap-bubbly flow 
regime, which, historically, has often been neglected in two-phase flow modeling. In order to 
fully realize the benefit of the two-group interfacial area transport equation as a constitutive 
model for the interfacial area concentration in the two-fluid model, it is paramount to consider the 
transition from bubbly flow to cap-bubbly flow. During this transition, group-I bubbles coalesce 
to form larger cap-shaped bubbles. Thus, the bubbly to cap-bubbly transition corresponds to the 
initiation of the transition from a one-group flow to a two-group flow. Robust models for the 
bubble interaction mechanisms that are responsible for the generation of group-II bubbles from 
group-I bubbles during the bubbly to cap-bubbly transition are essential to dynamically predicting 
the continuous progression from bubbly to cap-bubbly flows and through to more established 
two-group conditions such as slug and churn-turbulent flows. Therefore, the major unique 
objective of this dissertation is to develop interfacial area transport models to achieve a smooth 
transition from one-group to two-group flow conditions.  
 
In order to achieve this goal, an in-depth understanding of the two-group interfacial area 
transport formulation is required. Therefore, the one- and two-group interfacial area transport 
equations are derived in detail following the statistical approach in order to clearly establish the 
interfacial area transport equation framework applicable to bubbly, cap-bubbly, slug, and churn-
turbulent two-phase flows. A new approach is used in developing the constitutive models for the 
expansion/contraction source/sink terms in the interfacial area transport equations. It is identified 
that the existing expansion/contraction source/sink terms in the two-group interfacial area 
transport equations include the effects of intergroup particle interactions and intergroup 

30 
 
expansion/contraction. This causes these effects to be counted twice in the existing two-group 
interfacial area transport equations. Therefore, revised expansion/contraction source/sink terms 
are developed in this dissertation to correct this issue. This effort is a significant contribution to 
the research on interfacial area transport. 
 
While the available experimental database contains measurements in a wide range of 
two-phase flow conditions, the majority of the database exists either in purely one-group flows or 
two-group flows with a significant contribution of group-II bubbles (i.e. area-averaged group-II 
void fractions (〈𝛼2〉) greater than approximately 0.05). There are few conditions with low group-
II contribution, which are necessary to assess and develop models for the bubble interaction 
mechanisms responsible for transition between the groups. Therefore, to support assessment and 
development of the two-group interfacial area transport equation, an experimental database that 
highlights transition from one-group to two-group flows is required. The present dissertation 
establishes an experimental database of local two-phase flow parameters using the state-of-the-art 
four-sensor conductivity probe in vertical-upward air-water two-phase flows that highlights the 
transition from one-group to two-group flows.  
 
Additionally, investigations into several practical aspects of the conductivity probe 
measurement system are performed to improve the local conductivity probe measurement 
technique for two-phase flows. Specifically, the effects of signal “ghosting” electrical 
interference on the conductivity probe measurements are investigated experimentally with a novel 
conductivity probe design. Furthermore, the sensitivities of the measured two-phase flow 
parameters to sampling frequency, measurement duration, and orientation of the probe sensors are 
studied. Based on these studies, guidelines are provided to improve conductivity probe data 
acquisition.  
 
 

31 
 
1.4 Dissertation Objectives 
 
In view of the literature survey and the significance of the current work, the objectives of 
this dissertation are to: 
 
1) Derive and establish the interfacial area transport equation framework with a 
focus on intergroup transfer. 
2) Improve the local conductivity probe measurement technique through systematic 
investigation. 
3) Establish an experimental database and characterize transition phenomena in 
one-group to two-group transition flows. 
4) Develop interfacial area transport models for dynamic transition from one-group 
to two-group flows. 

32 
 
  
Chapter 2
 
Formulation of the Interfacial Area Transport Equation:  
Derivation of New Expansion Source Terms 
The derivation of both the one-group and two-group interfacial area transport equations is 
revisited in this Chapter following an approach similar to Ishii and Kim (2004). The derivation of 
the equations is discussed in detail in order to clearly establish the interfacial area transport 
equation framework applicable to bubbly, cap-bubbly, slug, and churn-turbulent two-phase flows. 
Moreover, a new approach is used in developing the constitutive models for the expansion source 
terms in the interfacial area transport equations. The appropriate forms of the expansion source 
terms in the interfacial area transport equations are discussed in view of maintaining consistency 
with other interfacial area concentration source terms and with the two-fluid model. 
 
Before discussing the particle distribution function transport equation in Section 2.1 that 
forms the basis of the interfacial area transport equation, it is noted that the development in this 
Chapter is presented from the perspective of dispersed-gas bubbles in a continuous-liquid. Thus, 
the terms “particle” and “bubble” may be used interchangeably, and the nomenclature reflects 
that the gas-phase is under consideration. However, this choice of perspective does not detract 
from the generality of the approach to developing an interfacial area transport equation for other 
flows with dispersed, interacting fluid particles such as dispersed-liquid droplets continuous-gas. 
2.1 Particle Distribution Function Transport Equation  
 
Many gas-liquid two-phase flows consist of discrete fluid particles of one phase (e.g. gas 
bubbles or liquid droplets) that are dispersed throughout a continuous region of the other phase. 

33 
 
Physically, the interfacial area concentration is related to the surface area of the dispersed-phase 
particles. As such, the interfacial area concentration depends on the number of dispersed-phase 
particles and particle size, which can be characterized by the volume of a particle (𝑉). In view of 
this, the interfacial area transport equation can be formulated based on a transport equation for a 
particle distribution function, analogous to the classical Boltzmann transport equation. 
 
Define 𝑓(𝒙, 𝑉, 𝑡) to be the particle distribution function such that the quantity 
𝑓(𝒙, 𝑉, 𝑡)𝑑3𝑥𝑑𝑉 is the expected number of particles within a spatial volume 𝑑3𝑥 at position 𝒙 
that have particle volumes within 𝑑𝑉 of 𝑉 at time 𝑡. In other words, 𝑓(𝒙, 𝑉, 𝑡) is the number 
density that describes the expected number of particles per unit mixture and particle volume. 
Figure 2-1 schematically shows the physical space and particle volume space that are under 
consideration. The transport equation that describes how 𝑓(𝒙, 𝑉, 𝑡) evolves can be written as 
(Hulburt and Katz, 1964; Delhaye, 2001; Ishii and Kim, 2004): 
𝜕𝑓
𝜕𝑡+ ∇⋅(𝑓𝒗) + 𝜕
𝜕𝑉(𝑓𝑉̇ ) = ∑𝑆𝑗
𝑗
+ 𝑆𝑛𝑢𝑐 
[2-1] 
 
Considering the left-hand side of Equation [2-1], the first term represents the local time 
rate of change of the particle distribution function. The second term accounts for the advection of 
the particle distribution function in physical space, where 𝒗= 𝒗(𝒙, 𝑉, 𝑡) is the average velocity of 
particles with volume 𝑉. The third term describes the flux of the particle distribution function 
through the particle volume space, where  𝑉̇ = 𝑉̇ (𝒙, 𝑉, 𝑡) is the time rate of change of particle 
volume following a particle. Physically, the third term represents the change in the particle 
distribution function due to the expansion/contraction of existing particles.  The 
expansion/contraction of existing particles can occur due to evaporation/condensation across the 
fluid particle interfaces and changes in the particle mass density. On the right-hand side of 
Equation [2-1], 𝑆𝑗 represents the particle source/sink rate per unit mixture and bubble volume due 

34 
 
to the jth-type particle interaction mechanism (i.e. breakup and/or coalescence). Lastly, 𝑆𝑛𝑢𝑐 is the 
net source/sink rate due to nucleation/collapse phenomena, such as homogenous or heterogeneous 
nucleation in the bulk flow, nucleation at a heated wall, or flashing.  
 
In previous studies (Kocamustafaogullari and Ishii, 1995; Ishii and Kim, 2004), 𝑆𝑛𝑢𝑐 was 
denoted as 𝑆𝑝ℎ, where “ph” indicated “phase change.” It is important to recognize that 𝑆𝑛𝑢𝑐, or 
𝑆𝑝ℎ, only accounts for phase change phenomena that are responsible for the creation/destruction 
of particles. Evaporation/condensation across the interfaces of existing particles is distinct from 
nucleation/collapse phenomena since it does not involve the creation/destruction of particles. 
Evaporation/condensation across the interfaces of existing particles, resulting in their 
expansion/contraction, is already accounted for in the third term on the left-hand side of Equation 
[2-1]. In view of this, evaporation/condensation across the interfaces of existing particles and 
nucleation/collapse phenomena are identified as two distinct classes of phase change processes in 
the present work, and the subscript “nuc” is used instead of “ph” to clarify this point. The 
importance of this distinction will become more apparent later in this Chapter as the interfacial 
area transport equation is developed from Equation [2-1].  
 
Figure 2-1: Schematic of the physical and particle volume spaces. 

35 
 
 
The particle distribution function transport equation is too detailed for use in practical 
analyses of two-phase flow where one is typically interested in the evolution of macroscopic two-
phase flow parameters such as the void fraction or interfacial area concentration. However, 
transport equations for such parameters can be derived from Equation [2-1] by averaging with 
respect to the particle volume. If the particle transport phenomena (e.g. particle shape, particle 
interaction mechanisms, interfacial forces, etc.) do not vary significantly with particle volume, 
then the particles can be regarded as a single group, and transport equations for the macroscopic 
two-phase flow parameters of interest can be obtained by averaging over the entire particle 
volume domain. Dispersed bubbly two-phase flow, where bubbles can be approximated as 
spherical in shape and are subject to similar interfacial forces and interaction mechanisms, is an 
example of a situation where a one-group formulation is appropriate. If there are substantial 
differences in the transport phenomena for particles of different sizes, however, then the particle 
volume domain must be discretized into an appropriate number of groups, and averaging must be 
performed over each group to derive a set of multi-group transport equations; this is addressed 
further in Section 2.3. In view of these considerations, one-group transport equations for three 
important two-phase flow parameters, the number density, void fraction, and interfacial area 
concentration, are developed in Section 2.2. 
2.2 One-Group Transport Equations 
Averaging Equation [2-1] to obtain one-group transport equations for two-phase flow 
parameters of interest involves multiplying Equation [2-1] by an appropriate weighting function 
and integrating over all particle volumes. In order to develop a concise framework to obtain 
transport equations for the quantities of interest, this process is first performed with an arbitrary 
weighting function to develop a general one-group transport equation in Section 2.2.1. A similar 

36 
 
approach has been used by Yao and Morel (2004). The general one-group transport equation is 
then used in the remainder of this Section to obtain one-group transport equations for the number 
density, void fraction, and interfacial area concentration.

The one-group void transport equation given by Equation [2-21] must agree with the gas-
phase continuity equation in the two-fluid model. In fact, the void transport equation cannot be 
considered as a separate, independent equation from the continuity equation in the set of 
governing equations. However, by demanding agreement between the void transport and 
continuity equations, one can develop constitutive relationships for 𝑉̇ , 𝜂𝑗, and 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐 in Equation 
[2-21]. As can be seen in Section 2.2.4, a constitutive model for 𝑉̇  is required to obtain the 
interfacial area transport equation. As such, following a new approach, Equation [2-21] is 
combined with the gas-phase continuity equation in Section 2.2.3.2 to establish constitutive 
relationships for these terms.     
2.2.3.2 Development of Constitutive Model for 𝑽̇

Note that the total mass generation rate of the gas-phase (𝛤𝑔) includes both 
evaporation/condensation across existing interfaces and nucleation/collapse phenomena. These 
different processes can be explicitly accounted for by expressing 𝛤𝑔 as: 
𝛤𝑔= 𝛤𝑔,𝑖+ 𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐 
[2-24] 
𝛤𝑔,𝑖 is the net mass generation rate of the gas-phase due to evaporation/condensation across 
existing interfaces, as indicated by the subscript “i.” 𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐 is the net mass generation rate of the 
gas-phase due to nucleation/collapse phenomena, as indicated by the subscript “nuc.” As an aside, 
recall that the interfacial transfer condition on mass, Equation [1-4], requires that the total mass 
generation rate of the gas-phase must be equal and opposite to that of the liquid-phase. Therefore, 
it is also reasonable to require that corresponding mass generation rates for the liquid-phase (𝛤𝑓,𝑖 
and 𝛤𝑓,𝑛𝑢𝑐) be given as: 
𝛤𝑓,𝑖= −𝛤𝑔,𝑖 
[2-25] 
𝛤𝑓,𝑛𝑢𝑐= −𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐 
[2-26] 
Thus, the total mass generation rate of the liquid-phase (𝛤𝑓) would be the sum of 𝛤𝑓,𝑖 and 𝛤𝑓,𝑛𝑢𝑐.

 Recognizing that the terms in Equation [2-27] correspond to distinct physical 
phenomena, one can compare the left- and right-hand sides of Equation [2-27] to infer

∑𝜂𝑗

= 0 
[2-28] 
As previously mentioned, particle volume is expected to be conserved during particle interactions 
(i.e. breakup and/or coalescence). In other words, the coalescence of two, or more, particles yields 
a particle with volume equal to the sum of the original particle volumes. Likewise, the breakup of 
a particle yields two, or more, particles with a combined volume equal to the volume of the 
original particle. In view of this, one can go further and postulate that each 𝜂𝑗 is separately equal 
to zero, too, not just their sum as suggested by Equation [2-28].  
 
In a similar manner, both the left- and right-hand sides of Equation [2-27] each contain a 
term associated specifically with nucleation/collapse phenomena. Therefore, one can infer that:

continuity equation. Additionally, recall that Equation [2-20] defines 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐 as an integral over

requirement between models for 𝑆𝑛𝑢𝑐 and 𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐. These consistency requirements also directly 
impact the terms associated with nucleation/collapse phenomena in the interfacial area transport 
equation, which are discussed in more detail in Section 2.2.4.3.

As previously discussed, Equation [2-35] shows that 𝑉̇  consists of contributions due to 
mass generation across the interfaces of existing particles and changes in the particle mass 
density. Equation [2-36] can provide a slightly different interpretation. The first two terms inside 
the brackets in Equation [2-36] represent the total change in void fraction. The void fraction 
source/sink due to nucleation/collapse phenomena (𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐) is subtracted from the total change in 
void fraction. This shows that 𝑉̇  is given by the change in void fraction excluding the change due 
to nucleation/collapse phenomena, which are responsible for the creation/destruction of particles.        
 
The expressions for 𝑉̇   presently obtained, Equations [2-35] and [2-36], are similar to 
those obtained by Ishii and Kim (2004) using a different approach based on a postulated particle 
mass balance. Regardless of the approach used, the void transport equation is used in conjunction 
with the continuity equation to develop constitutive relationships for the source terms in the void 
transport equation. The results obtained for 𝑉̇  are used to obtain the one-group interfacial area 
transport equation in Section 2.2.4.

source/sink to the interfacial area concentration that accounts for the effect of particle

interfacial area transport equation, this expansion/contraction source/sink term must be specified. 
Along with the constitutive model for 𝑉̇  that was developed in Section 2.2.3.2, a model for 𝐴𝑖(𝑉) 
is required to complete the specification of the expansion/contraction source/sink term. A model 
for 𝐴𝑖(𝑉) is presented in Section 2.2.4.2 that is used to complete the development of the one-
group interfacial area transport equation in Section 2.2.4.3. However, note that if a different 
model for 𝐴𝑖(𝑉) is desired, Equation [2-42] should be the starting point for developing the final 
form of the one-group interfacial area transport equation.  
2.2.4.2 Constitutive Model for 𝑨𝒊(𝑽) 
 
In the one-group approach, it is assumed that the average surface area of particles with 
volume 𝑉 can be described by the same function (i.e. 𝐴𝑖(𝑉)) for all sizes of particles; this loosely 
corresponds to assuming that all particles have the same shape. For dispersed-gas-continuous-
liquid two-phase flows, the one-group approach is most appropriate for bubbly two-phase flows 
where bubbles can be assumed to be approximately spherical for simplicity. Rather than directly 
assuming that the particles are spherical, however, one can follow Ishii and Kim’s (2004) 
approach to develop a model for  𝐴𝑖(𝑉) that can account for deviations from a spherical shape.

size distribution. 𝐷𝑆𝑚 is distinct from 𝐷𝑆𝑚

𝜙𝑛𝑢𝑐 also require a particle shape model. If needed, 𝜑 could be incorporated into the models for 
these terms. Moreover, the shape factor approach to specifying 𝐴𝑖(𝑉) is used in Section 2.3 in the 
development of the two-group interfacial area transport equations as a convenient way to 
introduce different shapes for the bubbles in each group.   
 
Using Equation [2-50] in Equation [2-42], the one-group interfacial area transport 
equation is obtained as: 
𝜕𝑎𝑖
𝜕𝑡+ ∇⋅(𝑎𝑖𝒗𝑖) = 2
3 (𝑎𝑖
𝛼) [𝜕𝛼
𝜕𝑡+ ∇⋅(𝛼𝒗𝑔) −𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐] + ∑𝜙𝑗
𝑗
+ 𝜙𝑛𝑢𝑐 
[2-52] 
The terms on the left-hand side of Equation [2-52] represent the local time rate of change and the 
advection of the interfacial area concentration, respectively. The first term on the right-hand side 
of Equation [2-52] is the interfacial area concentration source/sink due to the 
expansion/contraction of existing particles that results from evaporation/condensation and 
changes in the particle mass density. The last two terms are the interfacial area concentration 
sources/sinks due to the jth-type particle interaction mechanisms and nucleation/collapse 
phenomena, respectively. It is important to recall that the expansion/contraction source/sink term 
originated from the left-hand side of the particle distribution function transport equation, 
Equation [2-1]. It accounts for the effect of the change in volume of existing particles on the 
interfacial area concentration, which does not involve the creation/destruction of particles. 
Therefore, it is distinct from the last two terms, which can be regarded as “true” sources/sinks in 
the sense that they account for processes that do involve the creation/destruction of particles. In 

52 
 
order to solve Equation [2-52] for the interfacial area concentration, 𝒗𝑖 must be specified. 𝛼 and 
𝒗𝑔 can be obtained from the continuity and momentum equations in the two-fluid model. 
Moreover, constitutive models for the 𝜙𝑗’s and 𝜙𝑛𝑢𝑐 are required. 
The expansion/contraction source/sink term in Equation [2-52] is driven by the total 
change in void fraction excluding the change due to nucleation/collapse phenomena. Since the 
total change in void fraction would be determined from the solution of the two-fluid model field 
equations, it is imperative that the models for 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐 and 𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐 agree as prescribed by Equation [2-
29]. If the model used for 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐 in Equation [2-52] is inconsistent with that used for 𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐 in the 
two-fluid model field equations (i.e. violating Equation [2-29]), the change in void fraction due to 
nucleation/collapse phenomena would not be correctly excluded from the total change in void 
fraction. Consequently, the expansion/contraction source/sink term would incorrectly include the 
effects of nucleation/collapse phenomena on the interfacial area concentration, and the effects of 
existing particle expansion/contraction on the interfacial area concentration due to 
evaporation/condensation across existing interfaces and changes in the particle mass density 
would be misrepresented. Moreover, if 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐 and 𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐 are inconsistent, the effects of 
nucleation/collapse on the interfacial area concentration would be included twice in Equation [2-
52]: once, incorrectly, in the expansion/contraction source/sink term and again in 𝜙𝑛𝑢𝑐, the 
correct source/sink term. As such, it is of utmost importance to ensure that Equation [2-29] is 
satisfied when developing source/sink term models and implementing the interfacial area 
transport equation given by Equation [2-52] with the two-fluid model.   
 
Additionally, the models for 𝜙𝑛𝑢𝑐 and 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐 should also be consistent. Accounting for a 
phase change process in the model for 𝜙𝑛𝑢𝑐 and not including it in the model for 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐, or 𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐 
for that matter, could lead to a similar situation where the effect of that process on the interfacial 
area concentration would be incorrectly included in both the expansion/contraction source/sink 

53 
 
term and in 𝜙𝑛𝑢𝑐. For illustrative purposes, consider adding an additional source term to the right-
hand side of Equation [2-52] that accounts for the change in interfacial area concentration due to 
evaporation/condensation of existing particles (𝜙𝑒𝑣𝑎𝑝). If 𝜙𝑒𝑣𝑎𝑝 is added to the right-hand side, a 
corresponding 𝜂𝑒𝑣𝑎𝑝 term that is analogous to 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐 would have to be included in the existing 
expansion/contraction source term in order to prevent double-counting the effect of 
evaporation/condensation on the interfacial area concentration.   
 
To avoid the possibility of inconsistently specifying 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐, one can alternatively use 
Equation [2-35] for 𝑉̇  to evaluate the expansion/contraction source/sink term in Equation [2-42]. 
Then, the one-group interfacial area transport equation can be expressed in an equivalent form as: 
𝜕𝑎𝑖
𝜕𝑡+ ∇⋅(𝑎𝑖𝒗𝑖) = 2
3 (𝑎𝑖
𝛼) [𝛤𝑔,𝑖
𝜌𝑔
−𝛼
𝜌𝑔

𝐷𝑡] + ∑𝜙𝑗
𝑗
+ 𝜙𝑛𝑢𝑐 
[2-53] 
The form of the expansion/contraction source/sink term in Equation [2-53] may be less prone to 
error since 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐 does not have to be specified. Furthermore, this form more explicitly shows the 
contributions to expansion/contraction due to evaporation/condensation across the interfaces of 
existing particles and changes in the particle mass density. However, other considerations, such as 
the effects on the numerical solution of the equation, should be evaluated when deciding whether 
to use the form of the expansion/contraction source/sink term given in Equation [2-52] or [2-53].  
 
The present development of the one-group interfacial area transport equation has 
provided valuable insight into the relationship among 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐, 𝜙𝑛𝑢𝑐, and 𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐 that must be 
considered to consistently develop models for the source/sink terms in the interfacial area 
transport equation and incorporate it with the two-fluid model. Having examined the development 
of the one-group interfacial area transport equation in detail, the motivation for a multi-group 
interfacial area transport approach and, specifically, the development of the two-group transport 
equations is considered in Section 2.3.   

54 
 
2.3 Two-Group Transport Equations 
In the one-group approach, Equation [2-1] is integrated over all particle volumes 
assuming that the particle transport phenomena are similar for all sizes of particles. In general 
dispersed-gas-continuous-liquid two-phase flows, the transport phenomena can vary significantly 
with bubble size. If there are substantial differences in the transport phenomena of different size 
particles, then the particle volume domain must be discretized into an appropriate number of 
groups, and averaging must be performed over each group to derive a set of multi-group transport 
equations. The appropriate number of groups must be chosen such that the important differences 
in the transport phenomena of different size particles are adequately captured while maintaining a 
manageable number of equations. Currently, in view of drag characteristics and major interaction 
mechanisms, bubbles are categorized into two groups (Ishii and Kim, 2004).  Group-I includes 
spherical and distorted bubbles, while group-II includes cap, slug, and churn-turbulent bubbles. 
The boundary between the groups is defined by a critical bubble volume (𝑉𝑐), which is assumed 
to be approximately constant within a given two-phase flow condition. 
 
Following the same approach used to develop the one-group transport equations, general 
two-group transport equations are first established in Section 2.3.1 to provide a concise 
framework for obtaining two-group transport equations for quantities of interest. Then, the 
general two-group transport equations are used in the remainder of this Section to obtain transport 
equations for the group number densities, void fractions, and interfacial area concentrations.

multiplied by the arbitrary weighting function 𝜓. Instead of integrating Equation [2-6] over all

Note that the third terms on the left-hand sides of Equations [2-54] and [2-55] do not integrate to 
zero since the particle distribution function evaluated at the group boundary has not been assumed 
to be zero. Therefore, additional terms (i.e. ±(𝜓𝑘𝑓𝑉̇ )|𝑉𝑐) occur on the left-hand sides of 
Equations [2-56] and [2-57] that are not present in the general one-group transport equation, 
Equation [2-11]. These terms are intergroup expansion/contraction source/sink terms since they 
represent the transfer between the groups due to the expansion/contraction of existing particles 
that cause them to cross over the group boundary. As discussed in the one-group development, 
the expansion/contraction of existing particles can occur due to evaporation/condensation across 
the particle interfaces and changes in the particle mass density. By adding Equations [2-56] and 
[2-57], a total general transport equation can be obtained. The intergroup expansion/contraction 
source/sink terms should cancel each other in this total equation, which places restrictions on how 
the ±(𝜓𝑘𝑓𝑉̇ )|𝑉𝑐terms are specified. 
 
Similar to the one-group development, appropriate choice of 𝜓1 and 𝜓2 in Equations [2-
56] and [2-57] yields transport equations for average quantities of interest with respect to particle 
volume. Using the general two-group transport equations given by Equations [2-56] and [2-57], 
transport equations for the group number densities, void fractions, and interfacial area 
concentrations are developed in Sections 2.3.2, 2.3.3, and 2.3.4, respectively.

The first and second terms on the left-hand sides of Equations [2-66] and [2-67] account for the

interfaces and changes in the particle mass density. If Equations [2-66] and [2-67] are added

other as previously mentioned. The terms on the right-hand sides are the source/sink rates to the

for the group number densities. Also, the intergroup expansion/contraction source/sink terms 
must be specified; an approach to modeling these terms is discussed in Section 2.3.3.2. Lastly, 
constitutive models for the source/sink terms on the right-hand side are required.

intragroup interactions) and interactions that occur between particles in different groups (i.e. 
intergroup interactions). Consequently, not all of the 𝜂𝑗,𝑘’s can be assumed to be independently 
zero as was done in the one-group formulation. This is discussed further in Section 2.3.3.2.
