61 
 
 
In the two-group approach, the two-fluid model must be extended to include gas-phase 
continuity equations for each bubble group (Ishii and Kim, 2004; Sun et al., 2003). The two-
group void transport equations given by Equations [2-76] and [2-77] must agree with the gas-
phase continuity equations for each bubble group. Additionally, the total void transport equation 
must agree with the total gas-phase continuity equation. As previously discussed in the one-group 
development, the void transport and gas-phase continuity equations cannot be considered as 
independent equations. Rather, the equations can be used in conjunction with each other to 
develop constitutive relationships. As such, following an approach similar to that used in the one-
group development, the two-group void transport and continuity equations are combined in 
Section 2.3.3.2. In particular, constitutive relations for 𝑉̇  are obtained, which are used to complete 
the two-group interfacial area transport equations in Section 2.3.4.3.  
2.3.3.2 Development of Constitutive Model for 𝑽̇  
To start, let us consider the gas-phase continuity equations that are required in the two-
fluid model with the two-group approach. Assuming that the mass density of the gas-phase is the 
same for each group, the gas-phase continuity equations for each group can be written as: 
𝜕𝛼1𝜌𝑔
𝜕𝑡
+ ∇⋅(𝛼1𝜌𝑔𝒗𝑔1) = 𝛤𝑔1 + 𝑚1 
[2-78] 
𝜕𝛼2𝜌𝑔
𝜕𝑡
+ ∇⋅(𝛼2𝜌𝑔𝒗𝑔2) = 𝛤𝑔2 + 𝑚2 
[2-79] 
where 𝛤𝑔1 and 𝛤𝑔2 are the total mass generation rates for group-I and group-II, respectively, due 
to phase change. Now with two bubble groups, there can be mass transfer between the groups due 
to intergroup particle interactions and expansion/contraction of particles across the group 

62 
 
boundary. Therefore, 𝑚1 and 𝑚2 are the net intergroup mass transfer rates for group-I and group-
II, respectively, that account for these effects. 
 
Adding the group continuity equations together must yield the total gas-phase continuity 
equation. The total gas-phase continuity equation, which is given by Equation [2-22], is repeated 
here for convenience: 
𝜕𝛼𝜌𝑔
𝜕𝑡
+ ∇⋅(𝛼𝜌𝑔𝒗𝑔) = 𝛤𝑔 
[2-22] 
In the two-group approach, the parameters in Equation [2-22] should be regarded as total 
parameters, that is, a combination of group-I and group-II parameters. The appropriate 
combinations are given by the following constitutive relationships that are required to obtain 
Equation [2-22] by adding Equations [2-78] and [2-79]: 
𝛼= 𝛼1 + 𝛼2 
[2-80] 
𝛤𝑔= 𝛤𝑔1 + 𝛤𝑔2 
[2-81] 
𝑚1 + 𝑚2 = 0 
[2-82] 
𝒗𝑔= 𝛼1𝒗𝑔1 + 𝛼2𝒗𝑔2
𝛼
 
[2-83] 
 
Equation [2-80] simply expresses that the total void fraction is equal to the sum of the 
group void fractions, which is a direct consequence of the continuity of the gas-phase. Likewise, 
Equation [2-81] indicates that the total mass generation rate of the gas-phase due to phase change 
is the sum of the total mass generation rates due to phase change for each group. Although mass 

63 
 
can transfer between the groups due to intergroup particle interactions and expansion/contraction 
of particles across the group boundary, these effects should not cause a change in the total mass 
of the gas-phase. Therefore, Equation [2-82] requires that the net intergroup mass transfer rates 
for each group (𝑚1 and 𝑚2) be equal and opposite to each other; this is analogous to the 
interfacial mass transfer condition between the gas and liquid phases, Equation [1-4]. Note that 
the form of total gas-phase velocity given by Equation [2-83] is based on the assumption that the 
mass density of the gas-phase is the same for each group and, hence, for the total gas-phase, too. 
 
It is noted that the two-group continuity equations are currently presented in a slightly 
different manner than in previous works on the two-group interfacial area transport equation 
(Ishii and Kim, 2004) and development of a modified two-fluid model to include additional 
balance equations for the two bubble groups (Sun et al., 2003; Ishii and Hibiki, 2006). In the 
previous works, a term Δ𝑚̇ 12 is defined as the intergroup mass transfer rate from group-I to 
group-II due to intergroup particle interactions and particle expansion/contraction across the 
group boundary, and intergroup mass transfer from group-I to group-II is defined to be positive. 
Therefore, Δ𝑚̇ 12 is subtracted from the right-hand side of the group-I continuity equation and 
added to the right-hand side of the group-II continuity equation in the previous works (Ishii and 
Kim, 2004; Sun et al., 2003; Ishii and Hibiki, 2006). In the present approach, distinct intergroup 
mass transfer terms (𝑚1 and 𝑚2) are included on the right-hand sides of their respective group 
continuity equation. Then, by demanding that the sum of the group continuity equations yields the 
total gas-phase continuity equation in order to guarantee conservation of mass of the gas-phase, a 
constitutive relationship between  𝑚1 and 𝑚2, Equation [2-82], is obtained. The present approach 
to the intergroup mass transfer rates is completely analogous to the specification of the mass 
generation rates due to phase change (𝛤𝑘) in the conventional two-fluid model (Ishii, 1975). 
 
As in the one-group development, evaporation/condensation across existing interfaces 
and nucleation/collapse phenomena can be identified as two distinct processes that may require 

64 
 
separate modeling considerations. Therefore, these different processes can be explicitly accounted 
for by expressing 𝛤𝑔1 and 𝛤𝑔2 as: 
𝛤𝑔1 = 𝛤𝑔1,𝑖+ 𝛤𝑔1,𝑛𝑢𝑐 
[2-84] 
𝛤𝑔2 = 𝛤𝑔2,𝑖+ 𝛤𝑔2,𝑛𝑢𝑐 
[2-85] 
where 𝛤𝑔1,𝑖 and 𝛤𝑔2,𝑖 are the net mass generation rates for group-I and group-II, respectively, due 
to evaporation/condensation across the interfaces of existing particles within each group. 
Likewise, 𝛤𝑔1,𝑛𝑢𝑐 and 𝛤𝑔2,𝑛𝑢𝑐 are the net mass generation rates for group-I and group-II, 
respectively, due to nucleation/collapse phenomena. Granted, direct nucleation/collapse of large 
group-II bubbles is physically unlikely to occur. However, a treatment may be required to account 
for the sudden creation/destruction of large bubbles if the time scale of the processes leading to 
their creation/destruction is small compared to the macroscopic time scale. Therefore, in the 
interest of maintaining generality, group-II nucleation/collapse source/sink terms are included 
throughout the present work.   
 
Recalling Equation [2-24], which is repeated below for convenience, the total mass 
generation rate of the gas-phase can be expressed in a similar manner: 
𝛤𝑔= 𝛤𝑔,𝑖+ 𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐 
[2-24] 
where 𝛤𝑔,𝑖 is the net mass generation rate of the gas-phase due to evaporation/condensation across 
existing interfaces, and 𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐 is the net mass generation rate of the gas-phase due to 
nucleation/collapse phenomena. In view of this and Equations [2-84] and [2-85], 𝛤𝑔,𝑖 and 𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐 
can be expressed in terms of the group mass generation rates as: 

65 
 
𝛤𝑔,𝑖= 𝛤𝑔1,𝑖+ 𝛤𝑔2,𝑖 
[2-86] 
𝛤𝑔,𝑛𝑢𝑐= 𝛤𝑔1,𝑛𝑢𝑐+ 𝛤𝑔2,𝑛𝑢𝑐 
[2-87] 
 
Lastly, in view of the interfacial mass transfer condition between the phases, Equation [1-
4], the corresponding mass generation rates for the liquid-phase can be written as: 
𝛤𝑓,𝑖= −(𝛤𝑔1,𝑖+ 𝛤𝑔2,𝑖) 
[2-88] 
𝛤𝑓,𝑛𝑢𝑐= −(𝛤𝑔1,𝑛𝑢𝑐+ 𝛤𝑔2,𝑛𝑢𝑐) 
[2-89] 
Again, the total mass generation rate of the liquid-phase (𝛤𝑓) would be the sum of 𝛤𝑓,𝑖 and 𝛤𝑓,𝑛𝑢𝑐.

As previously discussed, Equation [2-108] shows that 𝑉̇  consists of contributions due to 
mass generation across the interfaces of existing particles and changes in the particle mass density 
within each group. Equivalently, Equation [2-109] shows that 𝑉̇   is related to the change in the 
group void fractions excluding the changes due to nucleation/collapse phenomena and intergroup 
transfer. As mentioned in the one-group development, nucleation/collapse phenomena are 
responsible for the creation/destruction of particles. Likewise, intergroup transfer can be regarded 
as the creation/destruction, or perhaps more correctly the addition/removal, of particles within a 
group.  𝑉̇  is the rate of change of volume of existing particles. Therefore, processes that 
create/destroy particles should not contribute to 𝑉̇ .  
 
Note that in view of furnishing the intergroup expansion/contraction source/sink terms, 
Equations [2-108] and [2-109] express 𝑉̇ |𝑉𝑐in terms of group-I parameters, which is consistent 
with Ishii and Kim’s (2004) study. The physical basis for this choice is that many two-phase flow 
conditions of practical interest, such as boiling two-phase flows in energy systems, will evolve 
from a purely one-group flow into a two-group flow. Therefore, it is likely that group-I bubbles 
will be present in the flow before group-II bubbles, so the transfer at the group boundary can be 
best described based on group-I parameters. This choice for 𝑉̇ |𝑉𝑐 does not prevent transfer back to 
group-I from group-II, however, as long as group-I bubbles are present in the flow. Alternatively, 

71 
 
one could consider assigning 𝑉̇ |𝑉𝑐 as a combination of the expressions valid in each group, such 
as the arithmetic mean (Fu, 2001). However, the complexity of the model for 𝑉̇ |𝑉𝑐 should be 
considered in regards to practical implementation and its relative importance in the equations.  
 
A model for the particle distribution function evaluated at the group boundary (𝑓|𝑉𝑐) is 
also required complete the intergroup transfer terms due to particle expansion/contraction across 
the group boundary. Currently, the model proposed by Ishii and Kim (2004) is used. Considering 
a simplified representation of the particle volume distribution function, Ishii and Kim (2004) 
specified the product (𝑉𝑓)|𝑉𝑐 as: 
(𝑉𝑓)|𝑉𝑐= 𝜒𝑛1 
[2-110] 
where 𝜒 is the intergroup expansion/contraction transfer coefficient that describes the shape of the 
particle volume distribution. The choice of specifying (𝑉𝑓)|𝑉𝑐 in terms of the group-I number 
density is again based on the physical consideration that there will be a natural progression from 
one-group to two-group flows.  
 
Observe that the group-I number density can be specified as: 
𝑛1 = 𝛼1
𝑉̅1
 
[2-111] 
𝑛1 = 𝑎𝑖1
𝐴̅𝑖1
   
[2-112] 
where 𝑉̅1 and 𝐴̅𝑖1 are the average group-I bubble volume and surface area, respectively. Equations 
[2-111] and [2-112] allow the group-I number density to be expressed in terms of either 𝛼1 or 𝑎𝑖1, 
parameters that would be available from the solution of the two-fluid model with the interfacial 
area transport equation. Appropriate models for 𝑉̅1 or 𝐴̅𝑖1 in terms of available parameters, 
however, are still required to complete the specification. For now, 𝑉̅1 and 𝐴̅𝑖1 are retained 
unspecified in the remainder of this development in the interest of maintaining generality.

The effect of expansion/contraction of particles across the group boundary on the 
intergroup mass and void transfer is difficult to analyze and model since it requires knowledge of 
the particle volume distribution function, which is not readily attainable. If it can be argued that 
the particle volume distribution is approximately zero near the group boundary, the contribution 
from intergroup transfer to due to expansion/contraction can be neglected, which corresponds to 
setting 𝜒=0. In this case, the intergroup mass transfer is only due to intergroup particle 
interactions.  Likewise, as can be seen in Section 2.3.4.3, with 𝜒= 0, the intergroup 
expansion/contraction source/sink terms are removed from the two-group interfacial area 
transport equations, leaving only intergroup particle interaction mechanisms to transfer interfacial 
area concentration between the groups.

⁄

76 
 
that include the effects of intergroup transfer. As argued above, 𝑉̇  describes the rate of change of 
volume of existing particles. This can occur due to evaporation/condensation across the interfaces 
of existing particles and changes in the particle mass density. Intergroup transfer includes the 
effects of intergroup particle interactions and particle expansion/contraction across the group 
boundary, which effectively create/destroy particles within a given group. Therefore, the effects 
of intergroup transfer should not be included in the model for 𝑉̇ . Excluding the effects of 
intergroup transfer from 𝑉̇   is similar to excluding the effects of nucleation/collapse phenomena. 
 
This point can be seen more directly by comparing Equations [2-121] and [2-122] 
proposed by Ishii and Kim (2004) with Equation [2-109] obtained in the present derivation. In the 
present derivation, the changes in group void fraction due to nucleation/collapse phenomena and 
intergroup transfer are subtracted from the total change in group void fraction. This leaves the 
change in group void fraction due to evaporation/condensation and changes in the gas-phase mass 
density to drive 𝑉̇ . While the effects of nucleation/collapse phenomena are removed (or assumed 
negligible in the case of group-II) in Ishii and Kim’s (2004) models, the change due to intergroup 
transfer is not. Therefore, the change in the group void fraction due to intergroup transfer is 
contributing to 𝑉̇  in Ishii and Kim’s (2004) expressions. This finding has a direct impact on the 
expansion/contraction source/sink terms in the two-group interfacial area transport equations, 
which is discussed in more detail in Section 2.3.4.3.

a specific particle shape. Therefore, in this respect, Equations [2-136] and [2-137] can be 
considered as a general form of the two-group interfacial area transport equations. However, the 
third and fourth terms on the left-hand sides of Equations [2-136] and [2-137] still explicitly 
contain the particle distribution function. The third terms are intergroup expansion/contraction 
source/sink terms due to evaporation/condensation across existing interfaces and changes in the 
particle mass density that cause particles to cross over the group boundary, leading to transfer of 

80 
 
interfacial area concentration between the groups. The fourth terms are intragroup 
expansion/contraction source/sink terms due to evaporation/condensation across existing 
interfaces and changes in the particle mass density that account for the effect of 
expansion/contraction of particles that remain within their respective group.  In order to obtain a 
useable form of the two-group interfacial area transport equations these inter- and intragroup 
expansion/contraction source/sink terms must be specified. Along with the constitutive model for 
𝑉̇  that was developed in Section 2.3.3.2, a model for 𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉) is required to complete the 
specification of these terms. A general approach to expressing particle surface area as a function 
of particle volume using a shape factor was presented in Section 2.2.4.2. This approach is utilized 
in Section 2.3.4.2 to specify 𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉) for assumed group-I and group-II bubble shapes. These 
results are then used to complete the development of the two-group interfacial area transport 
equations in Section 2.3.4.3. However, note that if a different model for 𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉) is desired, 
Equations [2-136] and [2-137] should be the starting point for developing the final form of the 
two-group interfacial area transport equations.  
2.3.4.2 Constitutive Model for 𝑨𝒊𝒌(𝑽) 
Before discussing the models for 𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉) for some assumed bubble shapes, it is necessary 
to examine some geometric scaling considerations of two-phase flows. In a two-phase flow, it is 
important to consider how various bubble length scales compare to other characteristic lengths of 
the flow. For example, in confined two-phase flows it is important to consider bubble length 
scales with respect to the length scale(s) of the duct cross-section. If the bubble length scales are 
comparable to the length scale(s) of the duct, the bubble shapes and interaction mechanisms can 
be affected. Therefore, these scale effects must be characterized so that they can be accounted for 
in the two-group interfacial area transport approach.   

81 
 
 
Two important bubble length scales in the two-group approach are the maximum 
distorted bubble diameter and the maximum cap bubble diameter given by Equations [1-12] and 
[1-15], which are repeated here for convenience: 
𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥= 4√
𝜎
𝑔Δ𝜌 
[1-12] 
𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥= 40√
𝜎
𝑔Δ𝜌 
[1-15] 
𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥 is a bubble length scale that characterizes when the bubble shape, uninfluenced by wall 
effects, changes from distorted to cap-shaped. This corresponds to a change in the bubble’s 
interfacial drag characteristics (Ishii and Zuber, 1979). Once a cap bubble reaches a certain size, 
instability of the interface leads to its breakup into smaller cap bubbles. 𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥 characterizes 
this maximum cap bubble size at which breakup occurs (Kataoka and Ishii, 1987; Kitscha and 
Kocamustafaogullari, 1989; Brooks et al., 2012). When uninfluenced by wall effects, 𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥 can 
be considered as the length scale characterizing the boundary between group-I and group-II 
bubbles, while 𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥 characterizes the maximum group-II bubble size. Many factors including 
the complex shapes of actual bubbles, influence of surrounding bubbles, wall effects, liquid phase 
turbulence, fluid impurities, etc. govern bubble size in a two-phase flow and cannot be captured 
by simple expressions like Equations [1-12] and [1-15]. Therefore, 𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥 and 𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥 should 
not be regarded as hard set criteria, but rather as approximate length scales that provide estimates 
of when to expect the change in bubble shape from distorted to cap and the maximum cap bubble 
size, respectively. It is clear that the relationship between these bubble length scales and the duct 
length scale will govern the two-group flow structure.  

82 
 
Let us define 𝐿𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡 as the length scale of the duct cross-section that dictates the two-
group flow structure. For many flow ducts, 𝐿𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡 can be considered as the duct hydraulic 
diameter. However, for duct cross-sections with a large aspect ratio, the largest dimension of the 
cross-section may be a more appropriate duct length scale, since bubbles can grow to fill that 
dimension. For comparison, it is convenient to non-dimensionalize 𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥, 𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥, and 𝐿𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡 
by the Laplace length (𝐿𝑎= √𝜎𝑔Δ𝜌
⁄
) yielding: 
𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥
∗
= 𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥

[2-138]

[2-140] 
With these parameters, one can identify three duct size categories, large, moderate, and 
small sized ducts, that must be considered when modeling the group-II bubble shape and two-
group interaction mechanisms. In large size ducts, a “dispersed two-group” flow structure exists 
where group-II bubbles of all sizes are relatively unaffected, at least in one direction in the cross-
section, by the duct walls. Consequently, the group-II bubbles are cap-shaped and the two-group 
bubble interaction mechanisms are not restricted by the duct walls. This dispersed two-group flow 
structure occurs during the transition from a one-group to a two-group flow where group-II 
bubbles are created. It also persists into more established two-group flows as group-II bubbles 

83 
 
continue to grow in size. The maximum group-II bubble size without wall effects is determined 
by the stability of the cap bubble interface that is characterized by 𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥
∗
. Therefore, ducts 
with 𝐿𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡
∗
 greater than approximately 𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥
∗
 can be defined as large size ducts. 
 
In a moderate size duct, 𝐿𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡
∗
 is large enough such that a dispersed two-group flow 
structure can occur during the transition from a one-group to a two-group flow. Since 𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥
∗
 
characterizes the smallest group-II bubble size without wall effects, a lower bound on 𝐿𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡
∗
 for 
moderate size ducts can be chosen as approximately 2𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥
∗
. With this criterion, the wall effects 
on the group-II bubble shape can be assumed to be minimal such that group-II bubbles can be 
assumed to be cap-shaped during transition. Additionally, this criterion allows for group-II 
bubbles to be distributed in the cross-section. As group-II bubbles continue to grow in a moderate 
size duct, however, they will become comparable to 𝐿𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡
∗
 and, consequently, influenced by the 
duct walls. This leads to a “confined two-group” flow structure in established two-group flows in 
moderate size ducts where the duct walls strongly influence the shape of the group-II bubbles and 
constrain the two-group bubble interactions. The group-II bubbles will fill the duct cross-section 
and elongate to form slug bubbles in an established two-group flow in moderate size ducts. Thus 
stable slug flows can occur in moderate size ducts, but not in large size ducts since group-II 
bubbles will break up before they can fill the duct cross-section and elongate. Therefore, the 
upper bound on 𝐿𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡
∗
 for moderate size ducts is approximately 𝐷𝑐𝑎𝑝,𝑚𝑎𝑥
∗
. 
 
Lastly, a duct with 𝐿𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡
∗
 less than approximately 2𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥
∗
 can be classified as a small 
size duct. In small size ducts, a dispersed two-group flow structure cannot exist. Group-II bubbles 
are strongly influenced by the duct walls, and as such a confined two-group flow structure will 
exist during group-transition and in established two-group flows. In fact, if the duct size is small 
enough, cap-shaped bubbles may not even form during group transition; elongated group-II 
bubbles that fill the cross-section will form directly. The interactions for group-II bubbles, and 

84 
 
even group-I bubbles if the duct size is small enough, are geometrically constrained by the duct 
walls to be primarily along the axial direction of the flow in a small size duct. 
 
Table 2-1 summarizes the different duct size categories. The reader is cautioned that the 
bounds on these categories are only provided as a rough guide to introduce the effect of duct 
geometry in the two-group approach. Many factors will influence these bounds, which should be 
refined using experimental data.

group-I bubble shape is that 𝜑1 is constant. This assumption is slightly less restrictive than 
directly assuming that the group-I bubbles are spherical (i.e. 𝜑1 = 1), as is done in the previous 
work on the two-group interfacial area transport equation (Ishii and Kim, 2004; Sun et al., 2004; 
Fu and Ishii, 2002a). Retaining 𝜑1 as a constant in the formulation provides for the option to 
account later on for the effect of deviations from a spherical shape if necessary. A consequence of 
assuming that 𝜑1 is constant is that it drops out of the group-I intragroup expansion/contraction 
source/sink term in Equation [2-136], and the resulting prefactor on that term is 
2𝑎𝑖1 3𝛼1 = 4/𝐷𝑆𝑚1
⁄
. Thus, the group-I intragroup expansion/contraction source/sink term scales 
on the inverse of 𝐷𝑆𝑚1 when 𝜑1 is constant.    
 
As discussed above, group-II bubbles in dispersed two-group flows will be cap-shaped. 
For many moderate and large size ducts, the group-II bubble shape in dispersed two-group flows 
can be approximated as a spherical-cap. Figure 2-2(a) schematically shows the side view a 
spherical-cap bubble. The aspect ratio of a spherical-cap (𝐸𝑐𝑎𝑝) can be defined as the ratio of its 
height (ℎ) to its base-width (2𝑎). It can be shown that 𝐸𝑐𝑎𝑝 can be expressed as a function of only 
the wake angle (𝜃) as:

Equation [2-46], is also only a function of the wake angle given by:

Although the wake angle does depend on the Reynolds number of the cap bubble (Clift et 
al., 1978), which is a function of the cap bubble’s volume equivalent diameter and consequently 
the cap bubble’s volume, it rapidly approaches a nearly constant value of 50° for bubble 
Reynolds numbers greater than about a 100.  Table 2-2 compares the bubble Reynolds number 
based on the maximum distorted bubble diameter (𝑅𝑒𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥), which corresponds to the minimum 
cap bubble size, for ambient air-water and saturated steam-water conditions. The saturated steam-
water conditions are selected based on the nominal primary side pressures for typical pressurized 
water reactor (PWR) and boiling water reactor (BWR) nuclear power plants (Todreas and 
Kazimi, 2012). Also, the bubble terminal rise velocity in an infinite medium is used as the 
relevant bubble velocity scale to evaluate the bubble Reynolds number. For each of these 
conditions, the bubble Reynolds number is on the order of 1000. Therefore, it is reasonable to 
assume that the wake angle and consequently 𝐸𝑐𝑎𝑝 and 𝜑𝑐𝑎𝑝 are constant for many two-phase 
flow conditions of interest in experimental and practical systems.

Since this dissertation considers two-group flows in moderate diameter round pipes, a 
model for the group-II bubble shape in confined two-group flow (i.e. a slug bubble shape) is also 
presently considered. Similar to Ishii and Mishima (1980) and Fu and Ishii (2002a), an idealized 
slug bubble can be approximated as having a spherical-cap-shaped nose and a cylindrical body 
with a base diameter 𝐷𝑠𝑙𝑢𝑔= 𝜆𝐷ℎ. The factor 𝜆, which is less than one, represents the ratio of the 
slug bubble base diameter to the duct hydraulic diameter. The slug bubble base diameter is a 
fraction of the duct hydraulic diameter owing to the liquid film around the slug bubble. For the 
assumed slug bubble shape, the base-width of the spherical-cap-shaped nose is equal to 𝜆𝐷ℎ. 
Therefore, the height of the spherical-cap-shaped nose is equal to 𝐸𝑐𝑎𝑝𝜆𝐷ℎ, where 𝐸𝑐𝑎𝑝 is given 
by Equation [2-143] . The overall length of the slug bubble, which can be expressed as a function 

88 
 
of its volume, is denoted as 𝐿𝑠𝑙𝑢𝑔. Figure 2-2(b) shows the side view of the assumed slug bubble 
shape. The shape factor for the assumed slug bubble shape (𝜑𝑠𝑙𝑢𝑔) is given as: 
𝜑𝑠𝑙𝑢𝑔= 24𝑉−𝜋𝜆3𝐷ℎ

To specify the intergroup expansion/contraction source/sink terms in Equations [2-136] 
and [2-137], 𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉) evaluated at the group boundary is required. Recall that the intergroup 
expansion/contraction source/sink terms should cancel when Equations [2-136] and [2-137] are 
added together to obtain the total interfacial area transport equation. However, since different 
bubble shapes are assumed in each group, the models presented above for 𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉) are not equal 
when evaluated at the group boundary. As such, in order to ensure that the intergroup 
expansion/contraction source/sink terms cancel when obtaining the total interfacial area transport 
equation, the models presented above for 𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉) should be regarded as valid for bubble volumes 
within each bubble group, respectively, but not at the group boundary. At the group boundary, 
𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉) is defined to be 𝐴𝑖𝑐, For now, 𝐴𝑖𝑐 will be left unspecified and should be determined based 
on an appropriate shape for bubbles at the group boundary. 
 
Before proceeding to use the proposed models for 𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉) to complete the two-group 
interfacial area transport equations in Section 2.3.4.3, it is emphasized that the models presented 
for 𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉) in this Section were chosen with moderate diameter round pipes in mind. While they 
may be suitable to use in other duct geometries, one should assess their appropriateness before 
directly utilizing the above results. If different bubble shapes need to be assumed for a specific 
application, the approach outlined in this Section can provide guidelines for establishing new 
models for 𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉).

The left-hand sides of Equations [2-151] and [2-152] account for the local time rate of 
change and the advection of the group interfacial area concentrations, respectively. The first 
terms on the right-hand sides of the equations are the intragroup expansion/contraction 
source/sink terms. These terms represent the change in the group interfacial area concentrations 
due to expansion/contraction of existing particles within each group. This can occur due to 
evaporation/condensation across the interfaces of existing particles in the group and changes in 
the particle mass density. The second terms on the right-hand side of the equations are the 
intergroup expansion/contraction source/sink terms. These terms are sources/sinks to the group 
interfacial area concentrations due to expansion/contraction of particles across the group 
boundary. The remaining terms in the equations are the source/sink terms to the group interfacial 
area concentrations due to the jth-type particle interaction mechanisms, including both intra- and 
intergroup interactions (i.e. 𝜙𝑗,𝑘
𝑖𝑛𝑡𝑟𝑎 and 𝜙𝑗,𝑘
𝑖𝑛𝑡𝑒𝑟), and nucleation/collapse phenomena, respectively.

The differences observed between the intra- and intergroup expansion/contraction source/sink 
terms in Equations [2-151] and [2-152] and Equations [1-13] and [1-14] are due to the differences 
in the models obtained for 𝑉̇  in the present development and those used by Ishii and Kim (2004). 
Within the intra- and intergroup expansion/contraction source terms in Equations [2-151] and [2-
152], the effects of intergroup transfer are removed from the total change in the group void 
fractions; this is similar to how the effects of nucleation/collapse phenomena are removed. In this 
way, the changes in the group void fractions that drive the expansion/contraction source/sink 
terms in the present development are due to evaporation/condensation across the interfaces of 
existing particles and changes in the particle mass density. In Equations [1-13] and [1-14], the 
effects of intergroup transfer are not removed from the expansion/contraction source/sink terms. 
Therefore, the effects of intergroup transfer on the group interfacial area concentrations are 
effectively counted twice in Equations [1-13] and [1-14]. This situation is analogous to the 
example given in Section 2.2.4.3 where the effects of nucleation/collapse phenomena would be 
effectively double counted if 𝜂𝑛𝑢𝑐 is neglected in the expansion/contraction source/sink term.   
 
The form of the two-group interfacial area transport equations given by Equations [2-
151] and [2-152] is rather unwieldy. Also, it is prone to possible consistency issues similar to 
those discussed in Section 2.2.4.3 regarding the nucleation/collapse phenomena source/sink 
terms; now there can be possible consistency issues amongst the intergroup source/sink terms, as 

93 
 
well. To avoid possible inconsistencies in specifying the various source/sink terms and to 
simplify the equations, Equation [2-108] can be used to specify 𝑉̇  in Equations [2-136] and [2-

interfacial area transport equations:

The various terms in Equations [2-153] and [2-154] are interpreted in the same way as 
described above. In this form, it is clear that the expansion/contraction source/sink terms are 
driven by evaporation/condensation across the interfaces of existing particles and changes in the 
particle mass density. In order to solve Equations [2-153] and [2-154] for the group interfacial 
area concentrations, the velocities 𝒗𝑖1 and 𝒗𝑖2 need to be determined. The constitutive models for 
𝛤𝑔1,𝑖 and 𝛤𝑔2,𝑖 should be the same as those used in the group continuity equations. The group void 
fractions (𝛼1 and 𝛼2) and the velocities (𝒗𝑔1 and 𝒗𝑔2) in the derivatives of the gas-phase density 
(i.e. 𝐷𝑔1𝜌𝑔𝐷𝑡

 and𝐷𝑔2𝜌𝑔𝐷𝑡
⁄
) can be obtained from the solution of the continuity and 
momentum equations in the two-fluid model. Models are also required for 𝜒, 𝐴𝑖𝑐, and 𝐴̅𝑖1 to 
complete the intergroup expansion/contraction source/sink terms. Furthermore, constitutive 
models for the source/sink terms due to particle interactions and nucleation/collapse phenomena 
in each group (𝜙𝑗,𝑘 and 𝜙𝑛𝑢𝑐,𝑘) are required. 

94 
 
2.4 Chapter Summary 
The detailed derivation of both the one-group and two-group interfacial area transport 
equations following the statistical approach analogous to the Boltzmann transport equation was 
presented in this Chapter. A new approach that appealed directly to the necessary agreement 
between the void transport equation(s) and continuity equation(s) in the two-fluid model was used 
to develop constitutive models for the unknown terms in the void transport equation(s). In 
particular, models for the rate of change of particle volume (𝑉̇ ) were developed and used 
complete the interfacial area transport equation(s). 
For the one-group case, the model developed for 𝑉̇  using the present approach agreed 
with the previous model developed by Ishii and Kim (2004). Consequently, the resulting one-
group interfacial area transport equation obtained in the present work is identical to that obtained 
by Ishii and Kim (2004). The present development, however, provided valuable insight into the 
relationship between the void and interfacial area concentration source/sink terms due to 
nucleation/collapse phenomena and the corresponding mass generation rate in the gas-phase 
continuity equation. These terms were discussed in view of maintaining consistency when 
developing source/sink term models and incorporating the interfacial area transport equation with 
the two-fluid model. 
 For the two-group case, the present model for 𝑉̇  is different than that proposed by Ishii 
and Kim (2004). Ishii and Kim’s (2004) model for 𝑉̇  includes the effects of intergroup transfer 
due to intergroup particle interactions and expansion/contraction of particles across the group 
boundary. However, using the present approach by directly combining the group continuity and 
void transport equations, the resulting model for 𝑉̇  was found to not include these effects. This 
finding directly impacts the intra- and intergroup expansion/contraction source/sink terms in the 
two-group interfacial area transport equation. Effectively, by including the effects of intergroup 

95 
 
transfer in the model for 𝑉̇ , these effects are double counted in the two-group interfacial area 
transport equations. This issue is corrected in the present development.  
Additionally, through investigation into models for 𝐴𝑖𝑘(𝑉), it was found that if the 
particle shape factor for the kth bubble group (𝜑𝑘) is a constant, the intragroup 
expansion/contraction source terms in the interfacial area transport equations will always contain 
a prefactor of 2𝑎𝑖𝑘3𝛼𝑘
⁄
= 4 𝐷𝑆𝑚𝑘
⁄
. Thus, the intragroup expansion/contraction source/sink 
terms scale on the inverses of the group Sauter-mean diameters (i.e. 𝐷𝑆𝑚𝑘= 6𝛼𝑘/𝑎𝑖𝑘) when 𝜑𝑘 
is constant. However, if 𝜑𝑘 is not constant, the prefactor will be different than 2𝑎𝑖𝑘3𝛼𝑘
⁄
. To 
investigate this, two approximate group-II bubble shapes for moderate diameter round pipes were 
considered: a spherical-cap bubble and an idealized slug bubble. The shape factor for a spherical-
cap bubble only depends on the wake angle, which can be assumed to be constant in many two-
phase flows of practical interest. However, the shape factor for the idealized slug bubble depends 
on particle volume, which yields a prefactor of 4 𝜆𝐷ℎ
⁄
 instead of 2𝑎𝑖2 3𝛼2
⁄
 in the group-II 
intragroup expansion/contraction source/sink term. Therefore, for this case, the group-II 
expansion/contraction source/sink term is found to scale on the inverse of the slug bubble base 
diameter (i.e. (𝜆𝐷ℎ)−1). To generalize the effect of different group-II bubble shapes on the group-
II intragroup expansion/contraction source/sink term, an analytical expression for a group-II 
intragroup expansion/contraction source/sink term shape factor (𝛾2) was introduced.  
In view of the discussion in this Chapter regarding consistency of the source/sink terms, 
the recommended forms of the equations developed in this Chapter are summarized as follows:

Chapter 3
 
Experimental Investigations 
This Chapter discusses the experimental work performed in the present dissertation. First, 
an overview of the experimental facility that is utilized is provided in the Section 3.1. Then, 
efforts that were undertaken to improve the local multi-sensor conductivity probe measurement 
technique through systematic investigation of several practical aspects of the conductivity probe 
measurement system are discussed in Section 3.2. Lastly, the investigation into cap-bubbly 
transition two-phase flows to establish an experimental database that highlights transition from 
one-group to two-group flows is discussed in Section 3.3. 
3.1 Experimental Facility 
Experiments in air-water two-phase flow are performed in the Combinatorial Channel 
Two-Phase Flow Test Facility at the Advanced Multi-Phase Flow Laboratory at the Pennsylvania 
State University (Kim et al., 2008). The facility consists of both vertical and horizontal sections 
of 5.08cm inner diameter clear acrylic pipes connected by four glass 90° vertical elbows in a loop 
configuration. The facility was designed to study the geometric effects of vertical elbows and 
channel orientation change on two-phase flow transport. However, the focus of this dissertation is 
only on vertical-upward two-phase flow. Therefore, for the present study, the full-loop 
configuration of the facility was bypassed to create an exclusively vertical-upward test section. 
This expanded the range of two-phase flow conditions that could be investigated. The details of 
the vertical-upward air-water two-phase flow test facility are discussed in Section 3.1.1, and the 
instrumentation available at the facility is discussed in Section 3.1.2. 

98 
 
3.1.1 Vertical-Upward Air-Water Two-Phase Flow Test Facility 
Figure 3-1 shows a schematic of the vertical-upward air-water two-phase flow test 
facility, and a photograph of the test section is shown in Figure 3-2. The major components of the 
test facility include the: 1) air-water supply system, 2) dual-stage two-phase flow injector, 3) test 
section, 4) instrumentation ports, and 5) two-phase damper-separator system. The details of these 
components are discussed in Sections 3.1.1.1 through 3.1.1.5, respectively. 
 
 
Figure 3-1: Schematic of the vertical-upward air-water two-phase flow test facility. 

99 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 3-2: Photograph of the vertical-upward air-water two-phase flow test section; the 
instrumentation ports are labeled in red. 

100 
 
3.1.1.1 Air-Water Supply System 
A 15HP Quincy QGB15V rotary screw air compressor is used to provide compressed air 
to the test facility. The Quincy QGB15V compressor has an inlet capacity of 56.8 ACFM and 
provides compressed air at a gauge pressure of 689kpa (100psig).  After the compressor, an inline 
air dryer and filter system remove any moisture and contaminants from the compressed air that 
might have been introduced during the compression process. The dry, clean air is then fed to two 
air accumulator tanks that are connected in series with volumes 1.51m3 (400 gal.) and 0.45m3 
(120 gal.). The tanks provide sufficient capacity to minimize fluctuations in the air supply at high 
gas flow rates. From the air accumulator tanks, the air is regulated to a gauge pressure of 414kPa 
(60psig) and supplied to an array of air rotameters that meter the air flow to the test section.  
Water is supplied to the test section from a 2.27m3
 (600 gal.) water accumulator tank by a 
45kW (60HP) Dean Model pH-2140 centrifugal pump. The pump has a 0.22m (8.5 in.) impeller. 
An AC Tech MH4600B variable frequency drive is used to control the pump speed and, 
consequently, adjust the liquid flow rate. Prior to filling the water accumulator tank, the water is 
filtered through an active charcoal filter followed by a series of mixed resin bed filters to obtain 
deionized water.  Additionally, upstream of the test section, an inline 20μm filter is used to 
remove particulates that may enter the system. The filtered water is then treated with ammonium 
hydroxide and morpholine to control the conductivity and pH. The total ammonium hydroxide 
concentration in the water is 0.0012M, and the concentration of morpholine in the water is 
0.0015M. These chemicals provide conductivity in the water that is required to operate different 
instrumentation in the facility, and they minimize corrosion in the pump by making the water 
basic. The water conductivity is maintained at 100µS/cm, which is measured using a Hanna 
Instruments DiST3® (HI98303) conductivity meter. The calibration of the conductivity meter is 
checked regularly against standard calibration solutions from Hanna Instruments®.  

101 
 
3.1.1.2 Dual-Stage Two-Phase Flow Injector 
 
The air and water supplies join in a specialized dual-stage two-phase flow injector that is 
designed to introduce two-phase flow to the test section while maintaining a controlled inlet 
bubble size. This is achieved through a double-annulus liquid injection configuration. A 
schematic of the dual-stage two-phase flow injector is shown in Figure 3-3. The outer annulus is 
formed between an outer acrylic pipe with an inner diameter of 7.62cm (3in.) and an inner acrylic 
pipe with an outer diameter of 4.45cm (1.75in.). The outer and inner acrylic pipes are glued into a 
lower acrylic flange that is bolted to a stainless steel baseplate. The lower flange and baseplate 
have holes in their centers that line up with the inside of the inner pipe. A 3.18mm (0.125in.) O-
ring seals the lower flange against the baseplate. An upper acrylic flange is glued to the outer pipe 
such that the upper face of the flange is flush with the end of the outer pipe. A 7.63cm (3in.) inner 
diameter to 5.08cm (2in.) inner diameter acrylic reducer that is 7.63cm (3in.) long connects the 
upper flange of the injector to the first pipe of the test section. 3.18mm (0.125in.) O-rings seal the 
connections on each side of the reducer. The end of the inner pipe extends 2.54cm (1in.) beyond 
the upper flange into the reducer to minimize the change in flow area between the outer annulus 
and the test section. This is done to reduce the effects of the flow area change on the bubble 
injection.   
 
A 1.91cm (0.75in.) outer diameter stainless steel air sparger is centered within the inner 
acrylic pipe, which has an inner diameter of 3.18cm (1.25in.). The gap between the sparger and 
the inner acrylic pipe forms the inner annulus. The top 10.16cm (4in.) of the sparger is fabricated 
from sintered stainless steel with an average pore size of 10μm. A solid stainless end-cap is 
welded to the end of the sintered portion. The top of the end-cap is aligned to be even with the 
upper end of the inner pipe when the sparger is installed. The opposite end of the sparger is an 
open stainless steel tube that passes through the lower acrylic flange and a 3.81cm (1.5in.) 

102 
 
stainless steel tee that is welded to the underside of the stainless steel baseplate. The vertical leg 
of the tee that the sparger passes through has a 3.81cm (1.5in.) to 2.54cm (1in.) stainless steel 
reducer and 2.54cm (1in.) pipe stub welded to it. A 2.54cm (1in.) to 1.91cm (0.75in.) Swagelok® 
reducing union is used to connect the sparger to the pipe stub. The remaining portion of the 
sparger that passes through the reducing union is connected to the air-supply line through a 
Swagelok® elbow and check valve. When no air flow is supplied to the sparger, liquid can seep 
through the sintered stainless steel into the air line. Therefore, the check valve is installed in the 
air-supply line immediately before the connection to the sparger to minimize the volume where 
water could collect. Figure 3-4(a) shows a photograph of the dual-stage two-phase flow injector, 
acrylic reducer, and connection to the first pipe of the test section. Figure 3-4(b) shows a 
photograph of the underside of the dual-stage two-phase flow injector that highlights the 
connections to the sparger. 
 
Upstream of the injector, the water supply from the pump is separated into main and 
auxiliary liquid flows. The main liquid flow enters a manifold and is split further among three 
lines. These three lines supply the main liquid flow to the outer annulus through three 3.81cm 
(1.5in.) inlets that are spaced 120° apart around the circumference of the outer pipe to reduce 
swirling of the main liquid flow in the outer annulus. The auxiliary liquid flow is fed at 3.15x10-4 
m3/s (5gpm) to the horizontal leg of the stainless steel tee underneath the baseplate; see Figure 3-
4(b). As such, a constant liquid flow is supplied around the sparger in the inner annulus. Air is 
supplied to the sparger, and the constant auxiliary liquid flow in the inner annulus shears off 
bubbles at an average size of about 2-3mm from the porous end of the sparger, independent of the 
main liquid flow rate. Thus, only the main liquid flow rate supplied to the outer annulus is varied 
when setting a desired flow condition.  

103 
 
 
Figure 3-3: Schematic of the dual-stage two-phase flow injector. 
  
 
 
 
(a)  
(b) 
Figure 3-4: Photographs of the dual-stage two-phase flow injector. (a) Double-annulus injector 
body. (b) Auxiliary liquid and air supply connections underneath the injector baseplate. 

104 
 
3.1.1.3 Test Section 
The test section is constructed from clear cast acrylic pipes with a nominal inner diameter 
(D) of 5.08cm (2in.). The pipes and instrumentation ports are connected to each other through 
bolted flanged connections that are sealed by 3.18mm (0.125in.) O-rings. The instrumentation 
ports are discussed in detail Section 3.1.1.4. The pipes and instrumentation ports that were used to 
construct the vertical-upward test section were selected from among the available pipes and 
instrumentation ports at the Combinatorial Two-Phase Flow Test Facility (Kim et al., 2008). The 
inner diameters at both ends of the pipes and instrumentation ports were measured with a 
telescoping gauge and caliper. Adjacent pipes and instrumentation ports were selected to 
minimize differences in their inner diameters to ensure a smooth transition between the 
components at the flanged connections. The maximum and average differences between the inner 
diameters of adjacent flanged connections were 0.73mm (0.029in.) and 0.36mm (0.014in.), 
respectively. These differences in inner diameters correspond to maximum and average 
deviations at the inner pipe wall at the transition between adjacent flanged connections of 
0.36mm (0.014in.) and 0.18mm (0.007in.), respectively.  
The test section is supported along its length with acrylic connector blocks that are bolted 
to a surrounding steel structure. Figure 3-5 shows a schematic of a connector block. A connector 
block consists of two mating halves which have semicircular holes to accommodate a pipe with 
ample clearance. Threaded holes pass through the connector blocks at 120° apart around the 
circumference of the pipe.  3/8-16 PTFE threaded rods are tightened through these holes against 
the pipe to secure its position; PTFE is used to avoid scratching the acrylic pipe. Adjusting the 
threaded rods separately allows for precise alignment of the pipe sections. During assembly, great 
care was taken to align the test section to within ±0.1° of vertical using both a conventional spirit 
level and a digital level. The total assembled length of the test section is 3.35m (11ft.).  

105 
 
 
Figure 3-5: Schematic of the connector block used to support and align the test section. 
3.1.1.4 Instrumentation Ports 
The instrumentation ports provide access to the flow for various instruments and facilitate 
visualization of the flow. The instrumentation ports are constructed from blocks of clear cast 
acrylic that have a hole bored through their lengths with the same inner diameter as the pipe 
sections. Pressure taps are implemented in the instrumentation ports to obtain pressure 
measurements in the flow. Furthermore, the instrumentation ports facilitate precise positioning of 
the multi-sensor conductivity probe with a linear traverse so that local measurements of various 
two-phase flow parameters can be obtained throughout the cross-section. Lastly, a flat face is 
machined onto the port to reduce the optical distortion due to the curvature of the pipe wall 
during flow visualization with a high speed video camera. 
Two types of instrumentation port are available at the facility: 1) fixed and 2) rotating 
instrumentation ports. In vertical-upward two-phase flow, the distribution of two-phase flow 
parameters in the pipe cross-section can be assumed to be axisymmetric. Therefore, fixed 
instrumentation ports are available that allow the conductivity probe to be traversed across one 

106 
 
diameter of the pipe during an experiment. Figures 3-6(a) and (b) show an exploded view 
drawing and a photograph of a fixed instrumentation port, respectively. 
If the distribution of two-phase flow parameters is asymmetric, however, it is necessary 
to perform conductivity probe measurements throughout the entire cross-section. Horizontal two-
phase flows present such a case where the gas-phase distribution is inherently asymmetric due to 
buoyancy. The flow structure downstream of flow restrictions can also be asymmetric and present 
interesting features that could not be readily captured with a fixed instrumentation port design. 
Therefore, unique rotating instrumentation ports are available at the Combinatorial Two-Phase 
Flow Test Facility (Kim et al., 2008). These ports can be rotated around the axis of the pipe to 
every 22.5° without stopping the flow. This allows the conductivity probe to be traversed along 
eight diameters of the pipe to obtain measurements throughout the entire cross-section. Figures 3-
7(a) and (b) show an exploded view drawing and a photograph of a rotating instrumentation port. 
Four instrumentation ports are available along the length of the vertical-upward test 
section. The centers of the instrumentation ports are located at 7.5, 18, 34.5, and 63 pipe inner 
diameters downstream of the test section inlet. The bottom of the first pipe section that connects 
to the top of the acrylic reducer at the dual-stage two-phase flow injector is regarded as the test 
section inlet (i.e. L=0D). When constructing the vertical-upward test section, two fixed and two 
rotating instrumentation ports were used. The rotating instrumentation ports are 30.48cm (12in.) 
long, and the fixed instrumentation ports are 15.24cm (6in.) long. Therefore, the longer rotating 
instrumentation ports were used to obtain the desired axial measurement locations and to meet 
length requirements of the test section to connect to the bypass line to the to the damper/separator 
system. As a benefit, the rotating instrumentation ports provide the capability to confirm that the 
flow is axisymmetric in the vertical-upward test facility without stopping the flow. Rotating 
instrumentation ports, P1 and P3, are located at 18D and 63D from the test section inlet, and fixed 
instrumentation ports, P0 and P2, are located at 7.5D and 34.5D from the test section inlet.

assembled fixed instrumentation port with a conductivity probe installed.

3.1.1.5 Two-Phase Damper-Separator System 
After exiting the test section, the two-phase flow travels to a two-stage damper-separator 
system that is designed to separate the phases to prevent air from returning to the water 
accumulator tank and entering the pump. In the first stage, the damper slows down the flow and 
breaks up large gas pockets into smaller bubbles to promote better phase separation. The damper 
is constructed from a 0.106m3 (28 gal.) rectangular plastic tank. The flow is supplied to the 
damper through two inlet lines to counteract large scale rotation of the flow in the damper. Inside 
the damper, a series of three successively finer wire mesh screens are installed to facilitate 
breaking up the gas into smaller bubbles and reducing the inertia of the flow. After passing 
through the last screen, the flow exits through the bottom of the damper to the separator. 
The separator is constructed from a similar plastic tank. Inside the separator there are two 
perforated baffles. The baffles stagnate the flow in the separator, allowing the air to separate from 
the liquid. Plastic lids cover the damper and separator tanks during operation of the test facility.  
However, the damper-separator system is not sealed, such that the air exits to the room. The 
liquid exits the bottom of the separator to return to the water accumulator tank to be recirculated 
through the facility. Photographs of the damper and separator tanks are shown in Figure 3-8.  
 
Figure 3-8: Photograph of the damper (left) and separator (right) tanks. The plastic lids that cover 
the tanks during operation of the test facility are not shown to highlight the internal components.  

110 
 
3.1.2 Instrumentation 
3.1.2.1 Flow Meters 
 
For measurement of water flow rates, the test facility employs both rotameters and an 
electromagnetic flow meter. A Yamatake MagneW Two-wire PLUS electromagnetic flow meter 
(Model MTG18B) with an accuracy of ±0.5% is employed for water flow rates greater than 
1.0m/s because of lower sensitivity at smaller flow rates. Hence, for water flow rate 
measurements below 1.0m/s, a combination of rotameters with accuracies of ±2% of full scale are 
employed. The auxiliary flow rate in the dual-stage two-phase flow injector is metered by a 
rotameter with the same accuracy. The air flow rate is measured downstream of a pressure 
regulator using an array of Dwyer Series RMB rotameters with accuracies of ±3% of full scale.
 
Experimental conditions in the test section are specified by the superficial liquid velocity 
(〈𝑗𝑓〉) and the superficial gas velocity (〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧). By definition, the superficial liquid and gas 
velocities are the volumetric fluxes of liquid and gas through the pipe cross-section such that: 
〈𝑗𝑓〉= 𝑄𝑓
𝐴 
[3-1] 
〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧= 𝑄𝑔,𝑧
𝐴
= 𝑄𝑔,𝑎𝑡𝑚
𝐴
(
𝑝𝑎𝑡𝑚
𝑝𝑎𝑡𝑚+ 𝑝𝑧
) 
[3-2] 
𝑄𝑓 is the liquid volumetric flow rate through the cross-sectional area of the test section (𝐴). 
Likewise, 𝑄𝑔,𝑧 is the gas volumetric flow rate through the cross-sectional area of the test section. 
𝑄𝑔,𝑎𝑡𝑚 is the equivalent gas volumetric flow rate at atmospheric pressure, which is calculated 
from the gas flow rate set at the gas rotameters and the back pressure. 𝑝𝑎𝑡𝑚 is atmospheric 

111 
 
pressure, and 𝑝𝑧 is the gauge pressure. The subscript “z” indicates the axial location along the test 
section. For example, 〈𝑗𝑔〉18𝐷 corresponds to the gas superficial velocity evaluated at 18D from 
the inlet of the test section. Here, the brackets  〈
〉 are used to indicate that 〈𝑗𝑓〉 and 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧 
represent cross-sectional average quantities, as opposed to local quantities that are defined point-
wise in the cross-section like the local conductivity probe measurements. The brackets do not 
imply that an averaging operation was performed on local values of 𝑗𝑓 and 𝑗𝑔 to calculate 〈𝑗𝑓〉 and 
〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧. The data reduction procedures used to calculate 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧 at different axial locations in the test 
section based on the gas rotameter and pressure measurements are provided in Appendix A.  
 3.1.2.2 Differential Pressure Transducer and Dial Pressure Gauges 
Pressure is measured in the test section using a Yamatake ST3000 Series 900 (Model 
STD920) differential pressure transmitter with an accuracy of ±0.075% of the calibrated span.   
The pressure transducer can be used to measure gauge pressure by leaving one pressure port open 
to the atmosphere. Additionally, a 0-60psig dial gauge with an accuracy of ±1% of the full scale 
reading for pressures over the entire scale is available to perform gauge pressure measurements of 
the flow in the test section. This dial gauge is used in conjunction with the differential pressure 
transmitter to perform pressure measurements in the facility if the gauge pressures to be measured 
are outside the range of the transmitter. In this case, the dial gauge is used to measure the gauge 
pressure at a reference port (e.g. P0 (7.5D)), and the differential pressure transmitter is used to 
measure the differential pressures between the other ports. The gauge pressure at the exit of the 
air rotameters, referred to as the back pressure (𝑝𝑏𝑎𝑐𝑘), is measured using a 0-100psig dial gauge. 
For pressures within the first and last quarters of the scale on this gauge, the accuracy is ±2% of 
the full scale reading, and for pressures within the middle half of the scale (i.e. pressures within 
25% to 75% of the full scale) the accuracy is ±1% of the full scale reading. 

112 
 
3.1.2.3 High-Speed Video Camera 
A Photron 512 PCI FASTCAM high-speed video camera is available to perform flow 
visualizations. It has full resolution of 512 x 512-pixels up to 2000 frames per second (fps) and 
reduced resolution with increasing frame rate up to 32,000 fps. High intensity halogen and LED 
lights are available to provide adequate illumination of the flow to capture high speed videos. 
3.1.2.4 Local Four-Sensor Conductivity Probe 
The state-of-the-art four-sensor conductivity probe is used to acquire measurements of 
local time-averaged local two-phase flow parameters. A schematic of the four-sensor conductivity 
probe is shown in Figure 3-9. Some of the most important parameters that can be obtained with 
the conductivity probe are the: void fraction, interfacial area concentration, bubble velocity, 
bubble Sauter-mean diameter, and bubble frequency. The basic operating principle of the 
conductivity probe was discussed in Chapter 1. Also, Kim et al. (2000) provide a concise 
overview of the four-sensor conductivity probe methodology and signal processing scheme used 
in the present work, and additional details are given by Fu (2001) and Bernard (2014). As 
discussed in Chapter 1, previous benchmark studies indicate ±10% agreement between two-phase 
flow parameters obtained with the conductivity probe and other two-phase flow instrumentation 
(Kim et al., 2000; Le Corre et al., 2003; Manera et al., 2009).  
 
Figure 3-9: Schematic of a four-sensor conductivity probe (Kim et al., 2000). 

113 
 
3.1.2.5 Data Acquisition System 
A data acquisition system (DAQ) is used to acquire voltage measurements from the 
electromagnetic flow meter, pressure transducer, and four-sensor conductivity probe. The data 
acquisition system consists of a National Instruments PCI-6259 data acquisition board and 
shielded SCB-68 connector box. The PCI-6259 is a high speed multifunction M-series data 
acquisition board optimized for superior accuracy at fast sampling rates. It has 32 analog 16-bit 
inputs with a sample rate of 1.25 million samples per second (MS/s) for a single channel and 1 
MS/s (aggregate) for multichannel inputs. The SCB-68 is a noise rejecting shielded I/O connector 
block for use with 68-pin M-Series data acquisition devices. Measurements from the instruments 
are recorded using the National Instruments LabVIEW software. 
3.2 Improvement of Local Conductivity Probe Measurement System 
As discussed in Chapter 1, significant efforts have been made to develop and benchmark 
the conductivity probe, and it has been widely used to establish extensive databases of local two-
phase flow parameters. While the efficacy of the conductivity probe has been clearly 
demonstrated, few studies have been performed to systematically assess the effects of different 
components of a conductivity probe measurement system on the measured two-phase flow 
parameters. Therefore, to further advance the local multi-sensor conductivity probe measurement 
technique, several practical aspects of a conductivity probe measurement system are investigated 
including: 1) signal “ghosting” electrical interference among probe sensors, 2) data acquisition 
sampling frequency, 3) measurement duration, and 4) sensor orientation in the flow channel. The 
investigations into each of these items are discussed in Sections 3.2.1 through 3.2.4, respectively.       

114 
 
3.2.1 Signal “Ghosting” in Conductivity Probe Measurement 
Talley (2012) observed artificially high bubble velocity measurements and, consequently, 
artificially low interfacial area concentration measurements with increasing data acquisition 
sampling frequency (𝑓𝑠) while determining an appropriate sampling frequency for conductivity 
probe measurements in his study of horizontal bubbly two-phase flows. This behavior was found 
to be due to a hardware related issue known as signal “ghosting” (DAQ M Series User Manual, 
2008). Signal ghosting is inherent when a multiplexing DAQ is used to sample multiple high 
impedance voltage sources, such as the conductivity probe sensors. In a multiplexing DAQ, a 
multiplexer switches between each input channel and a common analog-to-digital converter 
(ADC) in rapid succession to acquire digitized representations of the analog input signals at the 
specified sampling frequency. High source impedances sampled by the input channels increase 
the settling time of the DAQ’s instrumentation amplifier due to its capacitance. The increased 
settling time can cause remnants of a signal sampled on the previous channel to appear on the 
current channel being sampled (DAQ M Series User Manual, 2008). This effect becomes more 
pronounced at higher sampling frequencies, as the time between samples becomes closer to the 
DAQ’s settling time.  
Signal ghosting can affect the measured time delay between the upstream and 
downstream sensors, thus influencing the velocity and interfacial area concentration 
measurements. The effects of ghosting depend on sampling frequency and the impedance of the 
sensor circuit. Sampling frequency is a critical parameter in conductivity probe measurements, as 
the sampling frequency must be high enough to preserve the sensor signal quality for accurate 
analysis. An appropriate sampling frequency depends on several factors including bubble speed, 
size, and packing. Therefore, it is impractical to restrict sampling frequency to mitigate ghosting 
effects. The most robust solution to eliminating signal ghosting is to use a simultaneous sampling 

115 
 
DAQ, in which each input channel has a dedicated instrumentation amplifier and ADC, 
eliminating the need for a multiplexer. This DAQ architecture provides improved isolation 
amongst the sampled input signals and is not subject to signal ghosting. However, if a 
multiplexing DAQ is to be used, a revision to the conductivity probe circuit is required to reduce 
the source impedances of the sensors to eliminate the effects of signal ghosting.  
3.2.1.1 Conductivity Probe Circuit Revision 
Figure 3-10 shows a schematic of a simple DC circuit for the kth-sensor of a multi-sensor 
conductivity probe that has been used in previous studies (Cartellier and Achard, 1991; Kim et al. 
2000) The sensor can be thought of as having some resistance (𝑅𝑐,𝑘) when the sensor is in the 
liquid phase and having an effectively infinite resistance when the sensor is in the gas phase. 
Thus, the measured voltage (𝑉𝑘) indicates whether the sensor is in the liquid (low voltage signal) 
or gas phase (high voltage signal). Simply based on the switch-like behavior of the conductivity 
probe sensors, the conductivity probe circuit is inherently a high impedance source that is 
susceptible to ghosting with a multiplexing DAQ. 
To eliminate signal ghosting of high impedance sources, the source impedances seen by 
the DAQ must be reduced. This can be achieved through the addition of a voltage follower to the 
source circuits (DAQ M Series User Manual, 2008). The output of an ideal voltage follower 
exactly reproduces the input signal with zero impedance. Thus, a voltage follower can transform a 
high impedance source to a low impedance source. Figure 3-11 shows the revised conductivity 
probe circuit that incorporates a voltage follower to eliminate signal ghosting. A resistor (Rd) of 
value within the recommended input impedance range for the DAQ, is implemented between the 
output of the voltage follower and the DAQ to ensure stability of the op-amp. In the current 
study, Ra and Rd are 51kΩ and 500Ω resistors, respectively. Rb,k is a 1MΩ potentiometer. Since 

116 
 
each sensor in a multi-sensor conductivity probe has its own impedance, the corresponding 
potentiometer for each sensor is set to a different resistance value to maintain a baseline voltage 
level when the kth-sensor is in the liquid phase. The potentiometer resistances are set to around 
200kΩ for the probe sensors used in this study to maintain a baseline voltage level around 4V.    
 
Figure 3-10: Simple DC circuit for the kth-sensor in a multi-sensor conductivity probe. 
 
Figure 3-11: Revised circuit for the kth-sensor in a multi-sensor conductivity probe that 
incorporates a voltage follower to eliminate signal ghosting.  

117 
 
3.2.1.2 Experimental Investigation 
Experiments were performed to characterize the effects of signal ghosting on the 
conductivity probe measurement with the original circuit and to assess the performance of the 
revised circuit. In order to obtain concurrent measurements with the two circuits to directly 
compare the ghosted and un-ghosted signals obtained from the same bubbles, a specialized 
conductivity probe, referred to herein as a dual double-sensor probe, was fabricated.  
The sensors of the dual double-sensor probe are shown in Figure 3-12. Following the 
design of the miniaturized four-sensor conductivity probe (Kim et al., 2000) the probe sensors 
were fabricated from stainless steel acupuncture needles. The probe was designed with two 
upstream sensors (sensors 0 and 1) and two downstream sensors (sensors 2 and 3) to form two 
independent double-sensor probes that could be used to perform concurrent measurements with 
the original and the revised circuits. Recall from Figure 1-4 that the sensors in a double-sensor 
probe are electrically insulated from each other. Each sensor is connected to a grounded metallic 
probe body through the surrounding conductive liquid in the flow. When a sensor is located 
inside of a bubble, the circuit is broken, yielding a rise in the measured voltage for that sensor. 
The dual-double sensor probe operates in the same way. Having two double sensor probes at 
effectively the same position in the flow allows one to characterize the effects of the original and 
revised conductivity probe circuits on the measured two-phase flow parameters based on the 
same bubbles. As such, the upstream sensors were positioned such that the sensing tips were as 
close as possible to minimize differences in the measurements. Likewise, the downstream sensors 
were positioned at approximately the same axial distance from the upstream sensors. Sensors 0 
and 3 were chosen as one double-sensor pair, and sensors 1 and 2 were chosen as the other 
double-sensor pair because these pairs were most in-line with each other. The axial distances 
between the upstream and downstream sensors for sensor pairs 0-3 and 1-2 were each 1.68mm.  

118 
 
 
Figure 3-12: Dual double-sensor conductivity probe fabricated to investigate the signal 
characteristics of the original and revised conductivity probe circuits.  
 
Measurements of co-current vertical-upward air-water two-phase flow were performed 
with the dual double-sensor probe to investigate the effects of signal ghosting on the conductivity 
probe measurement. The dual-double sensor probe was installed in P2 (L=34.5D) in the vertical-
upward test section. This axial location was chosen for the measurements since the flow was 
determined to be sufficiently developed by this point. Measurements were performed in a flow 
condition with 〈𝑗𝑓〉 = 2.00 m/s and 〈𝑗𝑔〉34.5𝐷 = 0.19m/s. This bubbly flow condition was selected 
because multiple measurements had been previously performed with the revised circuit, providing 
confidence in the repeatability of the results. Measurements were acquired by traversing the dual-
double sensor probe across the pipe diameter in non-dimensional radial increments (r/R) of 0.1. 
Here, r is the dimensional radial coordinate, and R is inner radius of the test section. Data was 
acquired at each r/R for 60s at 𝑓𝑠=50kHz. This sampling frequency is similar to that used by 
Talley (2012) where significant ghosting effects were observed. To isolate the sensor pairs, two 
National Instruments PCI-6259 M-Series DAQs were used. One DAQ was used to measure the 
signals from one sensor pair connected through the original circuit, and the other DAQ was used 
to measure the signals from the other sensor pair connected through the revised circuit. Figure 3-
13 shows a schematic of the data acquisition setup with the dual-double sensor conductivity 
probe. Concurrent measurements were acquired with sensor pair 0-3 connected through the 
original circuit, while sensor pair 1-2 was connected through the revised circuit; this is denoted as 
“Circuit-Sensor-Pair Combination I.” Also, measurements were acquired with the opposite 

119 
 
circuit-sensor-pair combination (i.e. sensor pair 1-2 connected through the original circuit and 
sensor pair 0-3 connected through the revised circuit) without stopping the flow to identify any 
differences between the sensor pairs and separate DAQs. The latter combination is denoted as 
“Circuit-Sensor-Pair Combination II.” 
 
Figure 3-13: Schematic of the setup of data acquisition systems with dual double-sensor 
conductivity probe for signal ghosting investigation.  
Figure 3-14 shows the raw signals acquired with the dual double-sensor probe for a single 
bubble to demonstrate the characteristics of ghosting in the conductivity probe measurement. 
There are two distinct features of the signals from pair 1-2, connected through the original circuit, 
that are indicative of ghosting. The bubble first contacts sensor 1, causing its signal to rise. Even 
though the bubble has not contacted sensor 2 yet, a premature rise in the sensor 2 signal occurs 
due to ghosting off of the rising sensor 1 signal. When the bubble actually contacts sensor 2, the 
signal begins to rise faster. A similar behavior occurs on the falling edge of the sensor 1 signal. 
The bubble leaves sensor 1 first, so the signal begins to fall. However, a sharp fall is delayed 

120 
 
because the sensor 1 signal ghosts off of the sensor 2 signal that is at the high voltage state. The 
signals from sensors 0 and 3 that were acquired with the revised circuit demonstrate the expected 
rise and fall characteristics of the raw signal.  
 
Figure 3-14: Comparison of voltage signals acquired with the original and revised circuits to 
identify the characteristics of ghosting in the conductivity probe signals.  
An ideal conductivity probe would yield step responses. However, owing to capacitance 
in the probe circuit and the wetting/de-wetting phenomena involved as the sensors penetrate the 
bubble interfaces, the rising and falling edges of the measured voltage signals have an 
exponential character. Therefore, a major task in processing the raw signals is to generate square 
signals with rising and falling edges that correspond to the points of contact with the front and 
rear interfaces of the bubbles. This is achieved through voltage thresholds and slope criteria to 
identify the points of contact with the interfaces as when the raw signals rise above and below the 
baseline voltage and maximum voltage within a given bubble, respectively (Kim et al., 2000) 

121 
 
Therefore, the premature rise of the downstream sensor is problematic because it can cause the 
rising edge to be identified too early, leading to error in the bubble velocity and interfacial area 
concentration measurements. The delayed fall of the upstream sensor was not observed to affect 
the measurements for this flow condition as the falling edge is identified at the high side of the 
signal. However, the delayed fall may become more important in higher void fraction conditions 
where bubbles follow each other more closely.   
The void fraction measurements obtained from each of the upstream sensors (0 and 1) 
showed negligible differences, which indicates that ghosting did not influence the void fraction 
measurement for this flow condition. However, significant effects of ghosting were observed in 
the bubble velocity and interfacial area concentration measurements. Figures 3-15(a) and (c) 
show the local bubble velocity and interfacial area concentration measurements, respectively, that 
were acquired with sensor pair 0-3 connected through the original circuit and sensor pair 1-2 
connected through the revised circuit. Likewise, Figures 3-15(b) and (d) show the results obtained 
with the opposite combination of sensor pairs and circuits. Significantly higher bubble velocities 
were measured with both sensor pairs connected through the original circuit compared to the 
revised circuit. Assuming that the bubbles follow the liquid in a dispersed bubbly flow, the 
bubble velocities measured with the original circuit are higher than expected, particularly for 
sensor pair 0-3, for this 〈𝑗𝑓〉 = 2.00m/s flow condition. The interfacial area concentration 
measurements obtained with the original circuit are lower than those obtained with the revised 
circuit since the interfacial area concentration is inversely proportional to the measured velocity. 
Furthermore, comparing the measurements obtained with the original circuit with the 
different sensor pairs, it is observed that the magnitude of the ghosting effect depends on the 
sensors. Owing to differences in tip condition, each sensor has its own impedance. Therefore, one 
can expect to see different effects of ghosting with the different sensors. Sensor pair 0-3 with the 
original circuit yields much higher bubble velocity, and consequently lower interfacial area 

122 
 
concentration, measurements compared to the measurements with sensor pair 1-2 with the 
original circuit. On the other hand, the measurements obtained with each sensor pair with the 
revised circuit are very comparable. Thus, the revised circuit removes the variability that can 
occur with the original circuit due to ghosting. 
 
 
(a) vg, Circuit-Sensor-Pair Combination I  
(b) vg, Circuit-Sensor-Pair Combination II 
 
 
(c) ai, Circuit-Sensor-Pair Combination I   
(d) ai, Circuit-Sensor-Pair Combination II 
Figure 3-15: Effect of signal ghosting on bubble velocity and interfacial area concentration 
measurements with 𝑓𝑠=50kHz. (a) Bubble velocity and (c) interfacial area concentration 
measurements acquired with sensor pair 0-3 connected to the original circuit (subject to ghosting) 
and sensor pair 1-2 connected to the revised circuit (ghosting removed). (b) Bubbly velocity and 
(d) interfacial area concentration measurements acquired with sensor pair 1-2 connected to the 
original circuit (subject to ghosting) and sensor pair 0-3 connected the revised circuit (ghosting 
removed). (Flow condition: 〈𝑗𝑓〉 = 2.00 m/s and 〈𝑗𝑔〉34.5𝐷 = 0.19 m/s; L=34.5D)

In addition to comparing the local bubble velocity measurements, one can compare the 
volumetric gas flux (i.e. 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧) calculated from the local conductivity probe measurements to the 
volumetric gas flux based on the gas rotameter and pressure measurements. An estimate of the 
volumetric gas flux can be obtained by area-averaging the product of the local void fraction and 
bubble velocity measurements obtained with the conductivity probe (i.e. 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧= 〈𝛼𝑣𝑔〉𝑧). The 
area-average is performed by numerically integrating the product of the local the local 𝛼 and 𝑣𝑔 
measurements obtained with the local conductivity probe in the pipe cross-section. The 
volumetric gas flux based on the gas rotameter and pressure measurements is given by Equation 
[3-2]. The data reduction procedures used to calculate 〈𝛼𝑣𝑔〉𝑧 and 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧 are provided in Appendix 
A. Comparing 〈𝛼𝑣𝑔〉𝑧 to 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧 provides a way to benchmark the local conductivity probe 
measurements against the gas rotameter and pressure measurements.  
Table 3-1 shows a comparison of 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧 calculated in each way for each circuit-sensor pair 
combination shown in Figure 3-15. 〈𝛼𝑣𝑔〉𝑧 based on measurements acquired with the original 
circuit is greater than 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧 for both sensor pairs, which further supports that ghosting can cause 
higher bubble velocity measurements than expected. Moreover, the percent differences between 
〈𝛼𝑣𝑔〉𝑧 and 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧 for each sensor pair, +32% for pair 0-3 and +8% for pair 1-2, are significantly 
different, which clearly demonstrates the artificial variability in the measurement that can result 
from ghosting. 〈𝛼𝑣𝑔〉𝑧 based on measurements acquired with the revised circuit differ from 〈𝑗〉𝑧 
by about -10% for each sensor pair, demonstrating the improved consistency between the sensor 
pairs by removing the ghosting.

a conventional four-sensor conductivity probe at P1 (L=18D). The same inlet flow condition that 
was used for the experiment with the dual-double sensor probe was used for these measurements. 
The measurements at each r/R location were first performed using the revised circuit. Then, 
without stopping the flow, the measurements were retaken using the original circuit. The 
measured velocities with the original circuit compared to those with the revised circuit are 
consistently higher due to signal ghosting. However, the differences are significantly reduced due 
to the lower sampling frequency. The expected trend of lower interfacial area concentration 
values obtained with the original circuit compared to the revised circuit is also observed. 
Although the effects of signal ghosting at the lower sampling frequency are less pronounced, 
signal ghosting still introduces a systematic error that can be eliminated with the revised circuit.

3.2.2 Data Acquisition Sampling Frequency Sensitivity 
Having removed the effects of signal ghosting, the revised conductivity probe circuit can 
be used to investigate the effects of sampling frequency on the measured two-phase flow 
parameters. The DAQ samples continuous sensor voltages at discrete points in time. This 
discreteness impacts every aspect of the signal analysis. Small bubbles or bubbles that just graze 
the sensor may not be identified if their voltage signals contain an insufficient number of points. 
Similarly, as bubble velocity increases, higher sampling frequencies are required to adequately 
resolve the bubble signals so that they are identified. Not identifying bubbles directly impacts the 
measured bubble number frequency (𝑓𝑏), the number of bubbles detected by the upstream sensor 
divided by the measurement duration. The measured void fraction can also be affected by missing 
bubbles. Additionally, in view of Equation [1-19], the uncertainty in the measured void fraction 
depends on the sampling frequency since the sampling frequency influences the uncertainty in the 
measured gas residence times. Likewise, the sampling frequency affects the uncertainty in the 
measured time delays between the upstream and downstream sensors and, consequently, the

measured velocities. Thus, the sampling frequency also affects the measured interfacial area 
concentration since it depends on the number of interfaces detected and the measured velocities. 
To study the effect of sampling frequency on the measured two-phase flow parameters, 
conductivity probe measurements were performed in 13 two-phase flow conditions, which are 
given in Table 3-2. The flow conditions span the bubbly and slug flow regimes on the Mishima 
and Ishii (1984) flow regime map. The measurements were performed at P2 (L=34.5D) at r/R=0.0 
with a conventional four-sensor conductivity probe (Kim et al., 2000). In view of the maximum 
aggregate sampling frequency that the NI PCI-6259 DAQ is capable of, the sensors of a four-
sensor conductivity probe can each be sampled at a maximum sampling frequency of 250kHz. 
Therefore, measurements were acquired in each flow condition at 250kHz for a duration of 60s.

duration sensitivity studies.
