4.3 Chapter Summary 
 
The newly established experimental database that highlights one-group to two-group 
transition was used to assess the available two-group interfacial area transport model for 
moderate diameter pipes (Fu 2001; Fu and Ishii, 2002a) in TRACE-T. Predictions of the 
experimental data were made using the systems analysis code TRACE-T (Bernard, 2014) with 
Fu’s (2001) two-group source/sink term models. Major observations from the assessment include: 
 
 
The intergroup coalescence mechanisms between group-I bubbles that are responsible for 
generating group-II interfacial area concentration and void fraction from group-I were not 
active in the predictions of the one-group to two-group transition conditions. 
 
 
Intergroup wake-entrainment of group-I bubbles into group-II bubbles was effectively 
inactive in the predictions. Physically, this is a major mechanism during the transition 
process from one-group to two-group flows.  
 
 
The shearing-off mechanism was an active source of group-I interfacial area 
concentration in conditions with low contribution of group-II bubbles. This is unphysical, 
since the cap-bubbly flows at the beginning of transition from one-group to two-group 
flows do not contain group-II bubbles of sufficient size for shearing-off to occur.    
 
These observations clearly establish the need for an improved two-group interfacial area transport 
model for one-group to two-group transition flows.  

206 
 
  
Chapter 5
 
Development of Interfacial Area Transport Model 
for One-Group to Two-Group Transition Flows  
In the two-group interfacial area transport equation framework, source terms for the 
intergroup coalescence of group-I bubbles to form group-II bubbles are responsible for the 
generation of group-II interfacial area concentration and void fraction starting from a purely one-
group condition.  Furthermore, the coalescence of group-I bubbles with group-II bubbles drives 
the evolution of the flow towards an established two-group flow condition. The modeling 
approach to developing a two-group interfacial area transport model for one-group to two-group 
transition flows is discussed in Section 5.1. In Section 5.2, source/sink terms for the interfacial 
area concentration and void fraction are developed to establish a two-group interfacial area 
transport model for one-group to two-group transition flows. 
5.1 Modeling Approach 
5.1.1 Framework of One-Group to Two-Group Transition 
Figure 5-1 schematically shows the framework of the transition from one-group to two 
group flows. The flow starts as a purely one-group flow, where bubbles are dispersed and similar 
in size. Here, the one-group interfacial area transport equation (Wu et al., 1998; Kim, 1999) is 
applicable. As larger group-I bubbles become present in the flow, the bubble transport 
characteristics become wake-dominated. Wake-entrainment coalescence was identified early on 
in previous studies as the major mechanism responsible for the transition from bubbly to slug 

207 
 
flow (Radovcich and Moissis, 1962; Mishima and Ishii, 1984). Additionally, the transition void 
fraction distributions observed in the experiments are due to the migration of larger group-I 
bubbles towards the center of the duct. This occurs due to a change in direction of the transverse 
lift force owing to the wake structure that develops behind larger deformed bubbles (Tomiyama et 
al., 2002). This can be regarded as another signature of wake-dominated bubble transport. 
Therefore, increasing importance of wake-dominated bubble transport characterizes the initiation 
of the transition from one-group to two-group flows. As the transition progresses, group-II 
bubbles will grow in size through wake-entrainment coalescence interactions with group-I and 
other group-II bubbles, and an established two-group flow structure will develop. As observed in 
the experiments, the interfacial area concentration decreases early in transition due to the active 
coalescence leading to transfer between the groups. However, as a more established two-group 
flow structure develops, the interfacial area concentration begins to increase again, which is 
attributed to the shearing-off mechanism becoming active. These trends characterize the 
development of an established two-group flow structure in which shearing-off is the major source 
and wake-entrainment as the major sink to the interfacial area concentration. 
 
Figure 5-1: Framework of one-group to two-group transition in moderate size ducts. 

208 
 
The framework of the two-group interfacial area transport equations was established in 
Chapter 2. In the development, the boundary between group-I and group-II is specified through a 
critical bubble volume (𝑉𝑐). This critical volume should be defined by a critical bubble length 
scale (𝐷𝑐). In general, the critical bubble length scale that defines the group boundary must be 
considered as a variable to be determined for the flow under consideration:   
𝐷𝑐= 𝐷𝑐(𝑓𝑙𝑢𝑖𝑑 𝑝𝑟𝑜𝑝𝑒𝑟𝑡𝑖𝑒𝑠, 𝑑𝑢𝑐𝑡 𝑔𝑒𝑜𝑚𝑒𝑡𝑦, 𝑏𝑢𝑏𝑏𝑙𝑒 𝑡𝑟𝑎𝑛𝑠𝑝𝑜𝑟𝑡 𝑝ℎ𝑒𝑛𝑜𝑚𝑒𝑛𝑎, ) 
[5-1] 
With 𝐷𝑐 specified, the critical bubble volume at the group boundary can be defined as:  
𝑉𝑐= 𝜋
6 𝐷𝑐3  
[5-2] 
In view of the differences in interfacial drag characteristics and major bubble interaction 
mechanisms for smaller dispersed bubbles and larger cap/slug/churn-turbulent bubbles, the 
maximum distorted bubble limit (Ishii and Zuber, 1979) is considered as the critical bubble length 
scale for moderate size ducts: 
𝐷𝑐= 𝐷𝑑,𝑚𝑎𝑥= 4√
𝜎
𝑔Δ𝜌  
[5-3] 
Flow in a small size duct is a good example of where a different length scale would have to be 
chosen to define the group boundary; see Section 2.3.4.2 for considerations on duct size 
categorization in regards to two-group interfacial area transport modeling. In the case of a small 
size duct, the bubble length scale would become comparable to the channel length scale before 
the maximum distorted bubble size is reached. Therefore, the critical bubble length scale should 
depend on the channel length scale in the case of a small size duct. The present dissertation 

209 
 
considers flows in moderate size ducts, so the critical bubble length scale given by Equation [5-3] 
is appropriate to define the group boundary.  
If one considers moving through the one-group to two-group transition, the flow starts as 
a pure one-group flow. Therefore, the one-group models (Wu et al., 1998; Kim, 1999) for 
intragroup turbulence impact breakup, random collision coalescence, and wake-entrainment 
coalescence of group-I bubbles are retained. As the transition progresses, however, the structure 
of the flow progressively deviates from the pure one-group flow structure that was assumed in 
developing the one-group models. Although the intragroup interactions of group-I bubbles likely 
still occur in established two-group flows, intergroup wake-entrainment of group-I bubbles into 
group-II bubbles and shearing-off become the major mechanisms that dictate the changes in the 
interfacial area concentration. Therefore, the importance of the one-group source/sink terms in the 
two-group interfacial area transport equation should diminish as the transition progresses towards 
and established two-group flow.  
It is observed in the present experiments that larger, distorted group-I bubbles and 
clusters of group-I bubbles are present in the flow in the initial stages of transition. The larger 
group-I bubbles and bubble clusters will have a greater chance of entraining other bubbles in their 
wakes. Therefore, intergroup wake entrainment of group-I bubbles is considered as the 
mechanism to create group-II bubbles starting from a one-group condition. Although intergroup 
random collision of group-I bubbles forming a group-II bubble could be possible, the signature of 
transition from one-group to two-group flows is the increasing importance of wake-dominated 
bubble transport. Furthermore, since the random collision mechanism is driven by the turbulent 
fluctuations in the liquid, the production of group-II bubbles by intergroup random collision 
would increase with increasing liquid flow rate, which is unphysical. Therefore, intergroup 
random collision of group-I bubbles is not considered as the mechanism for group-II production. 
Once group-II bubbles are available in the flow, they entrain group-I bubbles in their wake 

210 
 
regions. The coalescences that result from the intergroup wake-entrainment of group-I and group-
II bubbles drive the transition towards an established two-group flow. Additionally, intragroup 
coalescence of group-II bubbles is the dominant sink to the number of group-II bubbles, and 
consequently group-II interfacial area concentration, as the transition progresses.  
As pointed out in previous studies (Kim, 1999; Sun, 2001; Kockx, et al., 2005), shearing-
off should not occur until group-II bubbles reach a sufficient size. In moderate size ducts, this is 
related to the group-II bubbles growing such that they fill the cross-section and begin to elongate. 
At the initiation of transition, group-II bubbles have not yet filled the pipe cross-section, so 
shearing-off should not be active. However, as the transition progresses, the shearing-off 
mechanism should become active and start to offset the intergroup wake entrainment of between 
group-I and group-II bubbles. Therefore, a shearing-off mechanism that activates as an 
established two-group flow structure develops is needed to complete the model for one-group to 
two-group transition flows. 
Table 5-1 summarizes the major bubble interaction mechanism source/sink terms that are 
required for a two-group interfacial area transport model for one-group to two-group transition 
flows. The major modeling effort that is required is the development of intergroup wake 
entrainment source/sink terms to initiate and drive the one-group to two-group transition. 
Additionally, a model for intragroup wake entrainment of group-II bubbles needs to be 
developed. As a first step to including shearing-off, the available shearing-off model developed 
by Fu (2001) will be modified so that it becomes active as an established two-group flow 
develops. Also, the available models for the intragroup interactions of group-I bubbles will be 
modified to reflect their diminishing importance as an established two-group flow develops. 

211

5.1.2 Constitutive Form of Source/Sink Terms 
In previous developments of two-group interfacial area transport models (Fu, 2001; Sun, 
2001), an integral approach was used to derive the source/term models for the interfacial area 
concentration and void fraction. This approach requires one to know the bubble volume 
distribution function (𝑓(𝑉)). Since 𝑓(𝑉) is not known, one requires a model for 𝑓(𝑉) in order to 
develop interfacial area concentration and void fraction source/sink terms using the integral 
approach.  In previous developments of the two-group IATE (Fu, 2001; Sun 2001), uniform 
bubble volume distributions were assumed for the group-I and group-II bubbles. However, this 
assumption dictates the criterion at which the intergroup interactions responsible for creating 
group-II from group-I become active; this criterion was shown to be unphysical in Chapter 4. 
Even though the bubble volume distribution is incorporated through the integral approach, 
numerous approximations are required to arrive at useful forms of the source/sink terms, and one 
inevitably relies on experimental data to adjust the coefficients of proportionality that arise in the 

212 
 
source/sink term models. In view of this, a more tractable approach to developing the interfacial 
area concentration and void fraction source/sink term models is used in this dissertation. 
Similar to the development of the one-group bubble interaction source/sink terms (Wu et 
al., 1998; Kim, et al., 1999), it is assumed that the interfacial area concentration source/sink terms 
can be modeled as the product of a characteristic surface area change and an interaction rate per 
unit mixture volume as: 
𝜙𝑏,𝑐
(𝑎) = (Δ𝐴𝑖)𝑏,𝑐
(𝑎)𝑅𝑏
(𝑎) 
[5-4] 
Likewise, the void fraction source/sink terms for the intergroup bubble interactions are modeled 
as the product of a characteristic volume change and an interaction rate per unit mixture volume 
as: 
𝜂𝑏,𝑐
(𝑎) = (Δ𝑉)𝑏,𝑐
(𝑎)𝑅𝑏
(𝑎) 
[5-5] 
where:   
 
(a): relationship between interacting and resultant particles (e.g. (a) = (11,2)) 
b: interaction mechanism (e.g. b = WE) 
c: affected group: (e.g. c=1) 
(Δ𝐴𝑖)𝑏,𝑐
(𝑎): characteristic surface area change for interaction mechanism (b, (a)) for group c 
(Δ𝑉)𝑏,𝑐
(𝑎): characteristic volume change for interaction mechanism (b, (a)) for group c 
𝑅𝑏
(𝑎): interaction rate per unit mixture volume for interaction mechanism (b,(a)) 
As discussed in Chapter 2, bubble volume is expected to be conserved during bubble interactions. 
Thus, the characteristic volume changes and void fraction source/sink terms for intragroup 

213 
 
interactions are zero. For intergroup interactions, the characteristic volume changes and void 
fraction source/sink terms are constrained as: 
(Δ𝑉)𝑏,1

(𝑎)   ⟺ 𝜂𝑏,1

(𝑎)

Development of the interfacial area concentration and void fraction source/sink terms for: 
1) intergroup wake-entrainment of group-I bubbles to create group-II bubbles, 2) intergroup 
wake-entrainment of group-I bubbles into group-II bubbles, and 3) intragroup wake-entrainment 
of group-II bubbles are the major modeling efforts that are required to establish a two-group 
interfacial area transport model for one-group to two-group transition flows. The constitutive

5.1.3 Approximate Bubble Shapes 
 
As in the previous interfacial area transport studies (Wu et al., 1998; Kim, 1999; Sun, 
2001; Fu, 2001), the current work assumes that group-I bubbles are spheres. Therefore, the group-
I bubble shape factor (𝜑1) is assumed to be equal to one. Group-II bubbles are assumed to be 

214 
 
spherical-cap shaped with a wake angle of 50°. Recalling Equation [2-147], the shape factor for a 
spherical-cap bubble with a wake angle of 50° is given approximately as: 
𝜑2 = 𝜑𝑐𝑎𝑝≈1.7    ,   𝑓𝑜𝑟 𝜃= 50° 
[5-7] 
 
At the initiation of transition in a moderate diameter pipe, spherical-cap shaped group-II 
bubbles are a reasonable approximation. As the transition progresses, however, group-II bubbles 
grow in size and begin to fill the pipe cross-section and elongate. As the group-II bubbles grow, 
their shape will be increasingly influenced by the presence of the wall. Since the major objective 
of this work is to develop a two-group interfacial area transport model that has the capability of 
dynamically transitioning form a purely one-group flow into a two-group flow, the effect of 
changing group-II bubble shape is presently neglected. Assessing the effect of different group-II 
bubble shapes and developing a framework, if necessary, to evolve from one group-II bubble 
shape to another should be investigated in future studies.   
5.2 Source/Sink Term Model Development  
Wake-dominant bubble transport is a signature of the transition from one-group to two-
group flows. As such, models for intergroup wake entrainment to initiate and drive the one-group 
to two-group transition are required. In Section 5.2.1, a general wake-entrainment interaction rate 
model is developed to serve as the basis for developing the source/sink terms for: 1) intergroup 
wake-entrainment of group-I bubbles to produce a group-II bubble, 2) intergroup wake-
entrainment of group-I bubbles into group-II bubbles, and 3) intragroup wake entrainment of 
group-II bubbles. The source/sink terms for each of these mechanisms are developed in Sections 
5.2.2 through 5.2.4, respectively. As a first step, the available shearing-off model developed by 

215 
 
Fu (2001) is modified to activate as the transition progresses; this is discussed in Section 5.2.5. 
Lastly, the available intragroup interaction models for group-I bubbles are modified in Section 
5.2.6 to reflect their diminishing importance as an established two-group flow develops.   
5.2.1 General Wake Entrainment Interaction Rate

entrainment of a general following bubble, denoted by “x,” into a leading bubble, denoted by “y,” 
is developed in this Section.  Figure 5-2 schematically shows this interaction.

This generalized form of the wake-entrainment coalescence rate will be used in Sections 
5.2.2 through 5.2.4 to establish the required wake-entrainment source/sink terms for a two-group 
interfacial area transport model capable of achieving dynamic transition from one-group to two-
group flows. In addition to establishing models for the characteristic surface area and volume 
changes and the coalescence rate, supporting models for parameters such as the bubble number 
densities, drag coefficients, and relative velocities have to be determined to complete the 
development of the void fraction and interfacial area concentration source/sink terms.    
5.2.2 Intergroup Wake Entrainment (11,2) 
5.2.2.1 Approach  
One-group flows are predominately occupied by smaller group-I bubbles with the group-I 
Sauter-mean diameter as their characteristic length scale. Even though there is a chance that 
larger group-I bubbles could coalesce with each other to form a group-II bubble in a single 
interaction, it is more likely that coalescences will occur between the few larger group-I bubbles 
and the more numerous average group-I bubbles. Therefore, instead of viewing the wake 
entrainment (11,2) mechanism as a single coalescence interaction between two group-I bubbles to 
produce a group-II bubble, consider the generation of a group-II bubble as the result of an 
evolutionary process that consists of a series of successive coalescence interactions between a 
large group-I bubble and average group-I bubbles. A schematic of such a process is depicted in 

220 
 
Figure 5-3. In the first stage of the wake entrainment (11,2) process, a large group-I bubble with 
volume 𝑉𝑦 is the leading bubble in a wake-entrainment interaction with a following average 
group-I bubble. Define 𝑉𝑦 as a fraction 𝛽 of the bubble size at the group boundary such that: 
𝑉𝑦= 𝛽𝑉𝑐 
[5-19] 
The resultant bubble from the first stage has volume 𝑉𝑦+ 𝑉̅1
(1), where 𝑉̅1
(1) is the volume of the 
average group-I bubble during the first stage. In subsequent stages, the resultant bubble from the 
previous stage is the leading bubble. One can identify this bubble as the large bubble from the 
first stage having undergone a series of coalesces with following average group-I bubbles.
 
Although the size of the following average group-I bubbles may increase in successive 
stages, this change is small in comparison to the bubble size at the group boundary. Therefore, 
assume that the following bubble in each stage is approximately the same size such that:   
𝑉̅1
(1) ≈𝑉̅1
(2) ≈𝑉̅1
(3) ≈⋯≈𝑉̅1
(𝑁) ≈𝑉̅1 
[5-20] 
Furthermore, assume that the bubble produced at the final stage of the wake entrainment (11,2) 
process has a volume approximately equal to the critical bubble volume. In this way, this 
mechanism is limited to producing group-II bubbles that are just at the group boundary. Although 
it is possible for various combinations of group-I bubbles to coalesce and produce a spectrum of 
group-II bubbles with volumes ranging from 𝑉𝑐 to twice the maximum group-I bubble volume in 
the flow, as previously discussed the population of larger group-I bubbles is expected to be 
minimal compared to the population of average group-I bubbles.  Moreover, the microscopic 
details of the group-II bubble size distribution that is produced in transition is not of interest in a 
macroscopic two-phase flow model. Therefore, it is sufficient to consider that this mechanism 

221 
 
just produces group-II bubbles at the boundary. With this assumption and defining the larger 
group-I bubble from the first stage with Equation [5-19], one can express the number of stages in 
the process (𝑁) in terms of the average group-I bubble volume, the critical volume, and 𝛽 as: 
𝑁≈(1 −𝛽) 𝑉𝑐
𝑉̅1
 
[5-21] 
 
Figure 5-3: Schematic representation of the wake entrainment (11,2) mechanism as an 
evolutionary process.  
 

222 
 
The size of the leading bubble in the first stage of the wake entrainment (11,2) process is 
determined by the value of 𝛽. Because this leading bubble is at least the same size as the average 
group-I bubble and the mechanism is assumed to only produce bubbles at the group boundary, 𝛽 
is bounded by: 
𝑉̅1
𝑉𝑐
≤𝛽≤1 −𝑉̅1
𝑉𝑐
 
[5-22] 
Assume that 𝛽 is given by the average of these two bounds, which yields: 
𝛽= 0.5 
[5-23] 
𝛽= 0.5 has physical significance in view of progressing from a pure one-group flow into 
transition. When the largest group-I bubbles in the flow are less than half of the critical bubble 
volume at the group boundary, there is no chance of producing group-II through binary 
coalescence interactions. As soon as group-I bubbles are available that have volumes greater than 
half the critical volume, there is a finite chance that group-II can be produced. Therefore, it is 
natural to assume that the wake entrainment (11,2) process can be considered to become 
important when large group-I bubbles with volumes around 0.5𝑉𝐶 become available in the flow 
since this corresponds to the first chance of producing group-II.  
It is interesting to note that viewing the wake entrainment (11,2) mechanism as an 
evolutionary process reflects the structure of group-I bubble clusters that are observed in the 
experiments at the initiation of transition. Figure 5-4 shows an example of clusters of group-I 
bubbles rising through the center of the pipe. As these clusters of group-I bubbles travel up the 
pipe, they move in a similar fashion to small cap bubbles. Thus, even though they have not 
formed true group-II bubbles, they share some group-II transport characteristics. This supports 
the concept of group-II production as an evolutionary rather than instantaneous process.  

223 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 5-4: Bubble clusters rising near the center of the pipe. Still image from a high-speed video 
captured at 2000fps during Run 2 (〈𝑗𝑓〉 = 2.00 m/s and 〈𝑗𝑔〉18𝐷 = 0.25 m/s) at P3 (L=63D).

5.2.2.2 Characteristic Surface Area and Volume Changes 
In view of the wake entrainment (11,2) process outlined in Section 5.2.2.1, the

(11,2) source/sink terms are obtained by computing the arithmetic mean of 
the surface area and volume changes to each group from each stage of the process. In this way, 
the resulting average surface area and volume changes over the entire wake entrainment (11,2) 
process indicate what fraction of the actual transfer to group-II that occurs in the final stage can 
be attributed to the separate stages of the process. In doing so, the average surface area and 
volume changes can be associated with the interaction rate of the first stage of the process to 
formulate the 𝜙𝑊𝐸,1
(11,2), 𝜙𝑊𝐸,2
(11,2), and 𝜂𝑊𝐸,2
(11,2) source/sink terms.

to each stage. Assuming that a group-II bubble of volume 𝑉𝑐 is produced in the last stage, the

5.2.2.3 Interaction Rate 
Assume that the interaction rate for the (11,2) mechanism is proportional to the 
interaction rate for the first stage of the series of coalescences. With the characteristic surface area 
and volume changes computed as averages over the process, we have approximately accounted 
for the fact that we are not necessarily considering the transfer to group-II through a single 
interaction but through an evolutionary process. Considering the general wake entrainment 
interaction rate developed in Section 5.2.1, the larger group-I bubble in the wake entrainment 
(11,2) process described in Section 5.2.2.1 is the leading “y” bubble, and the average group-I 
bubble is the following bubble “x”. Therefore, 𝑅𝑊𝐸

the drag coefficient, projected area, and relative velocity of the large group-I bubble.

5.2.2.4 Transition Function: 𝑻(𝑫𝑺𝒎𝟏
∗

As discussed in Section 5.2.2.3 a model is required for the probability that a larger group-
I bubble is available in the flow for the wake entrainment (11,2) process to occur. A detailed 
description of this probability requires knowledge of the bubble size distribution, which is not 
readily attainable. Therefore, a simple approach is developed in the present study to increase the 
importance of the wake-entrainment (11,2) mechanism as the average group-I bubble size 
increases. First, consider the average group-I bubble volume non-dimensionalized by the critical 
bubble volume at the group boundary, i.e. 𝑉̅1
∗= 𝑉̅1/𝑉𝑐. When 𝑉̅1
∗ increases beyond a transition 
average bubble size (𝑉̅1,𝑡𝑟
∗), large group-I bubbles with non-dimensional volume 𝛽 become 
available to initiate the wake-entrainment (11,2) process. As 𝑉̅1
∗ continues to increase towards 𝛽, 
it becomes certain that wake-entrainment (11,2) will occur. Therefore, we seek a function 𝑇(𝑉̅1
∗) 
for the wake-entrainment (11,2) interaction rate with the following behavior:

5.2.3 Intergroup Wake Entrainment (12,2) 
In view of the general wake entrainment interaction rate developed in Section 5.2.1, 
consider a group-II bubble is the leading “y” bubble and a group-I bubble as the following “x” 
bubble. The number density of group-I bubbles represents the number of group-I bubbles per unit 
mixture volume. As group-II bubbles become present in the flow, they exclude volume that the 
group-I bubbles can occupy. This results in an effective increase in the following group-I bubble 
number density in the wake regions of group-II bubbles. Accounting for this effect, the number 
density of the following group-I bubbles can be estimated as: 
𝑛𝑥=
𝑛1
1 −𝛼2
 
[5-65]

with a wake angle of 50°, i.e. (𝐷𝑆𝑚/𝐷𝑒)2 ≈1/1.7.

5.2.4 Intragroup Wake Entrainment (22,2) 
As observed in the experiments, the group-II interfacial area concentration demonstrates 
a decreasing trend along the test section as the one-group to two-group transition progresses 

239 
 
towards more established group-II flows. This also corresponds to a decrease in the group-II 
bubble number frequency, indicating intragroup coalescence of group-II bubbles. Owing to the 
moderate size of the test section, the group-II bubble motion is restricted in the lateral direction, 
such that group-II bubbles primarily interact with each other and group-I bubbles in their wake 
regions. Figure 5-6 shows and example of a following cap bubble approaching a leading cap 
bubble. Using the general wake entrainment interaction rate developed in Section 5.2.1, the 
interaction rate for intragroup wake entrainment between two group-II bubbles to form a group-II 
bubble is given by:

5.2.5 Intergroup Shearing-Off (2,12) 
As observed in the experiments, the beginning stages of one-group to two-group 
transition are characterized by decreasing interfacial area concentration and noticeable transfer of 
void fraction from group-I to group-II. However, as the transition progresses and an established 
two-group flow structure develops, the interfacial area concentration begins to increase again. As 
an initial attempt to capture this behavior, the available shearing-off model developed by Fu 
(2001) is used. As pointed out in Chapter 4, the liquid film velocity reduction factor (𝜅𝑓𝑟) in Fu’s 
(2001) model has inconsistent units. This term was developed to account for the effect of wall 
friction on the falling film around an elongated slug bubble. Since the present transition 
conditions still represent cap-bubbly flows transitioning towards an established two-group 
structure, it is assumed that this effect can be neglected. In fact, evaluating 𝜅𝑓𝑟 as given by Fu 
(2001) for the present experimental conditions yields a value very close to one. Therefore, 𝜅𝑓𝑟 is 
set to one for use in the present model.  The formulation of the relative interfacial velocity at the 
bases of group-II bubbles in both transition and established group-II flow conditions should be 
investigated further in future work to establish a robust shearing-off model. 
As a first step towards developing a shearing-off model that activates once a sufficient 
group-II bubble size is achieved, a treatment similar to the one-group turbulence impact breakup 
mechanism is employed. A modification factor as a function of the group-II bubble Weber 
number (𝑊𝑒2) is given as:  
𝑔(𝑊𝑒2) =
{  
  √1 −𝑊𝑒𝑐𝑟2
𝑊𝑒2
   ,   𝑓𝑜𝑟   𝑊𝑒2 > 𝑊𝑒𝑐𝑟2 
0       ,    𝑒𝑙𝑠𝑒
 
[5-76] 
The Weber number is chosen as a governing parameter since it represents the ratio of the inertia 
force to the surface tension force. The group-II Sauter-mean diameter is the natural group-II 

242 
 
bubble length scale in the context of a two-phase flow simulation with the interfacial area 
transport equation since it can be readily calculated from the group-II void fraction and interfacial 
area concentration. Since the shearing-off mechanism is related to the relative motion of the 
group-II bubbles, the group-II relative velocity is employed as the velocity scale for one-group to 
two-group transition flows. Thus, the group-II bubble Weber number is defined as:  
𝑊𝑒2 = 𝜌𝑓𝑢𝑟2
2 𝐷𝑆𝑚2
𝜎
 
[5-77] 
𝑊𝑒𝑐𝑟2 is the critical group-II bubble Weber number for shearing-off to occur, which should be 
determined through experiment. The modification factor given by Equation [5-76] is multiplied to 
the available shearing-off interfacial area concentration and void fraction source/sink terms, 
𝜙𝑆𝑂,1
(2,12), 𝜙𝑆𝑂,2
(2,12), and 𝜂𝑆𝑂,2
(2,12), (Fu, 2001) that were presented in Chapter 4. When the group-II 
bubble Weber number is less than the critical group-II Weber number, the modification factor 
zeros the shearing-off mechanism. As the group-II bubble Weber number increases beyond the 
critical Weber number, the importance of the shearing-off mechanism increases.  
 
To gain a sense of the order of magnitude of the group-II Weber number, Equation [5-77] 
is evaluated assuming that the void fraction is equal to zero in the relative velocity expression. 
The Weber number is then evaluated with 𝐷𝑆𝑚2 = 𝐷𝑐 and 𝐷𝑆𝑚2 = 𝐷ℎ. Doing so yields Weber 
numbers of ~7 and ~170, respectively. Thus, we can expect the critical Weber number to be 
within these bounds.  
5.2.6 Modification of One-Group Models for Two-Group Flows 
At the beginning of transition from a one-group to two-group flow, the flow 
predominately consists of group-I bubbles. Therefore, the one-group bubble interaction 

243 
 
mechanisms previously developed (Wu et al., 1998, Kim, 1999) for turbulence impact breakup, 
random collision coalescence, and wake entrainment are still valid, but the models should be 
expressed in terms of group-I parameters. However, as the transition progresses and the flow 
becomes more populated with group-II bubbles, the flow structure progressively deviates from 
the pure one-group flow structure that was assumed in developing the one-group models. 
Moreover, as an established group-II flow structure develops, wake entrainment of group-I 
bubbles into group-II bubbles becomes the major sink to the group-I interfacial area concentration 
and shearing-off becomes the dominant source. Therefore, it is postulated that although 
intragroup random collision, wake entrainment, and turbulence impact of group-I bubbles do 
likely occur in established two-group flows, their contribution to the change in interfacial area 
concentration is not as significant compared to shearing-off and intergroup wake entrainment 
(12,2). Therefore, the importance of the one-group bubble interaction mechanisms should 
diminish as an established two-group flow develops. 
To achieve this, the one-group bubble interaction source/sink terms are written as:

interaction rate so that this mechanism diminishes in importance as well with the other one-group 
interactions. Thus, as an established two-group flow structure develops, the importance of the 
one-group bubble interaction mechanisms diminishes in the two-group interfacial area transport 
equations. If the flow were to move away from an established two-group flow structure back 
towards a pure one-group flow, the importance of the one-group mechanisms would naturally 
increase. 
5.3 Chapter Summary 
In this Chapter the necessary bubble interaction source/sink term models to achieve 
dynamic transition from one-group to two-group flows with the two-group interfacial area 
transport equation were developed. The framework of one-group to two-group transition flows 
was established. The increasing importance of wake-dominant bubble transport was identified as 
the characteristic of the initiation of transition. As the transition progresses towards a more 
established two-group flow structure the interfacial area concentration increases due to increasing 

245 
 
activity of the shearing-off mechanism. In an established two-group flow, shearing-off and wake 
entrainment become the major source and sink, respectively, to the interfacial area concentration. 
With this framework in mind, the following efforts were made to establish the required models 
for a two-group interfacial area transport model applicable to one-group to two-group transition 
flows: 
 
 
A general wake entrainment interaction rate model was established to serve as the basis 
for developing the required source/sink terms for one-group to two-group transition 
flows. 
 
 
Intergroup wake-entrainment of group-I bubbles to form group-II bubbles was considered 
as an evolutionary process to develop the necessary interfacial area concentration and 
void fraction source/sink terms to achieve dynamic transition from one-group to two-
group flows. 
 
 
Interfacial area concentration and void fraction source/sink terms for intergroup wake 
entrainment of group-I bubbles into group-II bubbles were developed. This is a major 
mechanism that drives the one-group to two-group transition and becomes the major sink 
to the interfacial area concentration in established two-group flows. 
 
 
A model for the interfacial area concentration sink term due to intragroup coalescence of 
group-II bubbles was developed. 
 
 
The available shearing-off model (Fu, 2001) was modified to include a function of the 
group-II bubble Weber number so that it would become active as an established two-
group flow structure develops. This treatment prevents non-physical shearing-off at the 
initiation of transition. 

246 
 
 
The existing intragroup turbulence impact, random collision, and wake entrainment 
models for group-I bubbles were revised to include a function of the group-II bubble 
Weber number to diminish their importance as an established two-group flow develops. 
This treatment was also included in the newly developed wake-entrainment (11,2) 
source/sink terms. 

247 
 
  
Chapter 6
 
Evaluation of Interfacial Area Transport Model 
for One-Group to Two-Group Transition Flows 
The interfacial area transport source/sink term models developed in Chapter 5 are 
evaluated in this Chapter against the one-group to two-group transition test conditions that were 
established in Chapter 3. Two-phase flows are commonly analyzed in a one-dimensional (i.e. 
cross-sectional averaged) sense such as in nuclear reactor thermal-hydraulic system analysis 
codes like TRACE and RELAP (TRACE v5.0 Theory Manual, 2007; RELAP5-3D Code Manual, 
2005). Therefore, in view of the practical significance of a one-dimensional model, the two-group 
interfacial area transport model is evaluated in one-dimensional form, assuming that the flow is in 
a steady-state condition. Furthermore, since the experiments were performed in adiabatic air-
water two-phase flows, the terms associated with phase change are neglected. Section 6.1 outlines 
the evaluation approach. This includes discussion of the governing equations, assumptions, and 
the numerical solution procedure in a MATLAB program that was developed to solve the steady-
state, one-dimensional, two-group continuity and interfacial area transport equations for adiabatic 
two-phase flows. Section 6.2 discusses the results of the evaluation against experimental data. 
6.1 Model Evaluation Approach 
6.1.1 Governing Equations 
 
The steady-state, one-dimensional group continuity equations for adiabatic two-phase 
flows are given as: 
 

248 
 
∂〈𝛼1〉𝜌𝑔〈〈𝑣𝑔1〉〉
∂z
= 〈𝑚1〉 
[6-1] 
∂〈𝛼1〉𝜌𝑔〈〈𝑣𝑔1〉〉
∂z
= 〈𝑚2〉 
[6-2] 
Note that it is assumed that the density of the gas phase is uniform in the duct cross-section (i.e. 
𝜌𝑔= 〈𝜌𝑔〉), which implies that the pressure is also assumed to be uniform over the cross-section.  
Note that the following relationships are required to satisfy total continuity of the gas phase: 
〈𝑚1〉+ 〈𝑚2〉= 0 
[6-3] 
〈𝛼〉= 〈𝛼1〉+ 〈𝛼2〉 
[6-4] 
〈〈𝑣𝑔〉〉=
〈𝛼1〉〈〈𝑣𝑔1〉〉+ 〈𝛼2〉〈〈𝑣𝑔2〉〉
〈𝛼〉
 
[6-5] 
 
Once the group-I bubble length scale reaches a certain size, which is less than the bubble 
length scale at the group boundary, there will be a possibility that the coalescence of group-I 
bubbles will form group-II bubbles. Once group-II bubbles are formed, intergroup wake-
entrainment between group-I and group-II bubbles drives the transition to established two-group 
flow. Therefore, it is unlikely that a significant amount of group-I bubbles will reach a size close 
to that at the group boundary. In view of this, it is assumed that the effects of intergroup 
expansion/contraction are negligible in the two-group interfacial area transport equations for the 
adiabatic flows under consideration. As such, the factor 𝜒 in the two-group interfacial area 
transport equations and intergroup mass transfer rates will be assumed to be zero in the present 

249 
 
development. However, this assumption may need to be revisited depending on the flow under 
consideration. For example, in heated flows the effects of evaporation/condensation may lead to 
significant intergroup expansion/contraction across the group boundary. Likewise, a rapid flow 
transient that causes a sudden increase/decrease in the pressure could also lead to a significant 
effect from intergroup expansion/contraction.  In such cases, modeling of the particle volume 
distribution (i.e. 𝜒𝑉𝑐/𝑉̅ and 𝜒𝐴𝑖𝑐/𝐴̅𝑖1) should be considered to properly include the effects of 
intergroup expansion/contraction. However, these cases are beyond the scope of the present work. 
 
In view of neglecting the effects of intergroup expansion/contraction (i.e. 𝜒= 0), the 
intergroup mass transfer rate in the continuity equations is due only to intergroup bubble 
interactions and is given as: 
〈𝑚2〉= 𝜌𝑔∑〈𝜂𝑗,2
𝑖𝑛𝑡𝑒𝑟〉
𝑗
 
[6-6] 
 
Furthermore, neglecting the intergroup expansion/contraction source/sink terms (i.e. 
𝜒= 0), the steady-state, one-dimensional two-group interfacial area transport equations for 
adiabatic two-phase flow are given as: 
𝜕〈𝑎𝑖1〉〈〈𝑣𝑖1〉〉𝑎𝑖

[6-8] 
It is noted that the covariance terms that arise from area-averaging the intragroup 
expansion/contraction source/sink terms in the two-group interfacial area transport equations have 
been neglected. Furthermore, the complicated covariance terms that would result from area-

250 
 
averaging the source/sink terms will be neglected. As such, the source/sink terms will be 
evaluated in terms of area-averaged two-phase flow parameters. 
 
In order to solve the interfacial area transport equations, constitutive relations are 
required for the velocities 〈〈𝑣𝑖1〉〉𝑎𝑖 and 〈〈𝑣𝑖2〉〉𝑎𝑖. In view of obtaining a useable model, it is 
assumed that these velocities can be approximated by the void-weighted bubble velocities for 
each group such that: 
〈〈𝑣𝑖1〉〉𝑎𝑖≈〈〈𝑣𝑔1〉〉 
[6-9] 
〈〈𝑣𝑖2〉〉𝑎𝑖≈〈〈𝑣𝑔2〉〉 
[6-10] 
 
To solve the group continuity and interfacial area transport equations, the pressure and 
group velocities are required. In the context of the two-fluid model, these would be obtained 
through a coupled solution of the group continuity and interfacial area transport equations with 
the group momentum and energy equations. However, this introduces many complexities due to 
the interdependence of the constitutive models in the field equations, such as the interfacial drag 
for each group. Therefore, the current approach is to decouple the continuity and interfacial area 
transport equations from the momentum and energy equations to isolate them from the 
uncertainties in other constitutive models. Thus, the experimental measurements of pressure, 
〈〈𝑣𝑔1〉〉, and 〈〈𝑣𝑔2〉〉 are linearly interpolated between the measurement locations to obtain their 
values throughout the computational domain to be used in the solution of the group continuity 
and interfacial area transport equations. This approach is consistent with previous works on the 
two-group interfacial area transport equation (Fu, 2001; Sun 2001). Future work should 
investigate the performance of the newly developed interfacial area transport model in a full two-
fluid model solution. 
 

251

6.1.4 Computational Scheme 
A simple finite difference scheme is used to solve the group continuity equations for the 
group void fractions and the group interfacial area transport equations for the group interfacial 
area concentrations, respectively. The group continuity equations are discretized to solve for the 
group void fractions as: 
〈𝛼1〉𝑖+1 =
(〈𝛼1〉𝜌𝑔〈〈𝑣𝑔1〉〉)𝑖+ 〈𝑚1〉𝑖Δ𝑧
(𝜌𝑔〈〈𝑣𝑔1〉〉)𝑖+1
  
[6-51] 
〈𝛼2〉𝑖+1 =
(〈𝛼2〉𝜌𝑔〈〈𝑣𝑔2〉〉)𝑖+ 〈𝑚2〉𝑖Δ𝑧
(𝜌𝑔〈〈𝑣𝑔2〉〉)𝑖+1
  
[6-52] 
Likewise, the group interfacial area transport equations are discretized to solve for the group 
interfacial area concentrations as: 
〈𝑎𝑖1〉𝑖+1 =

𝑗

Assuming that the flow is isothermal, the gas density along the test section is specified as 
a function of the absolute pressure ((𝑝)𝑖) as: 
(𝜌𝑔)𝑖= 𝜌𝑔,𝑎𝑡𝑚

𝑝𝑎𝑡𝑚
 
[6-57] 
where 𝜌𝑔,𝑎𝑡𝑚 as the density of the gas-phase at atmospheric pressure. The liquid density is 
assumed to remain constant along the flow. Thus, the void-weighted liquid velocity is calculated 
as a function of axial position from the liquid superficial velocity, which is specified as a constant 
inlet condition, and the void fraction along the test section as:  
〈〈𝑣𝑓〉〉𝑖=

[6-58] 
The built-in function interp1 in MATLAB is used to linearly interpolate the measured 
values pressure, 〈〈𝑣𝑔1〉〉, and 〈〈𝑣𝑔2〉〉 along the computational domain of the test section. If the 
measured group-II bubble velocity is zero, the group-II velocity in the simulation is set equal to 
the group-I velocity to prevent dividing by zero in the solution of the equations. This is a 

259 
 
reasonable treatment if one assumes that group-II bubbles at the initiation of transition behave 
similar to the larger distorted group-I bubbles. A schematic of the computational scheme used to 
solve the two-group continuity and interfacial area transport equations in MATLAB is shown in 
Figure 6-1. The commented source code of the MATLAB implementation is provided in 
Appendix E.

area transport equations.

6.1.5 Model Constants and Coefficients 
A systematic procedure is followed to determine the empirical coefficients in the newly 
developed two-group interfacial area transport model. First, the existing model coefficients for 
the one-group bubble interactions mechanisms for vertical-upward flows in round pipes are used 
(Ishii et al., 2002). These coefficients have been determined based on a substantial set of 
experimental data, so there is high confidence in their values. With the one-group coefficients 
specified in the model, 𝐶𝑊𝐸
(11,2) is determined first using Runs 1, 2, 3, and 7 by focusing on the 
group void fraction predictions. While determining 𝐶𝑊𝐸
(11,2), 𝐶𝑊𝐸
(12,2), 𝐶𝑊𝐸
(2), and 𝐶𝑆𝑂  are set to zero 
in order to isolate the wake-entrainment (11,2) source/sink terms. Also, 𝐷𝑆𝑚1,𝑡𝑟
∗
 is fixed at 0.4, 
which is based on the maximum 𝐷𝑆𝑚1 observed in the transition experiments. Additionally, the 
relaxation parameter 𝑎 in the transition function is fixed at 0.01, and 𝛽 is fixed at 0.5. 
Runs 2, 3, and 7 were selected to determine 𝐶𝑊𝐸
(11,2) because they have the lowest 
contributions of group-II void fraction and demonstrate growth along the test section. It is noted 
that although the group-II void fraction in Run 2 can be considered insignificant (i.e. 
〈𝛼2〉<0.005), it does demonstrate growth along the test section. Moreover, it is found that the 
small amount of group-II void fraction in this condition is repeatable in the experiments. 
Additionally, the increasing trend in the group-II void in this condition cannot be predicted 
without a void source term; the expansion due to the pressure drop alone is not enough to match 
the increase that is observed. Therefore, this condition is considered useful in determining 𝐶𝑊𝐸
(11,2).
 
The current model for 𝑅𝑊𝐸
(11,2) will always produce some amount of group-II due to the 
nature of the transition function. This is acceptable provided that the group-II that is produced 
remains a minimal contribution in one-group flows. Therefore, Run 1, a one-group condition near 

261 
 
the initiation of transition, is used to ensure that the group-II void fraction that is produced in that 
condition while adjusting 𝐶𝑊𝐸
(11,2) does not exceed 5% of the total void fraction.  
After setting 𝐶𝑊𝐸
(11,2),   𝐶𝑊𝐸
(12,2) is set using Runs 7 and 8. Run 8 demonstrates the most 
significant transfer from group-I to group-II, which is a clear signature of intergroup wake-
entrainment of group-I bubbles into group-II bubbles. Run 7 also demonstrates significant growth 
that was not able to be captured with wake-entrainment (11,2) alone while maintaining acceptable 
predictions in the other low group-II contribution conditions.  
After setting the intergroup wake-entrainment coefficients, a first estimate of 𝐶𝑆𝑂 was 
obtained using the highest gas flow rate conditions, Runs 6 and 9, which demonstrate clear 
growth in the interfacial area concentration as well as transfer of void from group-II to group-I. In 
determining this first estimate of 𝐶𝑆𝑂, 𝑊𝑒𝑐𝑟2 was set to zero so that the shearing-off mechanism 
would not be limited in these highest gas flow rate conditions. With this first estimate of 𝐶𝑆𝑂, the 
interfacial area concentration predictions in Runs 4, 5, and 8 were used to determine 𝑊𝑒𝑐𝑟2. 
Some iteration was required on the value of 𝐶𝑆𝑂 to obtain the best fit with the data after 
introducing 𝑊𝑒𝑐𝑟2. The coefficients 𝛾𝑆𝑂 and 𝛽𝑆𝑂 in the shearing-off model are unchanged from 
Fu’s (2001) study. Lastly, 𝐶𝑊𝐸
(2) was determined using Runs 6 and 9 again since they demonstrate 
clear decreasing trends in the group-II interfacial area concentration along the test section.  
Table 6-1 summarizes the model constants and coefficients in the present two-group 
interfacial area transport model. Here, model constants refer to numerical parameters with values 
that are fixed based on modeling arguments. Model coefficients are adjustable parameters that are 
systematically determined through experiment. 

262

6.2 Model Evaluation Results and Discussion 
This Section discusses results from the evaluation of the newly developed two-group 
interfacial area transport equation against the one-group to two-group transition test conditions. 
The complete set of results including the individual source/sink term contributions to the group-I 
and group-II void fractions and interfacial area concentrations is included in Appendix F. 
First, consider the predictions of void fraction and interfacial area concentration for Run 
1, the one-group flow condition, shown in Figure 6-2. The void fraction and interfacial area 
concentration are both predicted well within ±10%. This confirms that the present two-group 
model will function well in one-group flow conditions. It is noted that a small amount of group-II 
void fraction and interfacial area concentration are generated by the intergroup wake-entrainment 
(11,2) mechanism. However, the error this introduces into the overall prediction is negligible.   

263 
 
 
 
(a) 
 
 
(b) 
Figure 6-2: Predictions of the total, group-I, and group-II (a) void fractions and (b) interfacial 
area concentrations for Run 1 (〈𝑗𝑓〉 = 2.00 m/s and 〈𝑗𝑔〉18𝐷 = 0.18 m/s). 

264 
 
 
The prediction results for Runs 7 and 8 clearly demonstrate the efficacy of the current 
model in predicting the void fraction and interfacial area concentration in one-group to two-group 
transition conditions. Figure 6-3 shows the predictions of void fraction and interfacial area 
concentration for Run 7. Both the group-I and group-II void fractions are predicted very well 
along the length of the test section. The model is capable of predicting the increase in group-II 
void fraction and the corresponding decrease in group-I void fraction, indicating that the 
intergroup coalescence source/sink terms are active in the solution. The total and group-I 
interfacial area concentrations are somewhat under predicted towards the end of the test section in 
Run 7. However, the predictions are still within ±10% of the experimental data. Most 
importantly, the model captures the overall decreasing trend in the interfacial area concentration 
in this transition condition. Although the contribution of the group-II interfacial area 
concentration to the total interfacial area concentration is negligible in this case, it is important to 
note that the model does capture the increase in the group-II interface area concentration along 
the test section. This indicates that the model is exchanging void and interfacial area 
concentration correctly between the groups.  
 
To obtain a better sense of how the model is functioning, Figure 6-4 shows the individual 
source/sink term contributions to the changes in the group-I and group-II interfacial area 
concentrations along the test section for Run 7. A detailed explanation of the legends in the 
source/sink term contribution plots is provided in Appendix F. In vertical-upward adiabatic flows, 
the expansion/contraction source/sink terms (EXP1 and EXP2) are always sources to the group 
interfacial area concentrations. For Run 7, EXP1 is the only source to the group-I interfacial area 
concentration as shown in Figure 6-4(a). Both intergroup wake-entrainment (11,2) and (12,2) are 
significant sinks to the group-I interfacial area concentration in this condition. As the flow 
develops and the group-II void fraction increases, the intergroup wake-entrainment (12,2) 
mechanism becomes more important. The random collision sink term RC1(11,1) is also very 

265 
 
active in this condition. This may be why the interfacial area concentration is somewhat under 
predicted in this condition. Notice that the modification to the shearing-off source term keeps the 
shearing-off mechanism inactive in this cap-bubbly flow condition. 
 
Considering the contributions to the change in the group-II interfacial area concentration 
in Figure 6-4(b), the intergroup wake-entrainment (11,2) and (12,2) mechanisms are the dominant 
sources of group-II interfacial area concentration. Interestingly, the intergroup wake-entrainment 
(11,2) is a more significant source to the group-II interfacial area concentration for this condition. 
Towards the end of the test section, it is observed that the intragroup wake entrainment sink to 
group-II is starting to become more active. 
 
Figure 6-5 shows the void fraction and interfacial area concentration predictions for Run 
8. Similar to Run 7, the model is capable of predicting the exchange between the group void 
fractions along the pipe. Observe that the interfacial area concentration prediction has a non-
linear trend. This is consistent with the experimental data. The change in the total and group-I 
interfacial area concentration measurements per unit length between L=18D and L=34.5D is 
greater than that between L=34.5D and L=63D. Considering the contributions from the individual 
source/sink terms shown in Figure 6-6, it is observed that the shearing-off mechanism becomes 
active along the pipe in this condition. Correspondingly, the importance of the one-group 
source/sink terms begins to diminish, which can be seen clearly for the random collision sink 
term in Figure 6-6(a). Wake entrainment (12,2) is the major sink to the group-I interfacial area 
concentration in this condition, which is consistent with the significant exchange in the group 
void fractions that is occurring. 
 
In Figure 6-6(b) the intergroup wake-entrainment (12,2) mechanism is now more 
dominant than the intergroup wake-entrainment (11,2) mechanism. This is likely due to the 
increased group-II bubble size in this higher void fraction condition. Also, observe that intragroup 
wake entrainment (22,2) is now a significant sink to the group-II interfacial area concentration, 
which is important since it corresponds to a reduction in group-II number.

interfacial area concentrations for Run 8 (〈𝑗𝑓〉 = 1.00 m/s and 〈𝑗𝑔〉18𝐷 = 0.29 m/s). 

270 
 
Predictions of the void fraction and interfacial area concentration for Run 9 are shown in 
Figure 6-7. Now the flow is clearly transitioning into an established two-group flow structure 
since the interfacial area concentration is increasing along the test section, which is attributed to 
the shearing-off mechanism. Considering the individual source/sink term contributions for Run 9 
in Figure 6-8, the shearing-off source term is active throughout the pipe in this condition. The 
one-group source/sink terms have diminished significantly, and shearing-off and wake-
entrainment (12,2) are the dominant mechanisms. Intragroup wake-entrainment also continues to 
be a significant sink to the group-II interfacial area concentration. 
Figure 6-9 shows the absolute relative errors between the predictions and the 
experimental data for each run at Port2 (L=34.5D) and Port3 (L=63D). Overall, the average 
absolute relative errors for the  total interfacial area concentration and void fraction predictions 
are ±4% and ±3%, respectively. The maximum absolute relative errors for the total interfacial 
area concentration and void fraction are ±8% and ±9%. For the group-I interfacial area 
concentration and void fraction predictions, the average absolute relative errors are ±4% and 
±6%, and the maximum absolute relative errors are ±10% and ±24%. The largest errors in the 
group-I void fraction predictions occur for Runs 5 and 6, which are developing into more 
established two-group flows. This may suggest that the shearing-off mechanism in these 
conditions is not producing enough group-I void fraction. The balance between the shearing-off 
mechanism and intergroup wake entrainment (12,2) as more established two-group flows develop 
should be investigated further in future work. In Run 2, the experimental group-II void fraction 
and interfacial area concentration values are small. Therefore, the relative errors for the 
predictions of these parameters for Run 2 are magnified. Similarly for Run 1, small amounts of 
group-II void fraction and interfacial area concentration are predicted for this one-group 
condition, yielding very large relative errors. However, these errors are misleading in view of the 
relative magnitude of group-II void and interfacial area concentration to the total void and 

271 
 
interfacial area concentration. Excluding Runs 1 and 2, the average absolute relative errors for the 
group-II void fraction and interfacial concentration predictions are ±9% and ±11%, respectively. 
Likewise, the maximum absolute relative errors are ±21% and ±29%. Again, considering that the 
largest errors for the group-II void fraction and interfacial area concentration occur for relatively 
small quantities, these results are actually very encouraging.

6.3 Chapter Summary 
 
In this Chapter the two-group interfacial area transport model for one-group to two-group 
transition flows that was developed in Chapter 5 was evaluated against the nine one-group to two-
group transition test conditions. A program was developed in MATLAB to solve the steady-state, 
one-dimensional, adiabatic two-group continuity and interfacial area transport equations for the 
group void fractions and interfacial area concentrations. The major findings of the evaluation can 
be summarized as: 
 
 
The model has the capability to produce group-II void fraction and interfacial area 
concentration from one-group flows.  
 
 
The model accurately captures the exchange between the group void fractions and 
interfacial area concentrations in one-group to two-group transition conditions. 
 
 
The shearing-off mechanism dynamically activates as established two-group flows 
develop.  
 
 
The importance of the one-group source/sink terms diminishes as established two-group 
flows develop.    
 
 
The group-I void fraction and interfacial area concentration are predicted with average 
absolute relative errors of ±6% and ±4%, respectively. 
 
 
The group-II void fraction and interfacial area concentration are predicted with average 
absolute relative errors of ±9% and ±11%, respectively. 
 
 
The total void fraction and interfacial area concentration are predicted with average 
absolute relative errors of ±3% and ±4%, respectively.  

276 
 
  
Chapter 7
 
Summary and Recommendations for Future Work 
7.1 Summary of Current Work 
 
After review of the available literature on the development of the interfacial area 
transport equation, it was identified that no systematic study has been performed on the transition 
from one-group to two-group flows. The ability to transition from one-group to two-group flows 
is required to fully realize the benefit of the two-group interfacial area transport equation as a 
dynamic closure model for the interfacial area concentration in the two-fluid model. Therefore, 
the primary objective and unique contribution of this work is to develop models that can achieve 
a dynamic transition from one-group to two-group flow conditions. With this major goal in mind, 
this dissertation sought to address this issue through theoretical and experimental investigations. 
In view of the previous Chapters, the major accomplishments of this dissertation are summarized 
as follows: 
 
Derived new expansion source terms, improving the basic formulation of the interfacial area 
transport equation  
 
To clearly establish the interfacial area transport equation framework applicable to 
bubbly, cap-bubbly, slug, and churn-turbulent flows, a detailed derivation of both the one-group 
and two-group interfacial area transport equations was established. The major contributions of 
this effort are: 

277 
 
 
A new approach that appealed directly to the necessary agreement between the void 
transport equation(s) and continuity equation(s) in the two-fluid model was used to 
develop constitutive models for the unknown terms in the void transport equation(s). In 
particular, models for the rate of change of particle volume (𝑉̇ ) were developed and used 
to complete the one-group and two-group interfacial area transport equations. 
 
 
It was found that the existing expansion/contraction source/sink terms in the two-group 
interfacial area transport equation incorrectly include the effects of intergroup transfer. 
This effectively leads to double counting the effects of intergroup particle interactions 
and expansion/contraction across the group boundary on the group interfacial area 
concentrations. Revised models for these terms were developed to correct this issue. This 
is a significant contribution to the field of interfacial area transport.  
 
 
A shape factor (𝛾2) was introduced to properly account for the effect of different group-II 
bubble shapes in the intragroup expansion/contraction source/sink term in the group-II 
interfacial area transport equation. 
 
 
Consistency requirements among the source/sink terms in the interfacial area transport 
equations were identified and discussed to provide guidelines for model development.  
  
 
Recommendations were made on the appropriate forms of the interfacial area transport 
equation(s) for consistent implementation with the two-fluid model. 
 

278 
 
Improved the local conductivity probe measurement technique through systematic investigation  
 
 
 
Reliable experimental data is essential to two-phase flow model development. Therefore, 
the effects of several practical aspects of a conductivity probe measurement system on the 
measured two-phase flow parameters were investigated to provide guidelines for conductivity 
probe data acquisition. These investigations are summarized as:  
 
 
Signal ghosting, an electrical interface inherent to multiplexing DAQs, causes artificially 
high bubble velocity measurements and low interfacial area concentration measurements 
that depend on sampling frequency and sensor impedance. A revised electrical circuit that 
incorporates a voltage follower to reduce the source impedances of the conductivity 
probe sensors was suggested to eliminate this artificial variability. The characteristics of 
signal ghosting and the performance of the revised circuit were investigated 
experimentally with a novel dual double-sensor conductivity probe.  
 
 
Sensitivity of the measured two-phase flow parameters to data acquisition sampling 
frequency was investigated in 13 vertical-upward air-water two-phase flow conditions 
spanning the bubbly and slug flow regimes. Measurements acquired with a sampling 
frequency of 250kHz were downsampled to subsequently lower sampling frequencies to 
isolate the effect of sampling frequency. The measured void fraction and bubble velocity 
were found to be relatively insensitive to sampling frequency. However, for a given 
liquid flow rate, it was observed that with increasing gas flow rate, higher sampling 
frequencies are required to obtain bubble number frequency and interfacial area 
concentration measurements that are within ±5% of the 250kHz measurements. This 
trend is due to both the increase in gas velocity and the transition to the slug flow regime 
with increasing gas flow rate. The highest combination of gas and liquid flow rates 

279 
 
studied indicates that sampling frequencies greater than 100kHz may be required to 
adequately capture all of the measured two-phase flow parameters.  
 
 
The effect of measurement duration was investigated with the same set of conductivity 
probe measurements used in the sampling frequency sensitivity study by processing 
successively shorter durations of the full 60s signals. The measurements for both group-I 
and group-II bubbles were observed to be insensitive to the measurement duration and 
the number of bubbles, as long as the measurements are made for a duration long enough 
to capture a collection of samples characteristic to a given two-phase flow system.  
 
 
It is recommended that sensitivity studies on sampling frequency and measurement 
duration be implemented as part of data quality control practices to identify the 
appropriate sampling frequency and measurement duration for the flow conditions of 
interest. 
 
 
In regards to probe sensor orientation, it was found that orienting a double-sensor probe 
perpendicular to the duct wall leads to a significantly asymmetric measured bubble 
velocity profile across the pipe diameter, especially for group-II bubbles. However, if the 
double-sensor probe is oriented parallel to the duct wall, a symmetric profile is observed. 
Therefore, when fabricating a conductivity probe, it is recommended that the double 
sensor probe used for bubble velocity measurements be oriented such that it will be 
parallel to the pipe wall. 
 

280 
 
Established reliable experimental database of local two-phase flow parameters and 
characterized the transition from one-group to two-group flows 
 
 
Experiments were performed in nine vertical-upward air-water two-phase flow conditions 
in a 5.08cm inner diameter pipe at 〈𝑗𝑓〉 = 1.00m/s and 2.00m/s with increasing gas flow rates in 
the transition region between the conventional bubbly and slug flow regimes to establish a 
reliable experimental database highlighting transition from one-group to two-group flows.  In 
each flow condition, the state-of-the-art four-sensor conductivity probe was used to acquire 
measurements of local time-averaged two-phase flow parameters, including the void fraction and 
interfacial area concentration, for group-I and group-II bubbles at three axial locations along the 
test section. Signatures of the transition from one-group to two-group flows are identified as: 
 
 
The change in the local void fraction distribution from wall-peaked to center-peaked is a 
characteristic feature of the group transition. As group-I bubbles coalesce and increase in 
size, they migrate towards the center of the duct, causing this change in distribution. 
  
 
The shapes of the local void fraction and interfacial area concentration distributions 
deviate as the transition progresses since the larger bubbles that are responsible for the 
developing center-peaked void fraction profile contribute less interfacial area 
concentration.  
 
 
A key signature of the group transition is the development of a center-peaked 𝐷𝑆𝑚1 
profile. A maximum value during transition of approximately 4mm was identified for the 
air-water flows investigated. 
  

281 
 
 
The axial development of the area-averaged void fraction and interfacial area 
concentration indicates active coalescence in these flow conditions that leads to transfer 
between the groups as the flow develops along the pipe. 
 
 
The total and group-I interfacial area concentrations begin to increase along the pipe in 
more established two-group flows, which suggests that the shearing-off mechanism 
becomes active as the transition progresses. 
 
Assessed TRACE-T against newly established experimental database of one-group to two-
group transition flows 
 
 
The newly established experimental database that highlights one-group to two-group 
transition was used to assess the available two-group interfacial area transport model for 
moderate diameter pipes (Fu 2001; Fu and Ishii, 2002a) in TRACE-T (Bernard, 2014). 
Predictions of the experimental data were made using the system analysis code TRACE-T 
(Bernard, 2014) with Fu’s (2001) two-group interfacial area transport source/sink term models. 
Major observations from the assessment include: 
 
 
The intergroup coalescence mechanisms between group-I bubbles that are responsible for 
generating group-II interfacial area concentration and void fraction from group-I were not 
active in the predictions of the one-group to two-group transition conditions. 
 
 
Intergroup wake-entrainment of group-I bubbles into group-II bubbles was effectively 
inactive in the predictions. Physically, this is a major mechanism during the transition 
process from one-group to two-group flows.  
 

282 
 
 
The shearing-off mechanism was an active source of group-I interfacial area 
concentration in conditions with low contribution of group-II bubbles. This is unphysical, 
since the cap-bubbly flows at the beginning of transition from one-group to two-group 
flows do not contain group-II bubbles of sufficient size for shearing-off to occur. 
 
Developed interfacial area transport models for dynamic transition from one-group to two-
group flows 
 
The necessary bubble interaction source/sink term models to achieve dynamic transition 
from one-group to two-group flows with the two-group interfacial area transport equation were 
developed. The framework of one-group to two-group transition flows was established. The 
increasing importance of wake-dominant bubble transport was identified as the characteristic of 
the initiation of transition. As the transition progresses towards a more established two-group 
flow structure the interfacial area concentration increases due to increasing activity of the 
shearing-off mechanism. In an established two-group flow, shearing-off and wake entrainment 
become the major source and sink, respectively, to the interfacial area concentration. With this 
framework in mind, the following efforts were made to establish the required models for a two-
group interfacial area transport model applicable to one-group to two-group transition flows: 
 
 
A general wake entrainment interaction rate model was established to serve as the basis 
for developing the required source/sink terms for one-group to two-group transition 
flows. 
 
 
Intergroup wake-entrainment of group-I bubbles to form group-II bubbles was considered 
as an evolutionary process to develop the necessary interfacial area concentration and 

283 
 
void fraction source/sink terms to achieve dynamic transition from one-group to two-
group flows. 
 
 
Interfacial area concentration and void fraction source/sink terms for intergroup wake 
entrainment of group-I bubbles into group-II bubbles were developed. This is a major 
mechanism that drives the one-group to two-group transition and becomes the major sink 
to the interfacial area concentration in established two-group flows. 
 
 
A model for the interfacial area concentration sink term due to intragroup coalescence of 
group-II bubbles was developed. 
 
 
The available shearing-off model (Fu, 2001) was modified to include a function of the 
group-II bubble Weber number so that it would become active as an established two-
group flow structure develops. This treatment prevents non-physical shearing-off at the 
initiation of transition. 
 
 
The existing intragroup turbulence impact, random collision, and wake entrainment 
models for group-I bubbles were revised to include a function of the group-II bubble 
Weber number to diminish their importance as an established two-group flow develops. 
This treatment was also included in the newly developed wake-entrainment (11,2) 
source/sink terms. 
 

284 
 
Evaluated newly developed interfacial area transport model for one-group to two-group 
transition flows against experimental data 
 
 
A program was developed in MATLAB to solve the steady-state, one-dimensional, 
adiabatic two-group continuity and interfacial area transport equations for the group void 
fractions and interfacial area concentrations. The newly developed interfacial area transport 
model was implemented and evaluated against the experimental database of one-group to two-
group transition conditions. The major findings of the evaluation can be summarized as: 
 
 
The model has the capability to produce group-II void fraction and interfacial area 
concentration during transition from one-group to two-group flows.  
 
 
 
 
The model accurately captures the exchange between the group void fractions and 
interfacial area concentrations in one-group to two-group transition conditions. 
 
 
 
The shearing-off mechanism dynamically activates as established two-group flows 
develop.  
 
 
The importance of the one-group source/sink terms diminishes as established two-group 
flows develop. 
 
 
The group-I void fraction and interfacial area concentration are predicted with average 
absolute relative errors of ±6% and ±4%, respectively. 
 
 
The group-II void fraction and interfacial area concentration are predicted with average 
absolute relative errors of ±9% and ±11%, respectively. 
 

285 
 
 
The total void fraction and interfacial area concentration are predicted with average 
absolute relative errors of ±3% and ±4%, respectively. 
 
 
These accomplishments are necessary advancements to the state-of-the-art in interfacial 
area transport modeling. However, new topics for study are always identified and are an 
important result of the research process. As such, recommendations for future work are provided 
in Section 7.2 
7.2 Recommendations for Future Work 
In view of the current dissertation, the following recommendations are made for future 
studies to continue the efforts on developing the two-group interfacial area transport equation as a 
constitutive model for the interfacial area concentration in the two-fluid model: 
 
Experimental Studies: 
 
 
Perform experiments in vertical-upward one-group to two-group transition test conditions 
with longer flow development length and more axial measurement locations to establish 
additional experimental database to support continued model validation. 
 
 
Establish additional experimental database in vertical-upward one-group to two-group 
transition two-phase flows in different size channels to assess the scalability of the 
transition model. 
 
 
Investigate the transition from one-group to two-group flows in other two-phase flow 
configurations such as horizontal and vertical-downward two-phase flows. 

286 
 
 
Perform experiments to investigate the transition from an established two-group flow to a 
purely one-group flow. A revised two-phase flow injector that injects large as opposed to 
small bubbles is one way to achieve this. 
 
 
Establish experimental database highlighting the transition from a dispersed-gas two-
group flow to annular two-phase flow. 
 
Model Development: 
 
 
Refine the characterization of one-group to two-group transition phenomena. The present 
dissertation focused on the group-I Sauter-mean diameter as a transition signature. Other 
signatures such as the change in the transverse void fraction profile from wall-peaked to 
center-peaked should be investigated to establish complementary transition criteria to the 
Sauter-mean diameter. 
 
 
Assess the effect of different group-II bubble shapes in the interfacial area transport 
equation and develop a framework, if necessary, to evolve from one shape to another. 
 
 
Develop two-group interfacial area transport models for other two-phase flow 
configurations such as horizontal two-phase flows and vertical-downward two-phase 
flows. 
 
 
Perform an assessment on the importance of different two-phase flow configurations in 
practical applications to guide modeling efforts. 
 
 
Develop a framework and the necessary constitutive relations for the transition from 
established two-group flow to annular two-phase flow. 

287 
 
Computational Investigation: 
 
 
 
Assess the different forms of the expansion/contraction source/sink terms developed in 
this dissertation to determine which is more desirable for numerical implementation. 
 
 
Implement the newly developed interfacial area transport model for one-group to two-
group transitional flows into TRACE-T and assess its performance against available 
experimental database. 
 
 
 
Investigate the performance of the interfacial area transport equation in multi-
dimensional computational fluid dynamics simulations of one-group to two-group 
transition flows. 
 
The two-group interfacial area transport equation as a closure model for the interfacial 
area concentration in the two-fluid model can provide the capability to account for the dynamic 
evolution of the interfacial area concentration. This is required in order to properly account for 
changes in the interfacial structure in modeling two-phase flows. This dissertation addressed the 
essential issue of the transition from one-group to two-group flows. Fundamental improvements 
were made to the interfacial area transport equation through derivation of new expansion source 
terms that properly account for intergroup bubble transport. This major effort provides a solid 
foundation for future work in interfacial area transport model development and implementation 
with the two-fluid model. The systematic investigations into practical aspects of the conductivity 
probe measurement system provided guidelines that can be used in future experimental studies 
for enhanced conductivity probe data acquisition. Furthermore, a needed experimental database 
of local conductivity probe measurements of key two-phase flow parameters was established in 
one-group to two-group transition two-phase flows. With the database, characteristic signatures 

288 
 
of one-group to two-group transition were identified. This database and the signatures that were 
identified provide a basis for continuing the experimental efforts on transitional flows. Lastly, 
interfacial area transport models that provide the necessary capability to dynamically transition 
from one-group to two-group flows were developed. The recommendations for future work 
outlined in this section provide the necessary direction to continue development of the interfacial 
area transport equation.  

289
