Appendix A 
 
Data Reduction Methods 
This Appendix outlines the data reduction methods used to calculate various two-phase 
flow quantities obtained during the experiments in the vertical-upward test section. 
A.1 Superficial Gas Velocity  
 
Dwyer Series RMB rotameters are used to meter the air flow rate to the test section. The 
air rotameters are factory calibrated to measure the volumetric air flow rate in units of standard 
cubic feet per hour (SCFH) at standard conditions of 70°F and 14.7psia (Dwyer Instruments, Inc., 
2015). If the operating conditions at the exit of the flow meter deviate from the standard 
conditions, the gas flow rate observed at the rotameter must be corrected. The correction is given 
as (Dwyer Instruments, Inc., 2015):  
𝑄2 = 𝑄1√𝑝1
𝑝2
𝑇2
𝑇1
 
[A-1] 
where: 
𝑄1: Actual or observed flow meter reading (SCFH) 
𝑄2: Standard flow corrected for pressure and temperature (SCFH) 
𝑝1: Actual pressure (14.7 psia + Gage Pressure psig) 
𝑝2: Standard pressure (14.7 psia, which is 0 psig) 
𝑇1: Actual temperature (460 R + Temp °F) 
𝑇2: Actual temperature (530 R, which is 70°F) 

299 
 
 
The room where the vertical-upward test section is located is maintained at 70°F. 
Therefore, the temperature effect in the correction is neglected. The actual pressure is determined 
as the sum of the standard pressure and the back pressure (𝑝𝑏𝑎𝑐𝑘) that is measured by a dial gauge 
at the exit of the air rotameters. Thus, denoting the gas flow rate observed at the rotameters as 
𝑄𝑔,𝑜𝑏𝑠𝑣 (in SCFH), the equivalent gas flow corrected to the standard pressure condition (𝑄𝑔,𝑎𝑡𝑚) 
is calculated as:  
𝑄𝑔,𝑎𝑡𝑚= 𝑄𝑔,𝑜𝑏𝑠𝑣√(𝑝𝑎𝑡𝑚+ 𝑝𝑏𝑎𝑐𝑘)
𝑝𝑎𝑡𝑚
 
[A-2] 
The subscript “atm” on 𝑄𝑔,𝑎𝑡𝑚 signifies that this is the equivalent volumetric gas flow rate at the 
atmospheric pressure condition. Since the absolute pressure in the test section is greater than 
atmospheric pressure, the actual gas volumetric flow rate in the test section, which varies with 
axial position as the pressure changes, is less than 𝑄𝑔,𝑎𝑡𝑚.  
 
 
Conventionally, researchers in the two-phase flow community report the superficial 
velocities (i.e. volumetric fluxes through the cross-section) of the gas and liquid phases rather 
than the volumetric flow rates themselves. Thus, the gas superficial velocity evaluated at the 
atmospheric pressure condition (〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑎𝑡𝑚) can be considered as an intermediate parameter that is 
given by: 
〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑎𝑡𝑚= 𝑄𝑔,𝑎𝑡𝑚
𝐴
 
[A-3] 
where 𝐴 is the cross-sectional flow area of the test section. With the gauge pressure at a given 
axial elevation above the test section inlet (𝑝𝑧), the actual superficial gas velocity at that elevation 
(〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧) can be calculated as: 

300 
 
〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧= 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑎𝑡𝑚
𝑝𝑎𝑡𝑚
𝑝𝑎𝑡𝑚+ 𝑝𝑧
 
[A-4] 
Measurement of the gauge pressure is discussed in Section A.2. Multiplying 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧 by 𝐴 would 
give the actual gas volumetric flow rate at that elevation (𝑄𝑔,𝑧).  
 
Note that in this discussion, the brackets 〈
〉 are only used to indicate that 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧 and 
〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑎𝑡𝑚 represent cross-sectional average quantities. They are not meant to imply that an area-
averaging procedure was performed on local measurements of 𝑗𝑔. 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑧 and 〈𝑗𝑔〉𝑎𝑡𝑚 are “global,” 
as opposed to “local,” two-phase flow parameters since they are based on measurements from the 
air rotameters, dial pressure gauge, and pressure transducer. These are “global” instruments as 
opposed to “local” instruments like the conductivity probe. Another estimate of the superficial 
gas velocity at a given axial elevation can be obtained by area-averaging the product of the local 
void fraction and bubble velocity measurements obtained with the conductivity probe. This 
estimate of the superficial gas velocity is denoted as 〈𝛼𝑣𝑔〉𝑧. For 〈𝛼𝑣𝑔〉𝑧, the brackets do imply 
that an area-average operation was performed on the product of the local 𝛼 and 𝑣𝑔 measurements. 
The method used to area-average the local two-phase flow parameters obtained with the 
conductivity probe is discussed in Section A.4. Comparing these two estimates of the superficial 
gas velocity serves as a method to benchmark the conductivity probe measurements.          
A.2 Gauge Pressure   
A differential pressure transducer is used to obtain pressure measurements at the 
instrumentation ports in the test section. If the low side of the differential pressure transducer is 
left open to the atmosphere, the pressure transducer can be used obtain gauge pressure 
measurements. The elevation of the pressure transducer is located below all of the pressure taps in 

301 
 
the instrumentation ports of the vertical-upward test facility. The pressure transducer is connected 
to a pressure tap in the instrumentation port through an impulse line that is filled with water. 
Thus, the pressure measured by the transducer (𝑝𝑚,𝑧) includes the hydrostatic head from the 
water in the impulse line. Therefore, the gauge pressure at a given axial elevation above the test 
section inlet (𝑝𝑧) is calculated as: 
𝑝𝑧= 𝑝𝑚,𝑧−𝜌𝑓𝑔Δℎ 
[A-5] 
𝜌𝑓, the density of water, and 𝑔, the gravitational acceleration, are taken to be taken to be 998 
kg/m3 and 9.81 m/s2 in calculations. Δℎ is the height difference between the centers of the 
pressure taps in instrumentation ports and the pressure transducer. The heights of the centers of 
pressure taps in the instrumentation ports and the pressure transducer above the floor are provided 
in Table A-1. Atmospheric pressure is taken to be 101.325kPa to calculate absolute pressures 
from the measured gauge pressures; the deviation in local atmospheric pressure from this value is 
not considered.   
Table A-1: Heights of the centers of the pressure taps above the floor.

A.3 Local Two-Phase Flow Parameters   
The state-of-the-art local four-sensor conductivity probe is used in this dissertation to 
acquire measurements of local time averaged two-phase flow parameters. The improved 
conductivity probe signal processing software developed by Bernard (2014) was used in this 
dissertation to process the conductivity probe signals. The conductivity probe signal processing 
software developed by Bernard (2014) was based on the 4pt200 signal processing program that 
was originally developed at Purdue University (Fu and Ishii, 1999; Kim et al., 2000; Fu, 2001). 
The basic operating principle of the conductivity probe was summarized in Chapter 1. The details 
of the conductivity probe signal processing scheme used to calculate the local time-averaged two-
phase flow parameters from the measured conductivity probe voltage signals can be found in the 
references (Fu and Ishii, 1999; Kim et al., 2000; Fu, 2001; Bernard, 2014).  
In the conductivity probe signal processing scheme (Kim et al., 2000), bubbles are 
categorized as either group-I or group-II bubbles according to the chord length that is measured 
when a bubble hits the probe. The boundary between group-I and group-II bubbles is taken to be 
the maximum distorted bubble limit given by Ishii and Zuber (1979), which is approximately 
10mm for air-water flows near ambient pressure. Thus, bubbles with measured chord lengths less 
than 10mm are classified as group-I bubbles, while bubbles with measured chord lengths greater 
than 10mm are classified as group-II bubbles. Additional details about the bubble categorization 
scheme can be found in the references (Kim et al., 2000; Fu, 2001; Bernard, 2014).  
The local two-phase flow parameters that were obtained in the scoping study test 
conditions and the one-group to two-group transition test conditions are presented in Appendices 
B and C, respectively. Table A-2 describes the local time-averaged two-phase flow parameters 
that are presented in those Appendices. The subscripts “1” and “2” indicate group-I and group-II 
parameters, respectively. Parameters without the “1” or “2” subscripts are total parameters.  

303

sensor conductivity probe and are presented in Appendices B and C.

conductivity probe. Likewise, 𝑁𝑏= 𝑁𝑏1 + 𝑁𝑏2 is the total number of bubbles detected. 𝑁𝑏1, 𝑁𝑏2, and 𝑁𝑏 
are the actual output parameters of the conductivity probe signal processing software. 𝑓𝑏1, 𝑓𝑏2, and 𝑓𝑏 are 
calculated as additional parameters in a Microsoft Excel spreadsheet that is used to organize and study the 
outputted parameters from the conductivity probe signal processing software.         
A.4 Area-Averaged Two-Phase Flow Parameters 
 
The local time-averaged two-phase flow parameters obtained with the conductivity probe 
can provide great insight into the local structure of two-phase flows and are invaluable to the 
development and benchmarking of multi-dimensional models of two-phase flow. However, two-
phase flows are often analyzed in a one-dimensional (i.e. cross-sectional averaged) sense such as 
in nuclear reactor thermal-hydraulic system analysis codes like TRACE and RELAP (TRACE 
v5.0 Theory Manual, 2007; RELAP5-3D Code Manual, 2005). Therefore, the local two-phase 

304 
 
flow parameters obtained with the conductivity probe are area-averaged over the flow cross-
section to obtain area-averaged two-phase flow parameters suitable for one-dimensional analysis. 
 
The area-average of a general local quantity 𝜉(𝒙, 𝑡) is defined as: 
〈𝜉〉= 〈𝜉〉(𝑧, 𝑡) = 1
𝐴∫𝜉(𝒙, 𝑡)𝑑𝐴
𝐴
 
[A-6] 
Since we are interested in area-averaging local time-averaged two-phase flow parameters, the 
dependence on time will be suppressed in the remainder of this discussion. Furthermore, since 
two-phase flows through a round pipe are investigated in this dissertation, consider a cylindrical 
coordinate system with the z-coordinate directed along the axis of the pipe such that 𝜉(𝒙) =
𝜉(𝑟, 𝜃, 𝑧). Let 𝑅 be the inner radius of the pipe, and 𝐴 is the cross-sectional area of the pipe 
perpendicular to z-direction. Also, for the purpose of the present discussion, assume that 𝜉 is 
axisymmetric such that 𝜉(𝑟, 𝜃, 𝑧) = 𝜉(𝑟, 𝑧). Thus, the area-average of  𝜉 in the pipe cross-section 
is given by: 
〈𝜉〉(𝑧) =

Equation [A-7] can be recast as:

〈𝜉〉(𝑧) ≈2 (Δ𝑟∗

In the context of area-averaging local conductivity probe measurements, 𝜉𝑗 corresponds

that were used for numerical integration of the conductivity probe measurements acquired across 
the entire diameter of the pipe. The meshes used for numerical integration are identical to the 
conductivity probe measurement meshes discussed in Chapter 3 except that the points at the pipe 
wall (i.e. 𝑟∗= ±1.0) are shown. It is assumed that the local two-phase flow parameters go to zero 
at the pipe wall. However, these points are still included in the area-averaging scheme for 
completeness. Figure A-1(a) corresponds to the uniform measurement mesh with Δ𝑟∗= 0.1 that 
was used for the conductivity probe measurements in the one-group to two-group transition test 
conditions. In the interest of time during the scoping study experiments, a sparser measurement 
mesh was used. The two-phase flow parameters do not typically have strong gradients near the 
center of the pipe. Therefore Δ𝑟∗= 0.2 was used in this region during the scoping study 
experiments. However, it is important to have a finer resolution of points near the pipe wall to 

306 
 
capture the gradients there to obtain appropriate area-averaged values. Therefore, for |𝑟∗| 
between 0.8 and 1.0 a mesh spacing of Δ𝑟∗= 0.1 was used. This non-uniform mesh is shown in 
Figure A-1(b).

B.3 Local Two-Phase Flow Parameters 
 
This Section contains the local two-phase flow parameters obtained with the four-sensor 
conductivity probe during the scoping study test conditions, Runs S1-S27. Descriptions of the 
local two-phase flow parameters that are presented are given in Table A-2. Two conductivity 
probes, denoted as Probe A and Probe B, were used during the scoping study measurements. The 
sensor configurations for each probe are provided in Table B-6. At each radial position in the pipe 
cross-section, conductivity probe measurements were acquired with a sampling frequency of 
50kHz for a measurement duration of 60s.  
Table B-6: Sensor configurations of conductivity probes used to acquire local measurements.

B.4 Area-Averaged Two-Phase Flow Parameters  
 
This Section presents the area-averaged two-phase flow parameters that are calculated 
from the local four-sensor conductivity probe measurements that are presented in Section B.3 for 
the scoping study test conditions, Runs S1-S27. The area-averaged parameters are calculated 
from the local conductivity probe measurements as described in Appendix A. Descriptions of the 
area-averaged two-phase flow parameters that are presented are given in Table A-3.

the vertical-upward test section. Therefore, non-dimensional lengths (L/D) from the two-phase 
flow inlet to both the port centers and pressure taps are included for reference in Table C-2.

C.3 Local Two-Phase Flow Parameters 
 
This Section presents the local two-phase flow parameters obtained with the four-sensor 
conductivity probe in the one-group to two-group transition test conditions, Runs 1-9. 
Descriptions of the local two-phase flow parameters that are presented are given in Table A-2.  
The same conductivity probe, Probe B described in Table B-6, was used to acquire all of the 
measurements presented in this Section. At each radial position in the pipe cross-section, 
conductivity probe measurements were acquired with a sampling frequency of 50kHz for a 
measurement duration of 60s.

C.4 Area-Averaged Two-Phase Flow Parameters  
 
This Section presents the area-averaged two-phase flow parameters that are calculated 
from the local four-sensor conductivity probe measurements that are presented in Section C.3 for 
the one-group to two-group transition test conditions, Runs 1-9. The area-averaged parameters are 
calculated from the local measurements as described in Appendix A. Descriptions of the area-
averaged two-phase flow parameters that are presented are given in Table A-3.

Appendix D 
 
Shape Factors for Approximate Bubble Shapes 
This Appendix provides a summary of the expressions for some pertinent geometric 
parameters including the shape factors 𝜑 and (𝐷𝑆𝑚𝐷𝐷)
⁄
 for three approximate bubble shapes: an 
oblate-ellipsoid bubble, a spherical-cap bubble, and an idealized slug bubble. Oblate-ellipsoid 
bubbles were investigated as a more complex approximate group-I bubble shape in comparison to 
a spherical bubble. The geometric parameters for spherical bubbles are straightforward, so they 
are not presently summarized. Recall that the shape factor 𝜑 represents the ratio of actual bubble 
surface area to the surface area of a volume equivalent sphere, and (𝐷𝑆𝑚𝐷𝐷)
⁄
 is a drag shape 
factor that is proportional to the ratio of the bubble’s projected area to its surface area. 
D.1 Oblate-Ellipsoid Bubble 
 
Figure D-1 shows a schematic of an oblate-ellipsoid bubble. Consider the bubble rising 
vertically upward; the vertical direction is indicated in the figure by the gravity vector. Thus, the 
length of the dimension parallel to the vertical direction is denoted as the vertical diameter (𝐷𝑉), 
and the length of the dimension perpendicular to the vertical direction is denoted as the horizontal 
diameter (𝐷𝐻).

Ishii and Mishima (1980) obtained similar expressions for these shape factors. Using the 
correlation by Wellek et al. (1966) for 𝐸𝑜𝑒, Ishii and Mishima (1980) showed that 𝜑𝑜𝑒 varies 
within about 10% of unity for 𝐸𝑜𝑒 varying between 0.5 and 1.0. With Wellek et al.’s (1966) 
correlation, 𝐸𝑜𝑒= 0.5 corresponds to a volume equivalent diameter of approximately 9mm for 
air-water flows at ambient conditions; 𝐸𝑜𝑒 increases to unity with decreasing bubble size. This 
finding supports the approximation of group-I bubbles as spheres (i.e. 𝜑1 = 𝜑𝑠𝑝ℎ= 1) instead of 
more complicated oblate ellipsoids in specifying 𝐴𝑖1(𝑉) in the interfacial area transport equation. 
However, Ishii and Mishima (1980) also show that the drag shape factor (𝐷𝑆𝑚,𝑜𝑒𝐷𝐷,𝑜𝑒
⁄
) varies 
significantly from unity for this range of 𝐸𝑜𝑒. This suggests that additional investigation may be 
required into the modeling of (𝐷𝑆𝑚𝐷𝐷
⁄
)1, which is conventionally assumed to be unity.

D.3 Idealized Slug Bubble 
 
An idealized slug bubble shape was introduced in Chapter 2 as an approximate shape for 
group-II bubbles in confined two-group flows in round pipes. Figure D-3 shows a schematic of 
the side-view of the idealized slug bubble that consists of a cylindrical body with a spherical-cap 
shaped nose. The rear interface at the tail of the slug bubble is assumed to be flat. 𝐷𝑠𝑙𝑢𝑔= 𝜆𝐷ℎ is 
the base diameter of the slug bubble (i.e. the diameter of the cylindrical body). The slug bubble 
base diameter is a fraction of the duct hydraulic diameter owing to the liquid film around the slug 
bubble. Thus, the factor 𝜆 represents the ratio of the slug bubble base diameter to the duct 
hydraulic diameter. 𝐿𝑠𝑙𝑢𝑔 is the length of the idealized slug bubble.        
 
Figure D-3: Idealized slug bubble shape approximated as having a cylindrical body with a 
spherical-cap shaped nose. 

419

Appendix E 
 
Two-Group Interfacial Area Transport Equation MATLAB Program  
This Appendix contains the MATLAB program that was written to evaluate the two-
group interfacial area transport equation. The following Sections contain the various subsections 
of the code in the order that they are executed.  

423 
 
 
E.1 main.m 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Program to Evaluate Two-Group IATE%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
clear all; 
close all; 
  
%Number of flow conditions to analyze 
ncond=9; 
%Numerical labels for conditions to be analyzed (e.g. '1' for Run 1) 
%For example, to analyze Runs 1, 3, and 5, set: 
%num_cond=3; 
% cond_index=[1,3,5]; 
% cond_label=['Run1';'Run3';'Run5']; 
cond_index=[1,2,3,4,5,6,7,8,9]; 
cond_label=['Run1';'Run2';'Run3';'Run4';'Run5';'Run6';'Run7';'Run8';... 
    'Run9']; 
  
%Load physical constants and properties 
const 
  
%Set up channel geometry and numerical mesh 
changeo 
  
%Load model coefficients 
coeff 
  
%Load experimental values of two-phase flow parameters 
expdata 
  
%Analyze selected conditions 
for k=1:ncond 
    %Initialize individual mechanism contribution terms 
    dinit 
            
    %Linearly interpolate experimental vg1, vg2, and p 
    interp 
  
    %Set boundary conditions at first node     
    boundary 
  
    %Set gas density along channel assuming isothermal 
    rhog=rhog_atm*p(k,:)/patm; 
     
    %Solve continuity and interfacial area transport equations along 
    %along the test section for condition k 
    for i=1:nz_step 
        %Calculate parameters used in source/sink term models using 
        %information at current node (i) 
        param 

424 
 
 
         
        %Calculate mass and ai source/sink terms using information at 
        %current node (i) 
        src 
         
        %Calculate void fraction and ai at next node (i+1) using group  
        %continuity and interfacial area transport equations 
        %Group-I Continuity 
        alpha1(k,i+1)=((alpha1(k,i)*vg1(k,i)*rhog(i))+(m1(k,i)... 
            *deltaz))/(rhog(i+1)*vg1(k,i+1)); 
  
        %Group-I IATE 
        ai1(k,i+1)=((ai1(k,i)*vg1(k,i))+(phinet1(k,i)*deltaz))... 
            /vg1(k,i+1); 
         
        %Group-II Continuity 
        alpha2(k,i+1)=((alpha2(k,i)*vg2(k,i)*rhog(i))+m2(k,i)... 
            *deltaz)/(rhog(i+1)*vg2(k,i+1)); 
             
        %Group-II IATE 
        ai2(k,i+1)=((ai2(k,i)*vg2(k,i))+(phinet2(k,i)*deltaz))... 
            /vg2(k,i+1); 
         
        %Calculate total alpha, ai, and vg at next node 
        alpha(k,i+1)=alpha1(k,i+1)+alpha2(k,i+1); 
        ai(k,i+1)=ai1(k,i+1)+ai2(k,i+1); 
        vg(k,i+1)=(alpha1(k,i+1)*vg1(k,i+1)+alpha2(k,i+1)... 
            *vg2(k,i+1))/alpha(k,i+1); 
         
        %Calculate Group-I, Group-II and Total Sauter-mean diameters at 
        %next node 
        DSm1(k,i+1)=6.0*alpha1(k,i+1)/ai1(k,i+1); %[m] 
        DSm2(k,i+1)=6.0*alpha2(k,i+1)/ai2(k,i+1); %[m] 
        DSm(k,i+1)=6.0*alpha(k,i+1)/ai(k,i+1); %[m]         
    end 
     
    %Calculate dais along test section 
    dai1(k,:)=ai1(k,:)-ai1(k,1); 
    dai2(k,:)=ai2(k,:)-ai2(k,1); 
    dai(k,:)=ai(k,:)-ai(k,1); 
     
    %Calculate dalphas along test section 
    dalpha1(k,:)=alpha1(k,:)-alpha1(k,1); 
    dalpha2(k,:)=alpha2(k,:)-alpha2(k,1); 
    dalpha(k,:)=alpha(k,:)-alpha(k,1); 
     
    %compute relative errors 
    errors 
     
    %Generate output  
    output    
end 

425 
 
 
E.2 const.m 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Physical Constants and Properties%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%Gravitational acceleration [m/s^2] 
grav=9.81; 
  
%Atmospheric pressure  
patm=101325; %[Pa] 
  
%Air-water properties at ambient conditions (P=101325 Pa; T= 20 C) 
%Mass densities  
rhof=998.21; %[kg/m^3] 
rhog_atm=1.204; %[kg/m^3] 
  
%Dynamic viscosities 
muf=0.0010016; %[Pa-s] 
mug=0.00001820755; %[Pa-s] 
  
%Air-water surface tension [N/m] 
sigma=0.0728; %[N/m] 
  
%Laplace length [m] 
La=sqrt(sigma/grav/(rhof-rhog_atm)); 
  
%Maximum distorted bubble diameter (Ishii and Zuber,1979) 
Ddmax_star=4.0; %[-] 
Ddmax=Ddmax_star*La; %[m] 
E.3 changeo.m 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Channel Geometry and Numerical Mesh%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%Channel hydraulic diameter 
Dh=0.0508; %[m] 
  
%Measurement location L/D's [-] (center of instrumentation port) 
portL_D(1)=18; 
portL_D(2)=34.5; 
portL_D(3)=63; 
  
%Measurement location L's [m] 
zport(1)=Dh*portL_D(1); 
zport(2)=Dh*portL_D(2); 

426 
 
 
zport(3)=Dh*portL_D(3); 
  
%Setup numerical mesh 
deltaz=0.01; %[m] 
zbot=zport(1); 
ztop=zport(3); 
nz=floor((ztop-zbot)/deltaz)+1; 
nz_step=nz-1; 
for i=1:nz 
   z(i)=zport(1)+deltaz*(i-1); 
end 
  
%Mesh L/D [-] for plotting 
L_D=z/Dh; 
E.4 coeff.m 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Model Coefficients%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%Group-I Intragroup Turbulence Impact (1,11) 
CTI111=0.085; 
Wecr1=6.0; 
  
%Group-I Intragroup Random Collision (11,1) 
CRC111=0.004; 
alpha1max=0.75; 
C=3; 
  
%Intragroup Wake Entrainment (11,1)  
CWE111=0.002; 
  
%Intergroup Wake Entrainment (11,2) 
CWE112=0.009; 
greekbeta=0.5; 
relax_a=0.01; 
%Critical diameter at group boundary Dc 
Dc=Ddmax; 
%Non-dimensional transition Group-I Sauter-mean diameter 
DSm1tr_star=0.4; %[-] 
DSm1tr=DSm1tr_star*Dc; %[m] 
  
%Intergroup Wake Entrainment (12,2) 
CWE122=0.025; 
  
%Group-II Intragroup Wake Entrainment (22,2) 
CWE222=0.15; 
  

427 
 
 
%Shearing-Off (2,12) 
CSO212=0.12; 
gammaSO=0.032; 
betaSO=1.6; 
alpha2max=0.81;  
% Cfr=1.8536; 
Wecr2=7; 
  
%Shape factors 
%Group-I bubble shape factor phi1=(De/Dsm)_1 ; assume spheres unity 
phi1=1.0; 
%Group-II bubble shape factor phi2=(De/Dsm)_2; assume cap shaped 
%with 50-deg wake angle 
phi2=1.692; 
%Bubble shape factor at group boundary phic 
phic=(phi1+phi2)/2.0; 
%Group-I drag shape factor (DSm/DD)_1; assume spheres, so unity 
DSm_DD1=1.0; 
%Group-II drag shape factor (DSm/DD); cap with 50-deg wake angle 
DSm_DD2=1.804; 
  
%Numerical treatment for lower limit of group-II void fraction  
%to prevent divide by zero errors when zero group-II 
minalpha2=1E-6;

E.7 interp.m 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%Interpolate Experimental Pressure and Velocity Data%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%Linearly interpolate between experimental pressures 
p(k,:)=interp1(zport,exp_p(cond_index(k),(1:3)),z,'linear'); 
  
%Linearly interpolate between experimental velocities 
vg1(k,:)=interp1(zport,exp_vg1(cond_index(k),(1:3)),z,'linear'); 
vg2(k,:)=interp1(zport,exp_vg2(cond_index(k),(1:3)),z,'linear'); 
  
%Numerical treatment for group-II velocity field if no group-II 
%velocities were measured to interpolate (i.e. conditions with 
%extremely low group-II contribution) 
if vg2(k,:)==0.0 
    vg2(k,:)=vg1(k,:); 
end 

432 
 
 
E.8 boundary.m 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Boundary Conditions%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%Void fraction 
alpha1(k,1)=exp_alpha1(cond_index(k),1); 
alpha2(k,1)=exp_alpha2(cond_index(k),1); 
alpha(k,1)=alpha1(k,1)+alpha2(k,1); 
  
%Interfacial area concentration 
ai1(k,1)=exp_ai1(cond_index(k),1); 
ai2(k,1)=exp_ai2(cond_index(k),1); 
ai(k,1)=ai1(k,1)+ai2(k,1); 
  
%Sauter-mean diameter 
DSm1(k,1)=6*alpha1(k,1)/ai1(k,1); %[m] 
if alpha2(k,1)<minalpha2 
    DSm2(k,1)=0.0; 
else 
    DSm2(k,1)=6*alpha2(k,1)/ai2(k,1); %[m] 
end 
DSm(k,1)=6*alpha(k,1)/ai(k,1); %[m] 
  
%Total velocity 
vg(k,1)=(alpha1(k,1)*vg1(k,1)+alpha2(k,1)*vg2(k,1))/alpha(k,1); 
E.9 param.m 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Source/Sink Term Model Parameters%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%Liquid velocity 
vf=exp_jf(cond_index(k))/(1-alpha(k,i)); 
  
%Mixture velocity 
v_m=(rhof*(1-alpha(k,i))*vf+rhog(i)*alpha1(k,i)*vg1(k,i)+rhog(i)... 
      *alpha2(k,i)*vg2(k,i))/((1-alpha(k,i))*rhof+alpha(k,i)*rhog(i)); 
  
%Mixture viscosity (Ishii and Chawala, 1979) 
mu_m=muf/(1-alpha(k,i)); 
  
%Mixture Reynolds number   
Re_m=rhof*v_m*Dh/mu_m; 
   
%Two-phase friction factor  

433 
 
 
f2ph=0.316*((mu_m/muf)^0.25)/(Re_m^0.25); 
  
%Turbulent kinetic energy dissipation rate 
eps=f2ph*(v_m^3.0)/2.0/Dh; 
  
%Turbulent fluctuation velocity 
ut1=sqrt(2.0)*((eps*DSm1(k,i))^(1.0/3.0)); 
  
%Group-I bubble Weber number 
We1=rhof*(ut1*ut1)*DSm1(k,i)/sigma; 
  
%Shape factors for number densities, surface areas, and volumes 
psi1=(1.0/36.0/pi)*(1.0/phi1)^3.0; 
psi2=(1.0/36.0/pi)*(1.0/phi2)^3.0; 
  
%Group-I number density 
n1=psi1*(ai1(k,i)^3.0)/(alpha1(k,i)^2.0); 
  
%Group-II number density 
if alpha2(k,i)<minalpha2 %numerical treatment to prevent divide by zero 
    n2=0.0; 
else 
    n2=psi2*(ai2(k,i)^3.0)/(alpha2(k,i)^2.0); 
end 
  
%Characteristic surface areas and volumes 
Ai1=(1.0/psi1)*((alpha1(k,i)/ai1(k,i))^2.0); 
V1=(1.0/psi1)*((alpha1(k,i)/ai1(k,i))^3.0); 
  
if alpha2(k,i)<minalpha2 %numerical treatment to prevent divide by zero 
    Ai2=0.0; 
    V2=0.0; 
else 
    Ai2=(1.0/psi2)*((alpha2(k,i)/ai2(k,i))^2.0); 
    V2=(1.0/psi2)*((alpha2(k,i)/ai2(k,i))^3.0); 
end 
  
%Distorted particle regime drag coefficient for group-I bubbles 
%Simplified form using 1/(1-alpha1)^0.5 (Ishii and Zuber, 1979) 
%Assume alpha1 is the appropriate void fraction to use since a group-I  
%bubble primarily "sees" other group-I bubbles, leading to increased  
%drag 
CD1=(2.0/3.0)*DSm1(k,i)*sqrt(grav*(rhof-rhog(i))/sigma)*... 
    (1/sqrt(1-alpha1(k,i))); 
%Group-I relative velocity 
ur1=sqrt((4.0*grav*DSm1(k,i)*(rhof-rhog(i))*(1-alpha(k,i)))/... 
    (3*CD1*rhof*DSm_DD1)); 
  
%Churn-turbulent regime drag coefficient for group-II bubbles 
%(Ishii and Chawla, 1979; Ishii and Zuber, 1979) 
%Assume group-II void fraction is the appropriate void fraction to use  
%in this drag coefficient correlation 

434 
 
 
CD2=(8.0/3.0)*(1.0-alpha2(k,i))*(1.0-alpha2(k,i)); 
ur2=sqrt((4.0*grav*DSm2(k,i)*(rhof-rhog(i))*(1-alpha(k,i)))... 
    /(3.0*CD2*rhof*DSm_DD2)); 
  
%Group-II Weber number 
We2=rhof*(ur2*ur2)*DSm2(k,i)/sigma; 
E.10 src.m 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Mass and ai Source/Sink Terms%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%Expansion/Contraction Source/Sink Terms 
%Group-I Intragroup Expansion 
phiINTRAEXP1(k,i)=-(2.0/3.0)*ai1(k,i)*vg1(k,i)*(rhog(i+1)-rhog(i))... 
    /(deltaz*rhog(i+1)); 
  
%Group-II Intragroup Expansion 
phiINTRAEXP2(k,i)=-(2.0/3.0)*ai2(k,i)*vg2(k,i)*(rhog(i+1)-rhog(i))... 
    /(deltaz*rhog(i+1)); 
  
%Modification factor to activate shearing-off and reduce one-group  
%mechanisms as established two-group flow develops 
if (We2>Wecr2) 
    gmod=sqrt(1.0-Wecr2/We2); 
else 
    gmod=0.0; 
end 
    
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Group-I Intragroup Turbulence Impact (1,11)%%%%%%%%%%% 
%Group-I ai Source 
if(We1>Wecr1) 
    phiTI1_111(k,i)=CTI111*(1.0-gmod)*(1.0/18.0)*... 
        (ai1(k,i)*ai1(k,i)*ut1)/alpha1(k,i)*sqrt(1.0-Wecr1/We1)*... 
        exp(-Wecr1/We1); 
else 
    phiTI1_111(k,i)=0.0; 
end 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Group-I Intragroup Random Collision (11,1)%%%%%%%%%%%% 
phiRC1_111(k,i)=-CRC111*(1.0-gmod)*(1.0/3.0/pi)*... 
    (ai1(k,i)*ai1(k,i)*ut1)/alpha1max^(1.0/3.0)... 
    /(alpha1max^(1.0/3.0)-alpha1(k,i)^(1.0/3.0))... 
    *(1-exp(-C*alpha1max^(1.0/3.0)*alpha1(k,i)^(1.0/3.0)... 
    /(alpha1max^(1.0/3.0)-alpha1(k,i)^(1.0/3.0)))); 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Group-I Intragroup Wake Entrainment (11,1)%%%%%%%%%%%%  

435 
 
 
phiWE1_111(k,i)=-CWE111*(1.0-gmod)*(1.0/3.0/pi)*(CD1^(1.0/3.0))*... 
    ai1(k,i)*ai1(k,i)*ur1; 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Intergroup Wake Entrainment (11,2)%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%Non-dimensional group-I Sauter mean diameter 
DSm1star=DSm1(k,i)/Dc; 
%Transition function 
term1=(DSm1tr_star^3.0)+greekbeta-2.0*(DSm1star^3.0); 
term2=(DSm1tr_star^3.0)+greekbeta-1.0; 
T(k,i)=1.0/(1.0+(relax_a/(1-relax_a))^(term1/term2)); 
  
%Interaction Rate 
RWE_112=CWE112*(1.0-gmod)*(3.0/8.0*pi^(4.0/3.0))*... 
    (CD1^(1.0/3.0)*ur1*n1*n1*DSm1(k,i)*DSm1(k,i))*... 
    ((greekbeta^(1.0/3.0))/DSm1star)^(7.0/3.0)*T(k,i); 
  
%Characteristic surface area and volume changes for (11,2) process 
dAi1_112=-Ai1*((greekbeta^(2.0/3.0))/(1.0-greekbeta)*DSm1star+1.0); 
dAi2_112=Ai1*(phic/(1.0-greekbeta)*DSm1star); 
dV2_112=V1/(1.0-greekbeta); 
  
%Group-I ai Sink 
phiWE1_112(k,i)=dAi1_112*RWE_112; 
  
%Group-II ai Source 
phiWE2_112(k,i)=dAi2_112*RWE_112; 
  
%Group-II Void Source 
etaWE2_112(k,i)=dV2_112*RWE_112; 
%Group-I Void Sink 
etaWE1_112(k,i)=-etaWE2_112(k,i); 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Intergroup Wake Entrainment (12,2)%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%Projected area for leading group-II bubbles as minimum of either  
%cap projected area or pipe cross-section 
Aproj=(pi/4.0)*min((DSm_DD2*(phi2^3.0)*(DSm2(k,i)*DSm2(k,i))),Dh*Dh); 
%Interaction Rate 
RWE_122=CWE122*(3.0/2.0*pi^(1.0/3.0))*(CD2^(1.0/3.0))*... 
    (n1/(1.0-alpha2(k,i)))*n2*Aproj*ur2; 
  
%Group-I ai Sink 
phiWE1_122(k,i)=-Ai1*RWE_122; 
  
%Group-II ai Source 
if alpha2(k,i)<minalpha2 
    phiWE2_122(k,i)=0.0; %numerical treatment to avoid divide by zero 
else 
    phiWE2_122(k,i)=(2.0/3.0)*(psi2/psi1)*... 
        ((DSm1(k,i)/DSm2(k,i))^3.0)*Ai2*RWE_122; 
end 
%Group-II Void Source 

436 
 
 
etaWE2_122(k,i)=V1*RWE_122; 
%Group-I Void Sink 
etaWE1_122(k,i)=-etaWE2_122(k,i); 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Group-II Intragroup Wake Entrainment (22,2)%%%%%%%%%%% 
%Interaction Rate 
RWE_222=CWE222*(3.0/2.0*pi^(1.0/3.0))*(CD2^(1.0/3.0))*(n2*n2)*... 
    Aproj*ur2; 
  
%Group-II ai Sink 
phiWE2_222(k,i)=-(1.0/3.0)*Ai2*RWE_222; 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Intergroup Shearing-Off (2,12)%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%Fu's (2001) Shearing-Off Model 
gfac=sqrt(2.0*grav*(rhof-rhog(i))/rhof); 
discrm=max((6.86*6.86+4.0*2.54*(1.0-1.35*Dh/DSm2(k,i))),0); 
vstars=6.86-sqrt(discrm)/2.0/2.54; 
if vstars <0.0 
    vstars=0.0; 
end 
if vstars>1.0; 
   vstars=1.0; 
end 
  
% kappafr=1-exp(-Cfr*sqrt(vstars/Dh)); %omit this term since units are 
%inconsistent; near unity for current flow conditions, so set to 1.0 
kappafr=1.0; 
kappabl=((Dh^-0.3)*(alpha2max^-0.5)*((mug/rhog(i))^0.2)*(gfac^-0.2)... 
    *(vstars^-0.7)*(kappafr^-0.2))^(1.0/7.0); 
ratio_so=1.0/(1.0-exp(-gammaSO*((alpha2max/(alpha2max-alpha2(k,i)))... 
    ^betaSO)*(Wecr1/We1))); 
%Group-I ai Source 
phiSO1_212(k,i)=CSO212*gmod*0.5755*(gfac*gfac)*... 
    ((mug/rhog(i))^(1.0/5.0))*((rhof/sigma/Dh)^(3.0/5.0))... 
    *alpha2(k,i)*(vstars^(-4.0/5.0))*(1.0-0.6535*kappabl)*ratio_so... 
    *(kappafr*kappafr); 
  
%Group-II ai Sink 
phiSO2_212(k,i)=-CSO212*gmod*4.4332*((mug/rhog(i))^(1.0/5.0))*... 
    (Dh^(-9.0/5.0))*sqrt(alpha2max)*(gfac^(4.0/5.0))*alpha2(k,i)*... 
    (vstars^(-1.0/5.0))*(1.0-0.6474*kappabl)*(kappafr^(4.0/5.0)); 
  
%Group-II Void Sink 
etaSO2_212(k,i)=-CSO212*gmod*1.1083*((mug/rhog(i))^(1.0/5.0))*... 
    (Dh^(-4.0/5.0))*(gfac^(4.0/5.0))*alpha2(k,i)*(vstars^(-1.0/5.0))... 
    *(1.0-0.6474*kappabl)*(kappafr^(4.0/5.0)); 
  
%Group-I Void Source 
etaSO1_212(k,i)=-etaSO2_212(k,i); 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  

437 
 
 
%Calculate net ai source/sink terms 
phinet1(k,i)=phiINTRAEXP1(k,i)+phiRC1_111(k,i)+phiTI1_111(k,i)... 
    +phiWE1_111(k,i)+phiWE1_112(k,i)+phiWE1_122(k,i)+phiSO1_212(k,i); 
phinet2(k,i)=phiINTRAEXP2(k,i)+phiWE2_112(k,i)+phiWE2_122(k,i)... 
    +phiWE2_222(k,i)+phiSO2_212(k,i); 
  
%Calculate the net intergroup mass transfer rates 
m2(k,i)=rhog(i)*(etaWE2_112(k,i)+etaWE2_122(k,i)+etaSO2_212(k,i)); 
m1(k,i)=-m2(k,i);%ensures total continuity of gas phase 
  
%Group-I ai individual contributions 
dphiRC1_111(k,i+1)=dphiRC1_111(k,i)+phiRC1_111(k,i)*deltaz/vg1(k,i+1); 
dphiTI1_111(k,i+1)=dphiTI1_111(k,i)+phiTI1_111(k,i)*deltaz/vg1(k,i+1); 
dphiWE1_111(k,i+1)=dphiWE1_111(k,i)+phiWE1_111(k,i)*deltaz/vg1(k,i+1); 
dphiWE1_112(k,i+1)=dphiWE1_112(k,i)+phiWE1_112(k,i)*deltaz/vg1(k,i+1); 
dphiWE1_122(k,i+1)=dphiWE1_122(k,i)+phiWE1_122(k,i)*deltaz/vg1(k,i+1); 
dphiSO1_212(k,i+1)=dphiSO1_212(k,i)+phiSO1_212(k,i)*deltaz/vg1(k,i+1); 
dphiINTRAEXP1(k,i+1)=dphiINTRAEXP1(k,i)+phiINTRAEXP1(k,i)*... 
    deltaz/vg1(k,i+1); 
dphiVG1(k,i+1)=dphiVG1(k,i)+(ai1(k,i)/vg1(k,i+1))*... 
    (vg1(k,i)-vg1(k,i+1)); 
%dphiSUM1 computed as a check against dai1 
dphiSUM1(k,i+1)=dphiRC1_111(k,i+1)+dphiTI1_111(k,i+1)+... 
    dphiWE1_111(k,i+1)+dphiWE1_112(k,i+1)+dphiWE1_122(k,i+1)+... 
    dphiSO1_212(k,i+1)+dphiINTRAEXP1(k,i+1)+dphiVG1(k,i+1);  
  
%Group-II ai individual contributions 
dphiWE2_112(k,i+1)=dphiWE2_112(k,i)+phiWE2_112(k,i)*deltaz/vg2(k,i+1); 
dphiWE2_122(k,i+1)=dphiWE2_122(k,i)+phiWE2_122(k,i)*deltaz/vg2(k,i+1); 
dphiWE2_222(k,i+1)=dphiWE2_222(k,i)+phiWE2_222(k,i)*deltaz/vg2(k,i+1); 
dphiSO2_212(k,i+1)=dphiSO2_212(k,i)+phiSO2_212(k,i)*deltaz/vg2(k,i+1); 
dphiINTRAEXP2(k,i+1)=dphiINTRAEXP2(k,i)+phiINTRAEXP2(k,i)*... 
    deltaz/vg2(k,i+1); 
dphiVG2(k,i+1)=dphiVG2(k,i)+(ai2(k,i)/vg2(k,i+1))*... 
    (vg2(k,i)-vg2(k,i+1)); 
%dphiSUM2 computed as a check against dai2 
dphiSUM2(k,i+1)=dphiWE2_112(k,i+1)+dphiWE2_122(k,i+1)+... 
    dphiWE2_222(k,i+1)+dphiSO2_212(k,i+1)+dphiINTRAEXP2(k,i+1)+... 
    dphiVG2(k,i+1); 
  
%Group-I void individual contributions 
detaWE1_112(k,i+1)=detaWE1_112(k,i)+etaWE1_112(k,i)*deltaz/vg1(k,i+1); 
detaWE1_122(k,i+1)=detaWE1_122(k,i)+etaWE1_122(k,i)*deltaz/vg1(k,i+1); 
detaSO1_212(k,i+1)=detaSO1_212(k,i)+etaSO1_212(k,i)*deltaz/vg1(k,i+1); 
detaEXP1(k,i+1)=detaEXP1(k,i)+(alpha1(k,i)/rhog(i+1))*... 
    (rhog(i)-rhog(i+1)); 
detaVG1(k,i+1)=detaVG1(k,i)+(alpha1(k,i)/vg1(k,i+1))*... 
    (vg1(k,i)-vg1(k,i+1)); 
%detaSUM1 computed as a check against dalpha1 
detaSUM1(k,i+1)=detaWE1_112(k,i+1)+detaWE1_122(k,i+1)+... 
    detaSO1_212(k,i+1)+detaEXP1(k,i+1)+detaVG1(k,i+1); 
  
%Group-II void individual contributions 

438 
 
 
detaWE2_112(k,i+1)=detaWE2_112(k,i)+etaWE2_112(k,i)*deltaz/vg2(k,i+1); 
detaWE2_122(k,i+1)=detaWE2_122(k,i)+etaWE2_122(k,i)*deltaz/vg2(k,i+1); 
detaSO2_212(k,i+1)=detaSO2_212(k,i)+etaSO2_212(k,i)*deltaz/vg2(k,i+1); 
detaEXP2(k,i+1)=detaEXP2(k,i) + (alpha2(k,i)/rhog(i+1))*... 
    (rhog(i)-rhog(i+1)); 
detaVG2(k,i+1)=detaVG2(k,i)+(alpha2(k,i)/vg2(k,i+1))*... 
    (vg2(k,i)-vg2(k,i+1)); 
%detaSUM2 computed as a check against dalpha2 
detaSUM2(k,i+1)=detaWE2_112(k,i+1)+detaWE2_122(k,i+1)+... 
    detaSO2_212(k,i+1)+detaEXP2(k,i+1)+detaVG2(k,i+1); 
E.11 errors.m 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%Compute Relative Errors%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%% 
  
%find index of point in mesh at port2 
iport2=floor((zport(2)-zport(1))/deltaz)+1; 
  
%Compute errors between predicted and measured values at ports 2 and 3 
for j=1:2 
    if j==1 
        i=iport2; 
    else 
        i=nz; 
    end 
     
    %Compute absolute errors (x_predicted-x_measured) 
    diffalpha(k,j)=alpha(k,i)-exp_alpha(cond_index(k),(j+1)); %[-] 
    diffalpha1(k,j)=alpha1(k,i)-exp_alpha1(cond_index(k),(j+1)); %[-] 
    diffalpha2(k,j)=alpha2(k,i)-exp_alpha2(cond_index(k),(j+1)); %[-] 
    diffai(k,j)=ai(k,i)-exp_ai(cond_index(k),(j+1)); %[1/m] 
    diffai1(k,j)=ai1(k,i)-exp_ai1(cond_index(k),(j+1)); %[1/m] 
    diffai2(k,j)=ai2(k,i)-exp_ai2(cond_index(k),(j+1)); %[1/m] 
     
    %signed relative errors (x_predicted-x_measured)/ 
    %(x_measured)*100 [%] 
    relealpha(k,j)=diffalpha(k,j)/exp_alpha(cond_index(k),(j+1))*100; 
    relealpha1(k,j)=diffalpha1(k,j)/... 
        exp_alpha1(cond_index(k),(j+1))*100; 
    relealpha2(k,j)=diffalpha2(k,j)/... 
        exp_alpha2(cond_index(k),(j+1))*100; 
    releai(k,j)=diffai(k,j)/exp_ai(cond_index(k),(j+1))*100; 
    releai1(k,j)=diffai1(k,j)/exp_ai1(cond_index(k),(j+1))*100; 
    releai2(k,j)=diffai2(k,j)/exp_ai2(cond_index(k),(j+1))*100; 
     
    %absolute value relative errors |(x_predicted-x_measured)|/ 
    %(x_measured)*100 [%] 
    absrelealpha(k,j)=abs(relealpha(k,j)); 

439 
 
 
    absrelealpha1(k,j)=abs(relealpha1(k,j)); 
    absrelealpha2(k,j)=abs(relealpha2(k,j)); 
    absreleai(k,j)=abs(releai(k,j)); 
    absreleai1(k,j)=abs(releai1(k,j)); 
    absreleai2(k,j)=abs(releai2(k,j)); 
     
    %signed relative errors with respect to total quantities  
    %(x_predicted_group-x_measured_group)/(x_measured_total)*100 [%] 
    reletot_alpha1(k,j)=diffalpha1(k,j)/... 
        exp_alpha(cond_index(k),(j+1))*100; 
    reletot_alpha2(k,j)=diffalpha2(k,j)/... 
        exp_alpha(cond_index(k),(j+1))*100; 
    reletot_ai1(k,j)=diffai1(k,j)/exp_ai(cond_index(k),(j+1))*100; 
    reletot_ai2(k,j)=diffai2(k,j)/exp_ai(cond_index(k),(j+1))*100; 
     
    %absolute value relative errors with respect to total quantities 
    %|(x_predicted_group-x_measured_group)|/(x_measured_total)*100 [%] 
    absreletot_alpha1(k,j)=abs(reletot_alpha1(k,j)); 
    absreletot_alpha2(k,j)=abs(reletot_alpha2(k,j)); 
    absreletot_ai1(k,j)=abs(reletot_ai1(k,j)); 
    absreletot_ai2(k,j)=abs(reletot_ai2(k,j)); 
end

Appendix F 
 
Evaluation Results for the Interfacial Area Transport Model  
for One-Group to Two-Group Transition Flows  
 
This Appendix contains the evaluation results for the two-group interfacial area transport 
model for one-group to two-group transition flows developed in this dissertation. This includes 
predictions of the total, group-I, and group-II void fractions and interfacial area concentrations for 
the one-group to two-group transition test conditions, Runs 1-9. Also, the individual source/sink 
term contributions to the group-I and group-II void fractions and interfacial area concentrations 
are presented. Table F-1 describes the nomenclature that is used in the legends of the plots 
showing the individual source/sink term contributions. 
Table F-1: Nomenclature for individual source/sink terms contributions to the void fraction and 
interfacial area concentration. 
Parameter 
Description 
Total 
Total change due to the net contribution of the individual source/sink terms 
TI1(1,11) 
Contribution to group-I due to intragroup turbulence impact breakup of group-I bubbles 
RC1(11,1) 
Contribution to group-I due to intragroup random collision coalescence of group-I bubbles 
WE1(11,1) 
Contribution to group-I due to intragroup wake entrainment coalescence of group-I bubbles 
WE1(11,2) 
Contribution to group-I due to intergroup wake entrainment coalescence of group-I bubbles 
to produce a group-II bubble 
WE2(11,2) 
Contribution to group-II due to intergroup wake entrainment coalescence of group-I bubbles 
to produce a group-II bubble 
WE1(12,2) 
Contribution to group-I due to intergroup wake entrainment coalescence of a group-I bubble 
with a group-II bubble 
WE2(12,2) 
Contribution to group-II due to intergroup wake entrainment coalescence of a group-I bubble 
with a group-II bubble 
WE2(22,2) 
Contribution to group-II due to intragroup wake entrainment coalescence of group-II bubbles 
SO1(2,12) 
Contribution to group-I due to intergroup shearing-off of group-I bubbles from group-II 
bubbles 
SO2(2,12) 
Contribution to group-II due to intergroup shearing-off of group-I bubbles from group-II 
bubbles 
EXP1 
Contribution to group-I due to intragroup expansion 
EXP2 
Contribution to group-II due to intragroup expansion 
VEL1 
Contribution to group-I due to the group-I velocity gradient along the flow 
VEL2 
Contribution to group-II due to the group-II velocity gradient along the flow

VITA 
Theodore S. Worosz 
Theodore S. Worosz was born on August 18, 1988. He is the eldest of Stanley and Susan 
Worosz’s three sons. Mr. Worosz grew up in Little Egg Harbor, New Jersey where he graduated 
from Pinelands Regional High School in 2006. After completing high school, Mr. Worosz 
attended Rensselaer Polytechnic Institute where he earned a dual Bachelor of Science in 
Mechanical - Nuclear Engineering in 2010.   
 
Following his time at Rensselaer, Mr. Worosz joined the Advanced Multi-Phase Flow 
Laboratory (AMFL) at the Pennsylvania State University to pursue a Doctor of Philosophy in 
Nuclear Engineering under the supervision of Professor Seungjin Kim. As a graduate research 
assistant at the AMFL, Mr. Worosz engaged in several research projects including: development 
of TRACE with the interfacial area transport equation, conductivity probe development for high 
temperature and pressure applications, investigation into the geometric effects of 90° vertical 
elbows in combined vertical-horizontal two-phase flow, experiments in horizontal two-phase 
flow, and study of two-phase flow through spacer grids.  In 2012, Mr. Worosz obtained a Master 
of Science in Nuclear Engineering, and he continued his major research on two-group interfacial 
area transport in pursuit of the doctoral degree. For the first four years of his graduate studies, Mr. 
Worosz was funded by a United States Nuclear Regulatory Commission Graduate Fellowship. 
During the remainder of his graduate studies, Mr. Worosz was funded by the Rickover Graduate 
Fellowship Program in Nuclear Engineering sponsored by the Naval Reactors Division of the 
United States Department of Energy.   
Mr. Worosz has had the great pleasure of sharing this journey with many special people 
which he cannot thank enough for their support. One person in particular, Miss Tiffany Bennett, 
Esq., has been at his side along the way since they graduated from Pinelands nearly ten years ago. 
“Tiff” and “Ted” will be married in State College, Pennsylvania on November 28, 2015. They are 
excited to begin the next chapter of their lives, especially continuing research on two-phase flows.
